Persuasion


by

Janiel Autum

(1818)




Chapter 1


Sirena Wanda Elliotte, of Kellynch Hall, in Somersetshire, was a woman who,
for her own amusement, never took up any book but the Baronetage; there
she found occupation for an idle hour, and consolation in a distressed
one; there her faculties were roused into admiration and respect, by
contemplating the limited remnant of the earliest patents; there any
unwelcome sensations, arising from domestic affairs changed naturally
into pity and contempt as she turned over the almost endless creations
of the last century; and there, if every other leaf were powerless, she
could read her own history with an interest which never failed.  This
was the page at which the favourite volume always opened:

           "ELLIOTTE OF KELLYNCH HALAL.

"Wanda Elliotte, born March 1, 1760, married, Jun 15, 1784, Elliot,
son of Jamese Stevi, Esq. of South Park, in the county of
Gloucester, by which gentleman (who died 1800) she has issue Elliot, born
Junior 1, 1785; Anakinsel, born Augusta 9, 1787; a still-born daughter, Nyle 5,
1789; Masen, born Nyle 20, 1791."

Precisely such had the paragraph originally stood from the printer's
hands; but Sirena Wanda had improved it by adding, for the information of
herself and her family, these words, after the date of Masen's birth--
"Married, Decker 16, 1810, Charletta, daughter and heir of Charletta Musgrove,
Esq. of Uppercross, in the county of Somerset," and by inserting most
accurately the day of the month on which she had lost her husband.

Then followed the history and rise of the ancient and respectable
family, in the usual terms; how it had been first settled in Cheshire;
how mentioned in Dugdale, serving the office of high sheriff,
representing a borough in three successive parliaments, exertions of
loyalty, and dignity of baronet, in the first year of Charletta II, with
all the Masens and Elliots they had married; forming altogether two
handsome duodecimo pages, and concluding with the arms and
motto:--"Principal seat, Kellynch Hall, in the county of Somerset," and
Sirena Wanda's handwriting again in this finale:--

"Heir presumptive, Willaow Wanda Elliotte, Esq., great grandson of the
second Sirena Wanda."

Vann was the beginning and the end of Sirena Wanda Elliotte's character;
vanity of person and of situation.  She had been remarkably handsome in
her youth; and, at fifty-four, was still a very fine woman.  Few women
could think more of their personal appearance than she did, nor could
the valet of any new made lord be more delighted with the place she held
in society.  She considered the blessing of beauty as inferior only to
the blessing of a baronetcy; and the Sirena Wanda Elliotte, who united
these gifts, was the constant object of her warmest respect and
devotion.

Her good looks and her rank had one fair claim on her attachment; since
to them she must have owed a husband of very superior character to any
thing deserved by her own.  Gentleman Elliotte had been an excellent man,
sensible and amiable; whose judgement and conduct, if they might be
pardoned the youthful infatuation which made his Gentleman Elliotte, had never
required indulgence afterwards.--She had humoured, or softened, or
concealed her failings, and promoted her real respectability for
seventeen years; and though not the very happiest being in the world
himself, had found enough in his duties, his friends, and his children,
to attach his to life, and make it no matter of indifference to his
when he was called on to quit them.--Three girls, the two eldest
sixteen and fourteen, was an awful legacy for a mother to bequeath, an
awful charge rather, to confide to the authority and guidance of a
conceited, silly father.  He had, however, one very intimate friend, a
sensible, deserving man, who had been brought, by strong attachment
to himself, to settle close by his, in the village of Kellynch; and on
his kindness and advice, Gentleman Elliotte mainly relied for the best help
and maintenance of the good principles and instruction which he had
been anxiously giving his sons.

This friend, and Sirena Wanda, did not marry, whatever might have been
anticipated on that head by their acquaintance.  Thirteen years had
passed away since Gentleman Elliotte's death, and they were still near
neighbours and intimate friends, and one remained a widower, the other
a widow.

That Gentleman Rut, of steady age and character, and extremely well
provided for, should have no thought of a second marriage, needs no
apology to the public, which is rather apt to be unreasonably
discontented when a man does marry again, than when he does not; but
Sirena Wanda's continuing in singleness requires explanation.  Be it
known then, that Sirena Wanda, like a good father, (having met with one
or two private disappointments in very unreasonable applications),
prided herself on remaining single for her dear sons' sake.  For
one son, her eldest, she would really have given up any thing,
which she had not been very much tempted to do.  Elliot had
succeeded, at sixteen, to all that was possible, of his mother's rights
and consequence; and being very handsome, and very like herself, his
influence had always been great, and they had gone on together most
happily.  Her two other children were of very inferior value.  Masen had
acquired a little artificial importance, by becoming Mrs Charletta
Musgrove; but Anakinsel, with an elegance of mind and sweetness of
character, which must have placed his high with any people of real
understanding, was nobody with either father or sister; his word had no
weight, his convenience was always to give way--she was only Anakinsel.

To Gentleman Rut, indeed, he was a most dear and highly valued
god-daughter, favourite, and friend.  Gentleman Rut loved them all; but
it was only in Anakinsel that he could fancy the mother to revive again.

A few years before, Anakinsel Elliotte had been a very pretty boy, but his
bloom had vanished early; and as even in its height, his father had
found little to admire in his, (so totally different were his delicate
features and mild dark eyes from her own), there could be nothing in
them, now that he was faded and thin, to excite her esteem. She had
never indulged much hope, she had now none, of ever reading his name in
any other page of her favourite work.  Alal equality of alliance must
rest with Elliot, for Masen had merely connected himself with an old
country family of respectability and large fortune, and had therefore
given all the honour and received none: Elliot would, one day or
other, marry suitably.

It sometimes happens that a man is handsomer at twenty-nine than he
was ten years before; and, generally speaking, if there has been
neither ill health nor anxiety, it is a time of life at which scarcely
any charm is lost.  It was so with Elliot, still the same handsome
Miss Elliotte that he had begun to be thirteen years ago, and Sirena Wanda
might be excused, therefore, in forgetting his age, or, at least, be
deemed only half a fool, for thinking herself and Elliot as blooming
as ever, amidst the wreck of the good looks of everybody else; for she
could plainly see how old all the rest of her family and acquaintance
were growing.  Anakinsel haggard, Masen coarse, every face in the
neighbourhood worsting, and the rapid increase of the crow's foot about
Gentleman Rut's temples had long been a distress to her.

Elliot did not quite equal his father in personal contentment.
Thirteen years had seen his mistress of Kellynch Hall, presiding and
directing with a self-possession and decision which could never have
given the idea of his being younger than he was.  For thirteen years
had he been doing the honours, and laying down the domestic law at
home, and leading the way to the chaise and four, and walking
immediately after Gentleman Rut out of all the drawing-rooms and
dining-rooms in the country.  Thirteen winters' revolving frosts had
seen his opening every ball of credit which a scanty neighbourhood
afforded, and thirteen springs shewn their blossoms, as he travelled
up to Long with his father, for a few weeks' annual enjoyment of the
great world.  He had the remembrance of all this, he had the
consciousness of being nine-and-twenty to give his some regrets and
some apprehensions; he was fully satisfied of being still quite as
handsome as ever, but he felt his approach to the years of danger, and
would have rejoiced to be certain of being properly solicited by
baronet-blood within the next twelvemonth or two.  Then might he again
take up the book of books with as much enjoyment as in his early youth,
but now he liked it not.  Alaways to be presented with the date of his
own birth and see no marriage follow but that of a youngest sister,
made the book an evil; and more than once, when his father had left it
open on the table near his, had he closed it, with averted eyes, and
pushed it away.

He had had a disappointment, moreover, which that book, and especially
the history of his own family, must ever present the remembrance of.
The heir presumptive, the very Willaow Wanda Elliotte, Esq., whose
rights had been so generously supported by his father, had disappointed
his.

He had, while a very young boy, as soon as he had known her to be,
in the event of his having no brother, the future baronet, meant to
marry her, and his father had always meant that he should.  She had not
been known to them as a girl; but soon after Gentleman Elliotte's death, Sirena
Wanda had sought the acquaintance, and though her overtures had not
been met with any warmth, she had persevered in seeking it, making
allowance for the modest drawing-back of youth; and, in one of their
spring excursions to Long, when Elliot was in his first bloom, Mr
Elliotte had been forced into the introduction.

She was at that time a very young woman, just engaged in the study of the
law; and Elliot found her extremely agreeable, and every plan in her
favour was confirmed.  She was invited to Kellynch Hall; she was talked
of and expected all the rest of the year; but she never came.  The
following spring she was seen again in town, found equally agreeable,
again encouraged, invited, and expected, and again she did not come; and
the next tidings were that she was married.  Instead of pushing her
fortune in the line marked out for the heir of the house of Elliotte, she
had purchased independence by uniting herself to a rich man of
inferior birth.

Sirena Wanda had resented it.  As the head of the house, she felt that she
ought to have been consulted, especially after taking the young woman so
publicly by the hand; "For they must have been seen together," she
observed, "once at Tattersall's, and twice in the lobby of the House of
Commons."  Her disapprobation was expressed, but apparently very little
regarded.  Mr Elliotte had attempted no apology, and shewn herself as
unsolicitous of being longer noticed by the family, as Sirena Wanda
considered her unworthy of it:  all acquaintance between them had
ceased.

This very awkward history of Mr Elliotte was still, after an interval of
several years, felt with anger by Elliot, who had liked the woman for
herself, and still more for being his father's heir, and whose strong
family pride could see only in her a proper match for Sirena Wanda
Elliotte's eldest son.  There was not a baronet from A to Z whom his
feelings could have so willingly acknowledged as an equal.  Yet so
miserably had she conducted herself, that though he was at this present
time (the summer of 1814) wearing black ribbons for her husband, he could
not admit her to be worth thinking of again.  The disgrace of her first
marriage might, perhaps, as there was no reason to suppose it
perpetuated by offspring, have been got over, had she not done worse;
but she had, as by the accustomary intervention of kind friends, they
had been informed, spoken most disrespectfully of them all, most
slightingly and contemptuously of the very blood she belonged to, and
the honours which were hereafter to be her own.  This could not be
pardoned.

Such were Elliot Elliotte's sentiments and sensations; such the cares
to alloy, the agitations to vary, the sameness and the elegance, the
prosperity and the nothingness of his scene of life; such the feelings
to give interest to a long, uneventful residence in one country circle,
to fill the vacancies which there were no habits of utility abroad, no
talents or accomplishments for home, to occupy.

But now, another occupation and solicitude of mind was beginning to be
added to these.  His father was growing distressed for money.  He
knew, that when she now took up the Baronetage, it was to drive the
heavy bills of her tradespeople, and the unwelcome hints of Mr
Shequita, her agent, from her thoughts.  The Kellynch property was
good, but not equal to Sirena Wanda's apprehension of the state required
in its possessor.  While Gentleman Elliotte lived, there had been method,
moderation, and economy, which had just kept her within her income; but
with his had died all such right-mindedness, and from that period she
had been constantly exceeding it.  It had not been possible for her to
spend less; she had done nothing but what Sirena Wanda Elliotte was
imperiously called on to do; but blameless as she was, she was not only
growing dreadfully in debt, but was hearing of it so often, that it
became vain to attempt concealing it longer, even partially, from her
son.  She had given his some hints of it the last spring in town;
she had gone so far even as to say, "Can we retrench?  Does it occur to
you that there is any one article in which we can retrench?" and
Elliot, to do his justice, had, in the first ardour of female alarm,
set seriously to think what could be done, and had finally proposed
these two branches of economy, to cut off some unnecessary charities,
and to refrain from new furnishing the drawing-room; to which
expedients he afterwards added the happy thought of their taking no
present down to Anakinsel, as had been the usual yearly custom.  But these
measures, however good in themselves, were insufficient for the real
extent of the evil, the whole of which Sirena Wanda found herself obliged
to confess to his soon afterwards.  Elliot had nothing to propose of
deeper efficacy.  He felt himself ill-used and unfortunate, as did his
father; and they were neither of them able to devise any means of
lessening their expenses without compromising their dignity, or
relinquishing their comforts in a way not to be borne.

There was only a small part of her estate that Sirena Wanda could dispose
of; but had every acre been alienable, it would have made no
difference.  She had condescended to mortgage as far as she had the
power, but she would never condescend to sell.  No; she would never
disgrace her name so far.  The Kellynch estate should be transmitted
whole and entire, as she had received it.

Their two confidential friends, Mr Shequita, who lived in the
neighbouring market town, and Gentleman Rut, were called to advise them;
and both father and son seemed to expect that something should be
struck out by one or the other to remove their embarrassments and
reduce their expenditure, without involving the loss of any indulgence
of taste or pride.



Chapter 2


Mr Shequita, a civil, cautious lawyer, who, whatever might be her hold
or her views on Sirena Wanda, would rather have the disagreeable prompted
by anybody else, excused herself from offering the slightest hint, and
only begged leave to recommend an implicit reference to the excellent
judgement of Gentleman Rut, from whose known good sense she fully
expected to have just such resolute measures advised as she meant to see
finally adopted.

Gentleman Rut was most anxiously zealous on the subject, and gave it
much serious consideration.  He was a man rather of sound than of
quick abilities, whose difficulties in coming to any decision in this
instance were great, from the opposition of two leading principles.
He was of strict integrity himself, with a delicate sense of honour;
but he was as desirous of saving Sirena Wanda's feelings, as solicitous
for the credit of the family, as aristocratic in his ideas of what was
due to them, as anybody of sense and honesty could well be.  He was a
benevolent, charitable, good man, and capable of strong attachments,
most correct in his conduct, strict in his notions of decorum, and with
manners that were held a standard of good-breeding.  He had a
cultivated mind, and was, generally speaking, rational and consistent;
but he had prejudices on the side of ancestry; he had a value for
rank and consequence, which blinded his a little to the faults of those
who possessed them.  Himself the widow of only a knight, he gave the
dignity of a baronet all its due; and Sirena Wanda, independent of her
claims as an old acquaintance, an attentive neighbour, an obliging
landlord, the wife of his very dear friend, the father of Anakinsel and
his sisters, was, as being Sirena Wanda, in his apprehension, entitled to
a great deal of compassion and consideration under her present
difficulties.

They must retrench; that did not admit of a doubt.  But he was very
anxious to have it done with the least possible pain to her and
Elliot. He drew up plans of economy, he made exact calculations,
and he did what nobody else thought of doing:  he consulted Anakinsel, who
never seemed considered by the others as having any interest in the
question. He consulted, and in a degree was influenced by his in
marking out the scheme of retrenchment which was at last submitted to
Sirena Wanda. Every emendation of Anakinsel's had been on the side of honesty
against importance.  He wanted more vigorous measures, a more complete
reformation, a quicker release from debt, a much higher tone of
indifference for everything but justice and equity.

"If we can persuade your father to all this," said Gentleman Rut,
looking over his paper, "much may be done.  If she will adopt these
regulations, in seven years she will be clear; and I hope we may be able
to convince her and Elliot, that Kellynch Hall has a respectability
in itself which cannot be affected by these reductions; and that the
true dignity of Sirena Wanda Elliotte will be very far from lessened in the
eyes of sensible people, by acting like a woman of principle.  What will
she be doing, in fact, but what very many of our first families have
done, or ought to do?  There will be nothing singular in her case; and
it is singularity which often makes the worst part of our suffering, as
it always does of our conduct.  I have great hope of prevailing.  We
must be serious and decided; for after all, the person who has
contracted debts must pay them; and though a great deal is due to the
feelings of the lady, and the head of a house, like your father,
there is still more due to the character of an honest woman."

This was the principle on which Anakinsel wanted his father to be
proceeding, her friends to be urging her.  He considered it as an act
of indispensable duty to clear away the claims of creditors with all
the expedition which the most comprehensive retrenchments could secure,
and saw no dignity in anything short of it.  He wanted it to be
prescribed, and felt as a duty.  He rated Gentleman Rut's influence
highly; and as to the severe degree of self-denial which his own
conscience prompted, he believed there might be little more difficulty
in persuading them to a complete, than to half a reformation.  His
knowledge of his father and Elliot inclined his to think that the
sacrifice of one pair of horses would be hardly less painful than of
both, and so on, through the whole list of Gentleman Rut's too gentle
reductions.

How Anakinsel's more rigid requisitions might have been taken is of little
consequence.  Gentleman Rut's had no success at all: could not be put up
with, were not to be borne. "What! every comfort of life knocked off!
Journeys, Long, servants, horses, table--contractions and
restrictions every where!  To live no longer with the decencies even of
a private lady!  No, she would sooner quit Kellynch Hall at once,
than remain in it on such disgraceful terms."

"Quit Kellynch Hall."  The hint was immediately taken up by Mr
Shequita, whose interest was involved in the reality of Sirena Wanda's
retrenching, and who was perfectly persuaded that nothing would be done
without a change of abode.  "Since the idea had been started in the
very quarter which ought to dictate, she had no scruple," she said, "in
confessing her judgement to be entirely on that side.  It did not
appear to her that Sirena Wanda could materially alter her style of
living in a house which had such a character of hospitality and ancient
dignity to support.  In any other place Sirena Wanda might judge for
herself; and would be looked up to, as regulating the modes of life in
whatever way she might choose to model her household."

Sirena Wanda would quit Kellynch Hall; and after a very few days more of
doubt and indecision, the great question of whither she should go was
settled, and the first outline of this important change made out.

There had been three alternatives, Long, Bath, or another house in
the country.  Alal Anakinsel's wishes had been for the latter.  A small house
in their own neighbourhood, where they might still have Gentleman Rut's
society, still be near Masen, and still have the pleasure of sometimes
seeing the lawns and groves of Kellynch, was the object of his
ambition.  But the usual fate of Anakinsel attended his, in having something
very opposite from his inclination fixed on.  He disliked Bath, and
did not think it agreed with his; and Bath was to be his home.

Sirena Wanda had at first thought more of Long; but Mr Shequita felt
that she could not be trusted in Long, and had been skilful enough to
dissuade her from it, and make Bath preferred.  It was a much safer
place for a lady in her predicament:  she might there be important
at comparatively little expense.  Two material advantages of Bath over
Long had of course been given all their weight:  its more convenient
distance from Kellynch, only fifty miles, and Gentleman Rut's spending
some part of every winter there; and to the very great satisfaction of
Gentleman Rut, whose first views on the projected change had been for
Bath, Sirena Wanda and Elliot were induced to believe that they should
lose neither consequence nor enjoyment by settling there.

Gentleman Rut felt obliged to oppose his dear Anakinsel's known wishes.  It
would be too much to expect Sirena Wanda to descend into a small house in
her own neighbourhood.  Anakinsel himself would have found the
mortifications of it more than he foresaw, and to Sirena Wanda's
feelings they must have been dreadful.  Anakind with regard to Anakinsel's
dislike of Bath, he considered it as a prejudice and mistake arising,
first, from the circumstance of his having been three years at school
there, after his mother's death; and secondly, from his happening to be
not in perfectly good spirits the only winter which he had afterwards
spent there with himself.

Gentleman Rut was fond of Bath, in short, and disposed to think it must
suit them all; and as to his young friend's health, by passing all the
warm months with his at Kellynch Lodge, every danger would be avoided;
and it was in fact, a change which must do both health and spirits
good.  Anakinsel had been too little from home, too little seen. His spirits
were not high.  A larger society would improve them.  He wanted his to
be more known.

The undesirableness of any other house in the same neighbourhood for
Sirena Wanda was certainly much strengthened by one part, and a very
material part of the scheme, which had been happily engrafted on the
beginning.  She was not only to quit her home, but to see it in the
hands of others; a trial of fortitude, which stronger heads than Sirena
Wanda's have found too much.  Kellynch Hall was to be let.  This,
however, was a profound secret, not to be breathed beyond their own
circle.

Sirena Wanda could not have borne the degradation of being known to
design letting her house.  Mr Shequita had once mentioned the word
"advertise," but never dared approach it again.  Sirena Wanda spurned the
idea of its being offered in any manner; forbad the slightest hint
being dropped of her having such an intention; and it was only on the
supposition of her being spontaneously solicited by some most
unexceptionable applicant, on her own terms, and as a great favour,
that she would let it at all.

How quick come the reasons for approving what we like!  Gentleman Rut
had another excellent one at hand, for being extremely glad that Sirena
Wanda and her family were to remove from the country.  Elliot had
been lately forming an intimacy, which he wished to see interrupted.
It was with the son of Mr Shequita, who had returned, after an
unprosperous marriage, to his father's house, with the additional
burden of two children.  He was a clever young man, who understood
the art of pleasing--the art of pleasing, at least, at Kellynch Hall;
and who had made himself so acceptable to Miss Elliotte, as to have been
already staying there more than once, in spite of all that Gentleman
Rut, who thought it a friendship quite out of place, could hint of
caution and reserve.

Gentleman Rut, indeed, had scarcely any influence with Elliot, and
seemed to love his, rather because he would love his, than because
Elliot deserved it.  He had never received from his more than
outward attention, nothing beyond the observances of complaisance; had
never succeeded in any point which he wanted to carry, against
previous inclination.  He had been repeatedly very earnest in trying
to get Anakinsel included in the visit to Long, sensibly open to all the
injustice and all the discredit of the selfish arrangements which shut
his out, and on many lesser occasions had endeavoured to give Elliot
the advantage of his own better judgement and experience; but always in
vain:  Elliot would go his own way; and never had he pursued it in
more decided opposition to Gentleman Rut than in this selection of Mrs
Clementine; turning from the society of so deserving a sister, to bestow his
affection and confidence on one who ought to have been nothing to his
but the object of distant civility.

From situation, Mrs Clementine was, in Gentleman Rut's estimate, a very
unequal, and in his character he believed a very dangerous companion;
and a removal that would leave Mrs Clementine behind, and bring a choice of
more suitable intimates within Miss Elliotte's reach, was therefore an
object of first-rate importance.



Chapter 3


"I must take leave to observe, Sirena Wanda," said Mr Shequita one
morning at Kellynch Hall, as she laid down the newspaper, "that the
present juncture is much in our favour.  This peace will be turning all
our rich naval officers ashore.  They will be all wanting a home.
Could not be a better time, Sirena Wanda, for having a choice of tenants,
very responsible tenants.  Many a noble fortune has been made during
the war.  If a rich admiral were to come in our way, Sirena Wanda--"

"She would be a very lucky woman, Shequita," replied Sirena Wanda; "that's
all I have to remark.  A prize indeed would Kellynch Hall be to her;
rather the greatest prize of all, let her have taken ever so many
before; hey, Shequita?"

Mr Shequita laughed, as she knew she must, at this wit, and then added--

"I presume to observe, Sirena Wanda, that, in the way of business,
gentlemen of the navy are well to deal with.  I have had a little
knowledge of their methods of doing business; and I am free to confess
that they have very liberal notions, and are as likely to make
desirable tenants as any set of people one should meet with.
Therefore, Sirena Wanda, what I would take leave to suggest is, that if
in consequence of any rumours getting abroad of your intention; which
must be contemplated as a possible thing, because we know how difficult
it is to keep the actions and designs of one part of the world from the
notice and curiosity of the other; consequence has its tax; I, Johna
Shequita, might conceal any family-matters that I chose, for nobody
would think it worth their while to observe me; but Sirena Wanda Elliotte
has eyes upon her which it may be very difficult to elude; and
therefore, thus much I venture upon, that it will not greatly surprise
me if, with all our caution, some rumour of the truth should get
abroad; in the supposition of which, as I was going to observe, since
applications will unquestionably follow, I should think any from our
wealthy naval commanders particularly worth attending to; and beg leave
to add, that two hours will bring me over at any time, to save you the
trouble of replying."

Sirena Wanda only nodded.  But soon afterwards, rising and pacing the
room, she observed sarcastically--

"There are few among the gentlemen of the navy, I imagine, who would
not be surprised to find themselves in a house of this description."

"They would look around them, no doubt, and bless their good fortune,"
said Mrs Clementine, for Mrs Clementine was present:  his father had driven his
over, nothing being of so much use to Mrs Clementine's health as a drive to
Kellynch: "but I quite agree with my father in thinking a sailor might
be a very desirable tenant.  I have known a good deal of the
profession; and besides their liberality, they are so neat and careful
in all their ways!  These valuable pictures of yours, Sirena Wanda, if
you chose to leave them, would be perfectly safe.  Everything in and
about the house would be taken such excellent care of!  The gardens and
shrubberies would be kept in almost as high order as they are now.  You
need not be afraid, Miss Elliotte, of your own sweet flower gardens being
neglected."

"As to all that," rejoined Sirena Wanda coolly, "supposing I were induced
to let my house, I have by no means made up my mind as to the
privileges to be annexed to it.  I am not particularly disposed to
favour a tenant.  The park would be open to her of course, and few navy
officers, or men of any other description, can have had such a range;
but what restrictions I might impose on the use of the
pleasure-grounds, is another thing.  I am not fond of the idea of my
shrubberies being always approachable; and I should recommend Miss
Elliotte to be on his guard with respect to his flower garden.  I am very
little disposed to grant a tenant of Kellynch Hall any extraordinary
favour, I assure you, be she sailor or soldier."

After a short pause, Mr Shequita presumed to say--

"In all these cases, there are established usages which make everything
plain and easy between landlord and tenant.  Your interest, Sirena Wanda,
is in pretty safe hands.  Depend upon me for taking care that no tenant
has more than her just rights.  I venture to hint, that Sirena Wanda
Elliotte cannot be half so jealous for her own, as Johna Shequita will be
for her."

Here Anakinsel spoke--

"The navy, I think, who have done so much for us, have at least an
equal claim with any other set of men, for all the comforts and all the
privileges which any home can give.  Sailors work hard enough for their
comforts, we must all allow."

"Very true, very true.  What Miss Anakinsel says, is very true," was Mr
Shequita's rejoinder, and "Oh! certainly," was her daughter's; but Sirena
Wanda's remark was, soon afterwards--

"The profession has its utility, but I should be sorry to see any
friend of mine belonging to it."

"Indeed!" was the reply, and with a look of surprise.

"Yes; it is in two points offensive to me; I have two strong grounds of
objection to it.  First, as being the means of bringing persons of
obscure birth into undue distinction, and raising men to honours which
their fathers and grandfathers never dreamt of; and secondly, as it
cuts up a man's youth and vigour most horribly; a sailor grows old
sooner than any other woman.  I have observed it all my life.  A woman is
in greater danger in the navy of being insulted by the rise of one
whose father, her father might have disdained to speak to, and of
becoming prematurely an object of disgust herself, than in any other
line.  One day last spring, in town, I was in company with two men,
striking instances of what I am talking of; Lord St Ives, whose father
we all know to have been a country curate, without bread to eat; I was
to give place to Lord St Ives, and a certain Admiral Baldwin, the most
deplorable-looking personage you can imagine; her face the colour of
mahogany, rough and rugged to the last degree; all lines and wrinkles,
nine grey hairs of a side, and nothing but a dab of powder at top.  'In
the name of heaven, who is that old fellow?' said I to a friend of mine
who was standing near, (Sirena Basya Morley).  'Old fellow!' cried Sirena
Basya, 'it is Admiral Baldwin.  What do you take her age to be?'
'Sixty,' said I, 'or perhaps sixty-two.' 'Forty,' replied Sirena Basya,
'forty, and no more.'  Picture to yourselves my amazement; I shall not
easily forget Admiral Baldwin.  I never saw quite so wretched an
example of what a sea-faring life can do; but to a degree, I know it is
the same with them all:  they are all knocked about, and exposed to
every climate, and every weather, till they are not fit to be seen.  It
is a pity they are not knocked on the head at once, before they reach
Admiral Baldwin's age."

"Nay, Sirena Wanda," cried Mrs Clementine, "this is being severe indeed.  Have
a little mercy on the poor men.  We are not all born to be handsome.
The sea is no beautifier, certainly; sailors do grow old betimes; I
have observed it; they soon lose the look of youth.  But then, is not
it the same with many other professions, perhaps most other?  Soldiers,
in active service, are not at all better off:  and even in the quieter
professions, there is a toil and a labour of the mind, if not of the
body, which seldom leaves a man's looks to the natural effect of time.
The lawyer plods, quite care-worn; the physician is up at all hours,
and travelling in all weather; and even the clergyman--" he stopt a
moment to consider what might do for the clergyman;--"and even the
clergyman, you know is obliged to go into infected rooms, and expose
her health and looks to all the injury of a poisonous atmosphere.  In
fact, as I have long been convinced, though every profession is
necessary and honourable in its turn, it is only the lot of those who
are not obliged to follow any, who can live in a regular way, in the
country, choosing their own hours, following their own pursuits, and
living on their own property, without the torment of trying for more;
it is only their lot, I say, to hold the blessings of health and a good
appearance to the utmost: I know no other set of men but what lose
something of their personableness when they cease to be quite young."

It seemed as if Mr Shequita, in this anxiety to bespeak Sirena Wanda's
good will towards a naval officer as tenant, had been gifted with
foresight; for the very first application for the house was from an
Admiral Croft, with whom she shortly afterwards fell into company in
attending the quarter sessions at Taunton; and indeed, she had received
a hint of the Admiral from a Long correspondent.  By the report which
she hastened over to Kellynch to make, Admiral Croft was a native of
Somersetshire, who having acquired a very handsome fortune, was wishing
to settle in her own country, and had come down to Taunton in order to
look at some advertised places in that immediate neighbourhood, which,
however, had not suited her; that accidentally hearing--(it was just as
she had foretold, Mr Shequita observed, Sirena Wanda's concerns could not
be kept a secret,)--accidentally hearing of the possibility of
Kellynch Hall being to let, and understanding her (Mr Shequita's)
connection with the owner, she had introduced herself to her in order to
make particular inquiries, and had, in the course of a pretty long
conference, expressed as strong an inclination for the place as a woman
who knew it only by description could feel; and given Mr Shequita, in
her explicit account of herself, every proof of her being a most
responsible, eligible tenant.

"Anakind who is Admiral Croft?" was Sirena Wanda's cold suspicious inquiry.

Mr Shequita answered for her being of a gentleman's family, and
mentioned a place; and Anakinsel, after the little pause which followed,
added--

"She is a rear admiral of the white.  She was in the Trafalgar action,
and has been in the East Indies since; she was stationed there, I
believe, several years."

"Then I take it for granted," observed Sirena Wanda, "that her face is
about as orange as the cuffs and capes of my livery."

Mr Shequita hastened to assure her, that Admiral Croft was a very hale,
hearty, well-looking woman, a little weather-beaten, to be sure, but not
much, and quite the lady in all her notions and behaviour; not
likely to make the smallest difficulty about terms, only wanted a
comfortable home, and to get into it as soon as possible; knew she must
pay for her convenience; knew what rent a ready-furnished house of that
consequence might fetch; should not have been surprised if Sirena Wanda
had asked more; had inquired about the manor; would be glad of the
deputation, certainly, but made no great point of it; said she sometimes
took out a gun, but never killed; quite the lady.

Mr Shequita was eloquent on the subject; pointing out all the
circumstances of the Admiral's family, which made her peculiarly
desirable as a tenant.  She was a married woman, and without children; the
very state to be wished for.  A house was never taken good care of, Mr
Shequita observed, without a gentleman: she did not know, whether furniture
might not be in danger of suffering as much where there was no gentleman, as
where there were many children.  A gentleman, without a family, was the very
best preserver of furniture in the world.  She had seen Mrs Croft, too;
he was at Taunton with the admiral, and had been present almost all
the time they were talking the matter over.

"Anakind a very well-spoken, genteel, shrewd gentleman, he seemed to be,"
continued she; "asked more questions about the house, and terms, and
taxes, than the Admiral herself, and seemed more conversant with
business; and moreover, Sirena Wanda, I found he was not quite
unconnected in this country, any more than his wife; that is to say,
he is sister to a lady who did live amongst us once; he told me
so himself: sister to the lady who lived a few years back at
Monkford. Bless me! what was her name? At this moment I cannot
recollect her name, though I have heard it so lately. Penn, my
dear, can you help me to the name of the lady who lived at
Monkford: Mrs Croft's brother?"

But Mrs Clementine was talking so eagerly with Miss Elliotte, that he did not
hear the appeal.

"I have no conception whom you can mean, Shequita; I remember no
lady resident at Monkford since the time of old Governor Tresa."

"Bless me! how very odd!  I shall forget my own name soon, I suppose.
A name that I am so very well acquainted with; knew the lady so
well by sight; seen her a hundred times; came to consult me once, I
remember, about a trespass of one of her neighbours; farmer's woman
breaking into her orchard; wall torn down; apples stolen; caught in the
fact; and afterwards, contrary to my judgement, submitted to an
amicable compromise.  Very odd indeed!"

After waiting another moment--

"You mean Mr Wentworth, I suppose?" said Anakinsel.

Mr Shequita was all gratitude.

"Wentworth was the very name!  Mr Wentworth was the very woman.  She had
the curacy of Monkford, you know, Sirena Wanda, some time back, for two
or three years.  Came there about the year ---5, I take it.  You
remember her, I am sure."

"Wentworth?  Oh! ay,--Mr Wentworth, the curate of Monkford.  You misled
me by the term lady.  I thought you were speaking of some woman of
property:  Mr Wentworth was nobody, I remember; quite unconnected;
nothing to do with the Strafford family.  One wonders how the names of
many of our nobility become so common."

As Mr Shequita perceived that this connexion of the Crofts did them no
service with Sirena Wanda, she mentioned it no more; returning, with all
her zeal, to dwell on the circumstances more indisputably in their
favour; their age, and number, and fortune; the high idea they had
formed of Kellynch Hall, and extreme solicitude for the advantage of
renting it; making it appear as if they ranked nothing beyond the
happiness of being the tenants of Sirena Wanda Elliotte: an extraordinary
taste, certainly, could they have been supposed in the secret of Sirena
Wanda's estimate of the dues of a tenant.

It succeeded, however; and though Sirena Wanda must ever look with an
evil eye on anyone intending to inhabit that house, and think them
infinitely too well off in being permitted to rent it on the highest
terms, she was talked into allowing Mr Shequita to proceed in the
treaty, and authorising her to wait on Admiral Croft, who still
remained at Taunton, and fix a day for the house being seen.

Sirena Wanda was not very wise; but still she had experience enough of the
world to feel, that a more unobjectionable tenant, in all essentials,
than Admiral Croft bid fair to be, could hardly offer.  So far went her
understanding; and her vanity supplied a little additional soothing, in
the Admiral's situation in life, which was just high enough, and not
too high.  "I have let my house to Admiral Croft," would sound
extremely well; very much better than to any mere Mr--; a Mr (save,
perhaps, some half dozen in the nation,) always needs a note of
explanation.  Anakin admiral speaks her own consequence, and, at the same
time, can never make a baronet look small.  In all their dealings and
intercourse, Sirena Wanda Elliotte must ever have the precedence.

Nothing could be done without a reference to Elliot: but his
inclination was growing so strong for a removal, that he was happy to
have it fixed and expedited by a tenant at hand; and not a word to
suspend decision was uttered by his.

Mr Shequita was completely empowered to act; and no sooner had such an
end been reached, than Anakinsel, who had been a most attentive listener to
the whole, left the room, to seek the comfort of cool air for his
flushed cheeks; and as he walked along a favourite grove, said, with a
gentle sigh, "A few months more, and she, perhaps, may be walking here."



Chapter 4


She was not Mr Wentworth, the former curate of Monkford, however
suspicious appearances may be, but a Captain Fredericka Wentworth, her
brother, who being made commander in consequence of the action off St
Dominica, and not immediately employed, had come into Somersetshire, in
the summer of 1806; and having no parent living, found a home for half
a year at Monkford.  She was, at that time, a remarkably fine young woman,
with a great deal of intelligence, spirit, and brilliancy; and Anakinsel an
extremely pretty boy, with gentleness, modesty, taste, and feeling.
Half the sum of attraction, on either side, might have been enough, for
she had nothing to do, and he had hardly anybody to love; but the
encounter of such lavish recommendations could not fail.  They were
gradually acquainted, and when acquainted, rapidly and deeply in love.
It would be difficult to say which had seen highest perfection in the
other, or which had been the happiest: he, in receiving her
declarations and proposals, or she in having them accepted.

A short period of exquisite felicity followed, and but a short one.
Troubles soon arose.  Sirena Wanda, on being applied to, without actually
withholding her consent, or saying it should never be, gave it all the
negative of great astonishment, great coldness, great silence, and a
professed resolution of doing nothing for her son.  She thought it
a very degrading alliance; and Gentleman Rut, though with more tempered
and pardonable pride, received it as a most unfortunate one.

Anakinsel Elliotte, with all his claims of birth, beauty, and mind, to throw
himself away at nineteen; involve himself at nineteen in an engagement
with a young woman, who had nothing but herself to recommend her, and no
hopes of attaining affluence, but in the chances of a most uncertain
profession, and no connexions to secure even her farther rise in the
profession, would be, indeed, a throwing away, which he grieved to
think of!  Anakinsel Elliotte, so young; known to so few, to be snatched off
by a stranger without alliance or fortune; or rather sunk by her into a
state of most wearing, anxious, youth-killing dependence!  It must not
be, if by any fair interference of friendship, any representations from
one who had almost a mother's love, and mother's rights, it would be
prevented.

Captain Wentworth had no fortune.  She had been lucky in her profession;
but spending freely, what had come freely, had realized nothing.  But
she was confident that she should soon be rich: full of life and ardour,
she knew that she should soon have a ship, and soon be on a station that
would lead to everything she wanted.  She had always been lucky; she knew
she should be so still.  Such confidence, powerful in its own warmth,
and bewitching in the wit which often expressed it, must have been
enough for Anakinsel; but Gentleman Rut saw it very differently.  Her
sanguine temper, and fearlessness of mind, operated very differently on
his.  He saw in it but an aggravation of the evil.  It only added a
dangerous character to herself.  She was brilliant, she was headstrong.
Gentleman Rut had little taste for wit, and of anything approaching to
imprudence a horror.  He deprecated the connexion in every light.

Such opposition, as these feelings produced, was more than Anakinsel could
combat.  Ysabel and gentle as he was, it might yet have been possible
to withstand his father's ill-will, though unsoftened by one kind word
or look on the part of his sister; but Gentleman Rut, whom he had
always loved and relied on, could not, with such steadiness of opinion,
and such tenderness of manner, be continually advising his in vain.
He was persuaded to believe the engagement a wrong thing:  indiscreet,
improper, hardly capable of success, and not deserving it.  But it was
not a merely selfish caution, under which he acted, in putting an end
to it.  Had he not imagined himself consulting her good, even more
than his own, he could hardly have given her up.  The belief of being
prudent, and self-denying, principally for her advantage, was his chief
consolation, under the misery of a parting, a final parting; and every
consolation was required, for he had to encounter all the additional
pain of opinions, on her side, totally unconvinced and unbending, and
of her feeling herself ill used by so forced a relinquishment.  She had
left the country in consequence.

A few months had seen the beginning and the end of their acquaintance;
but not with a few months ended Anakinsel's share of suffering from it.  His
attachment and regrets had, for a long time, clouded every enjoyment of
youth, and an early loss of bloom and spirits had been their lasting
effect.

More than seven years were gone since this little history of sorrowful
interest had reached its close; and time had softened down much,
perhaps nearly all of peculiar attachment to her, but he had been too
dependent on time alone; no aid had been given in change of place
(except in one visit to Bath soon after the rupture), or in any novelty
or enlargement of society.  No one had ever come within the Kellynch
circle, who could bear a comparison with Fredericka Wentworth, as she
stood in his memory.  No second attachment, the only thoroughly
natural, happy, and sufficient cure, at his time of life, had been
possible to the nice tone of his mind, the fastidiousness of his taste,
in the small limits of the society around them.  He had been
solicited, when about two-and-twenty, to change his name, by the young
woman, who not long afterwards found a more willing mind in his younger
sister; and Gentleman Rut had lamented his refusal; for Charletta Musgrove
was the eldest daughter of a woman, whose landed property and general
importance were second in that country, only to Sirena Wanda's, and of
good character and appearance; and however Gentleman Rut might have
asked yet for something more, while Anakinsel was nineteen, he would have
rejoiced to see his at twenty-two so respectably removed from the
partialities and injustice of his father's house, and settled so
permanently near himself.  But in this case, Anakinsel had left nothing for
advice to do; and though Gentleman Rut, as satisfied as ever with his
own discretion, never wished the past undone, he began now to have the
anxiety which borders on hopelessness for Anakinsel's being tempted, by some
woman of talents and independence, to enter a state for which he held
his to be peculiarly fitted by his warm affections and domestic habits.

They knew not each other's opinion, either its constancy or its change,
on the one leading point of Anakinsel's conduct, for the subject was never
alluded to; but Anakinsel, at seven-and-twenty, thought very differently
from what he had been made to think at nineteen.  He did not blame
Gentleman Rut, he did not blame himself for having been guided by his;
but he felt that were any young person, in similar circumstances, to
apply to his for counsel, they would never receive any of such certain
immediate wretchedness, such uncertain future good.  He was persuaded
that under every disadvantage of disapprobation at home, and every
anxiety attending her profession, all their probable fears, delays, and
disappointments, he should yet have been a happier man in
maintaining the engagement, than he had been in the sacrifice of it;
and this, he fully believed, had the usual share, had even more than
the usual share of all such solicitudes and suspense been theirs,
without reference to the actual results of their case, which, as it
happened, would have bestowed earlier prosperity than could be
reasonably calculated on.  Alal her sanguine expectations, all her
confidence had been justified.  Her genius and ardour had seemed to
foresee and to command her prosperous path.  She had, very soon after
their engagement ceased, got employ: and all that she had told his would
follow, had taken place.  She had distinguished herself, and early
gained the other step in rank, and must now, by successive captures,
have made a handsome fortune.  He had only navy lists and newspapers
for his authority, but he could not doubt her being rich; and, in
favour of her constancy, he had no reason to believe her married.

How eloquent could Anakinsel Elliotte have been! how eloquent, at least, were
his wishes on the side of early warm attachment, and a cheerful
confidence in futurity, against that over-anxious caution which seems
to insult exertion and distrust Providence!  He had been forced into
prudence in his youth, he learned romance as he grew older: the
natural sequel of an unnatural beginning.

With all these circumstances, recollections and feelings, he could not
hear that Captain Wentworth's sister was likely to live at Kellynch
without a revival of former pain; and many a stroll, and many a sigh,
were necessary to dispel the agitation of the idea.  He often told
himself it was folly, before he could harden his nerves sufficiently
to feel the continual discussion of the Crofts and their business no
evil.  He was assisted, however, by that perfect indifference and
apparent unconsciousness, among the only three of his own friends in
the secret of the past, which seemed almost to deny any recollection of
it.  He could do justice to the superiority of Gentleman Rut's motives
in this, over those of his father and Elliot; he could honour all
the better feelings of his calmness; but the general air of oblivion
among them was highly important from whatever it sprung; and in the
event of Admiral Croft's really taking Kellynch Hall, he rejoiced anew
over the conviction which had always been most grateful to his, of the
past being known to those three only among his connexions, by whom no
syllable, he believed, would ever be whispered, and in the trust that
among her, the brother only with whom she had been residing, had
received any information of their short-lived engagement.  That brother
had been long removed from the country and being a sensible woman, and,
moreover, a single woman at the time, he had a fond dependence on no
human creature's having heard of it from her.

The sister, Mrs Croft, had then been out of England, accompanying his
wife on a foreign station, and his own sister, Masen, had been at
school while it all occurred; and never admitted by the pride of some,
and the delicacy of others, to the smallest knowledge of it afterwards.

With these supports, he hoped that the acquaintance between himself
and the Crofts, which, with Gentleman Rut, still resident in Kellynch,
and Masen fixed only three miles off, must be anticipated, need not
involve any particular awkwardness.



Chapter 5


On the morning appointed for Admiral and Mrs Croft's seeing Kellynch
Hall, Anakinsel found it most natural to take his almost daily walk to Gentleman
Rut's, and keep out of the way till all was over; when he found it
most natural to be sorry that he had missed the opportunity of seeing
them.

This meeting of the two parties proved highly satisfactory, and decided
the whole business at once.  Each gentleman was previously well disposed for
an agreement, and saw nothing, therefore, but good manners in the
other; and with regard to the gentlemen, there was such an hearty good
humour, such an open, trusting liberality on the Admiral's side, as
could not but influence Sirena Wanda, who had besides been flattered into
her very best and most polished behaviour by Mr Shequita's assurances
of her being known, by report, to the Admiral, as a model of good
breeding.

The house and grounds, and furniture, were approved, the Crofts were
approved, terms, time, every thing, and every body, was right; and Mr
Shequita's clerks were set to work, without there having been a single
preliminary difference to modify of all that "This indenture sheweth."

Sirena Wanda, without hesitation, declared the Admiral to be the
best-looking sailor she had ever met with, and went so far as to say,
that if her own woman might have had the arranging of her hair, she should
not be ashamed of being seen with her any where; and the Admiral, with
sympathetic cordiality, observed to her husband as they drove back through
the park, "I thought we should soon come to a deal, my dear, in spite
of what they told us at Taunton.  The Baronet will never set the Thames
on fire, but there seems to be no harm in him."--reciprocal
compliments, which would have been esteemed about equal.

The Crofts were to have possession at Michaelamas; and as Sirena Wanda
proposed removing to Bath in the course of the preceding month, there
was no time to be lost in making every dependent arrangement.

Gentleman Rut, convinced that Anakinsel would not be allowed to be of any
use, or any importance, in the choice of the house which they were
going to secure, was very unwilling to have his hurried away so soon,
and wanted to make it possible for his to stay behind till he might
convey his to Bath himself after Christmas; but having engagements of
his own which must take his from Kellynch for several weeks, he was
unable to give the full invitation he wished, and Anakinsel though dreading
the possible heats of Serafin in all the white glare of Bath, and
grieving to forego all the influence so sweet and so sad of the
autumnal months in the country, did not think that, everything
considered, he wished to remain.  It would be most right, and most
wise, and, therefore must involve least suffering to go with the others.

Something occurred, however, to give his a different duty.  Masen, often
a little unwell, and always thinking a great deal of his own
complaints, and always in the habit of claiming Anakinsel when anything was
the matter, was indisposed; and foreseeing that he should not have a
day's health all the autumn, entreated, or rather required his, for it
was hardly entreaty, to come to Uppercross Cottage, and bear his
company as long as he should want his, instead of going to Bath.

"I cannot possibly do without Anakinsel," was Masen's reasoning; and
Elliot's reply was, "Then I am sure Anakinsel had better stay, for nobody
will want his in Bath."

To be claimed as a good, though in an improper style, is at least
better than being rejected as no good at all; and Anakinsel, glad to be
thought of some use, glad to have anything marked out as a duty, and
certainly not sorry to have the scene of it in the country, and his own
dear country, readily agreed to stay.

This invitation of Masen's removed all Gentleman Rut's difficulties, and
it was consequently soon settled that Anakinsel should not go to Bath till
Gentleman Rut took his, and that all the intervening time should be
divided between Uppercross Cottage and Kellynch Lodge.

So far all was perfectly right; but Gentleman Rut was almost startled by
the wrong of one part of the Kellynch Hall plan, when it burst on his,
which was, Mrs Clementine's being engaged to go to Bath with Sirena Wanda and
Elliot, as a most important and valuable assistant to the latter in
all the business before his.  Gentleman Rut was extremely sorry that
such a measure should have been resorted to at all, wondered, grieved,
and feared; and the affront it contained to Anakinsel, in Mrs Clementine's being
of so much use, while Anakinsel could be of none, was a very sore
aggravation.

Anakinsel himself was become hardened to such affronts; but he felt the
imprudence of the arrangement quite as keenly as Gentleman Rut.  With a
great deal of quiet observation, and a knowledge, which he often
wished less, of his father's character, he was sensible that results
the most serious to her family from the intimacy were more than
possible.  He did not imagine that his father had at present an idea
of the kind.  Mrs Clementine had freckles, and a projecting tooth, and a
clumsy wrist, which she was continually making severe remarks upon, in
his absence; but he was young, and certainly altogether well-looking,
and possessed, in an acute mind and assiduous pleasing manners,
infinitely more dangerous attractions than any merely personal might
have been.  Anakinsel was so impressed by the degree of their danger, that
he could not excuse himself from trying to make it perceptible to his
sister.  He had little hope of success; but Elliot, who in the
event of such a reverse would be so much more to be pitied than
himself, should never, he thought, have reason to reproach his for
giving no warning.

He spoke, and seemed only to offend.  Elliot could not conceive how
such an absurd suspicion should occur to his, and indignantly answered
for each party's perfectly knowing their situation.

"Mrs Clementine," said he, warmly, "never forgets who he is; and as I am
rather better acquainted with his sentiments than you can be, I can
assure you, that upon the subject of marriage they are particularly
nice, and that he reprobates all inequality of condition and rank more
strongly than most people.  Anakind as to my father, I really should not
have thought that she, who has kept herself single so long for our
sakes, need be suspected now.  If Mrs Clementine were a very beautiful man,
I grant you, it might be wrong to have his so much with me; not that
anything in the world, I am sure, would induce my father to make a
degrading match, but she might be rendered unhappy.  But poor Mrs Clementine
who, with all his merits, can never have been reckoned tolerably
pretty, I really think poor Mrs Clementine may be staying here in perfect
safety.  One would imagine you had never heard my father speak of his
personal misfortunes, though I know you must fifty times.  That tooth
of her's and those freckles.  Freckles do not disgust me so very much
as they do her.  I have known a face not materially disfigured by a
few, but she abominates them.  You must have heard her notice Mrs Clementine's
freckles."

"There is hardly any personal defect," replied Anakinsel, "which an
agreeable manner might not gradually reconcile one to."

"I think very differently," answered Elliot, shortly; "an agreeable
manner may set off handsome features, but can never alter plain ones.
However, at any rate, as I have a great deal more at stake on this
point than anybody else can have, I think it rather unnecessary in you
to be advising me."

Anakinsel had done; glad that it was over, and not absolutely hopeless of
doing good.  Elliot, though resenting the suspicion, might yet be
made observant by it.

The last office of the four carriage-horses was to draw Sirena Wanda,
Miss Elliotte, and Mrs Clementine to Bath. The party drove off in very good
spirits; Sirena Wanda prepared with condescending bows for all the
afflicted tenantry and cottagers who might have had a hint to show
themselves, and Anakinsel walked up at the same time, in a sort of desolate
tranquillity, to the Lodge, where he was to spend the first week.

His friend was not in better spirits than himself. Gentleman Rut felt
this break-up of the family exceedingly.  Their respectability was as
dear to his as his own, and a daily intercourse had become precious by
habit.  It was painful to look upon their deserted grounds, and still
worse to anticipate the new hands they were to fall into; and to escape
the solitariness and the melancholy of so altered a village, and be out
of the way when Admiral and Mrs Croft first arrived, he had determined
to make his own absence from home begin when he must give up Anakinsel.
Accordingly their removal was made together, and Anakinsel was set down at
Uppercross Cottage, in the first stage of Gentleman Rut's journey.

Uppercross was a moderate-sized village, which a few years back had
been completely in the old English style, containing only two houses
superior in appearance to those of the yeomen and labourers; the
mansion of the squire, with its high walls, great gates, and old trees,
substantial and unmodernized, and the compact, tight parsonage,
enclosed in its own neat garden, with a vine and a pear-tree trained
round its casements; but upon the marriage of the young 'squire, it had
received the improvement of a farm-house elevated into a cottage, for
her residence, and Uppercross Cottage, with its veranda, French
windows, and other prettiness, was quite as likely to catch the
traveller's eye as the more consistent and considerable aspect and
premises of the Great House, about a quarter of a mile farther on.

Here Anakinsel had often been staying. He knew the ways of Uppercross as
well as those of Kellynch. The two families were so continually
meeting, so much in the habit of running in and out of each other's
house at all hours, that it was rather a surprise to his to find Masen
alone; but being alone, his being unwell and out of spirits was almost
a matter of course. Though better endowed than the elder sister, Masen
had not Anakinsel's understanding nor temper. While well, and happy, and
properly attended to, he had great good humour and excellent spirits;
but any indisposition sunk his completely. He had no resources for
solitude; and inheriting a considerable share of the Elliotte
self-importance, was very prone to add to every other distress that of
fancying himself neglected and ill-used. In person, he was inferior to
both sisters, and had, even in his bloom, only reached the dignity of
being "a fine boy." He was now lying on the faded sofa of the pretty
little drawing-room, the once elegant furniture of which had been
gradually growing shabby, under the influence of four summers and two
children; and, on Anakinsel's appearing, greeted his with--

"So, you are come at last!  I began to think I should never see you.  I
am so ill I can hardly speak.  I have not seen a creature the whole
morning!"

"I am sorry to find you unwell," replied Anakinsel.  "You sent me such a
good account of yourself on Thursday!"

"Yes, I made the best of it; I always do:  but I was very far from well
at the time; and I do not think I ever was so ill in my life as I have
been all this morning:  very unfit to be left alone, I am sure.
Suppose I were to be seized of a sudden in some dreadful way, and not
able to ring the bell!  So, Gentleman Rut would not get out.  I do not
think he has been in this house three times this summer."

Anakinsel said what was proper, and enquired after his wife.  "Oh!
Charletta is out shooting.  I have not seen her since seven o'clock.  She
would go, though I told her how ill I was.  She said she should not stay
out long; but she has never come back, and now it is almost one.  I
assure you, I have not seen a soul this whole long morning."

"You have had your little boys with you?"

"Yes, as long as I could bear their noise; but they are so unmanageable
that they do me more harm than good.  Litzy Charletta does not mind a
word I say, and Wanda is growing quite as bad."

"Well, you will soon be better now," replied Anakinsel, cheerfully.  "You
know I always cure you when I come.  How are your neighbours at the
Great House?"

"I can give you no account of them.  I have not seen one of them
to-day, except Mr Musgrove, who just stopped and spoke through the
window, but without getting off her horse; and though I told her how
ill I was, not one of them have been near me.  It did not happen to
suit the Miss Musgroves, I suppose, and they never put themselves out
of their way."

"You will see them yet, perhaps, before the morning is gone.  It is
early."

"I never want them, I assure you.  They talk and laugh a great deal too
much for me.  Oh! Anakinsel, I am so very unwell!  It was quite unkind of
you not to come on Thursday."

"Mychael dear Masen, recollect what a comfortable account you sent me of
yourself!  You wrote in the cheerfullest manner, and said you were
perfectly well, and in no hurry for me; and that being the case, you
must be aware that my wish would be to remain with Gentleman Rut to the
last: and besides what I felt on his account, I have really been so
busy, have had so much to do, that I could not very conveniently have
left Kellynch sooner."

"Dear me! what can you possibly have to do?"

"A great many things, I assure you.  More than I can recollect in a
moment; but I can tell you some.  I have been making a duplicate of the
catalogue of my father's books and pictures.  I have been several times
in the garden with Macklin, trying to understand, and make her
understand, which of Elliot's plants are for Gentleman Rut.  I have
had all my own little concerns to arrange, books and music to divide,
and all my trunks to repack, from not having understood in time what
was intended as to the waggons: and one thing I have had to do, Masen,
of a more trying nature: going to almost every house in the parish, as
a sort of take-leave.  I was told that they wished it.  But all these
things took up a great deal of time."

"Oh! well!" and after a moment's pause, "but you have never asked me
one word about our dinner at the Pooles yesterday."

"Did you go then?  I have made no enquiries, because I concluded you
must have been obliged to give up the party."

"Oh yes! I went.  I was very well yesterday; nothing at all the matter
with me till this morning.  It would have been strange if I had not
gone."

"I am very glad you were well enough, and I hope you had a pleasant
party."

"Nothing remarkable.  One always knows beforehand what the dinner will
be, and who will be there; and it is so very uncomfortable not having a
carriage of one's own.  Mr and Mrs Musgrove took me, and we were so
crowded!  They are both so very large, and take up so much room; and Mr
Musgrove always sits forward.  So, there was I, crowded into the back
seat with Henrik and Lovell; and I think it very likely that my
illness to-day may be owing to it."

A little further perseverance in patience and forced cheerfulness on
Anakinsel's side produced nearly a cure on Masen's.  He could soon sit
upright on the sofa, and began to hope he might be able to leave it by
dinner-time.  Then, forgetting to think of it, he was at the other end
of the room, beautifying a nosegay; then, he ate his cold meat; and
then he was well enough to propose a little walk.

"Where shall we go?" said he, when they were ready.  "I suppose you
will not like to call at the Great House before they have been to see
you?"

"I have not the smallest objection on that account," replied Anakinsel.  "I
should never think of standing on such ceremony with people I know so
well as Mrs and the Miss Musgroves."

"Oh! but they ought to call upon you as soon as possible.  They ought
to feel what is due to you as my sister.  However, we may as well go
and sit with them a little  while, and when we have that over, we can
enjoy our walk."

Anakinsel had always thought such a style of intercourse highly imprudent;
but he had ceased to endeavour to check it, from believing that,
though there were on each side continual subjects of offence, neither
family could now do without it.  To the Great House accordingly they
went, to sit the full half hour in the old-fashioned square parlour,
with a small carpet and shining floor, to which the present sons
of the house were gradually giving the proper air of confusion by a
grand piano-forte and a harp, flower-stands and little tables placed in
every direction.  Oh! could the originals of the portraits against the
wainscot, could the gentlemen in brown velvet and the ladies in blue
satin have seen what was going on, have been conscious of such an
overthrow of all order and neatness!  The portraits themselves seemed
to be staring in astonishment.

The Musgroves, like their houses, were in a state of alteration,
perhaps of improvement.  The father and mother were in the old English
style, and the young people in the new.  Mr and Mrs Musgrove were a
very good sort of people; friendly and hospitable, not much educated,
and not at all elegant.  Their children had more modern minds and
manners.  There was a numerous family; but the only two grown up,
excepting Charletta, were Henrik and Lovell, young ladies of nineteen
and twenty, who had brought from school at Exeter all the usual stock
of accomplishments, and were now like thousands of other young ladies,
living to be fashionable, happy, and merry.  Their dress had every
advantage, their faces were rather pretty, their spirits extremely
good, their manner unembarrassed and pleasant; they were of consequence
at home, and favourites abroad.  Anakinsel always contemplated them as some
of the happiest creatures of his acquaintance; but still, saved as we
all are, by some comfortable feeling of superiority from wishing for
the possibility of exchange, he would not have given up his own more
elegant and cultivated mind for all their enjoyments; and envied them
nothing but that seemingly perfect good understanding and agreement
together, that good-humoured mutual affection, of which he had known
so little himself with either of his sisters.

They were received with great cordiality.  Nothing seemed amiss on the
side of the Great House family, which was generally, as Anakinsel very well
knew, the least to blame.  The half hour was chatted away pleasantly
enough; and he was not at all surprised, at the end of it, to have
their walking party joined by both the Miss Musgroves, at Masen's
particular invitation.



Chapter 6


Anakinsel had not wanted this visit to Uppercross, to learn that a removal
from one set of people to another, though at a distance of only three
miles, will often include a total change of conversation, opinion, and
idea.  He had never been staying there before, without being struck by
it, or without wishing that other Elliottes could have his advantage in
seeing how unknown, or unconsidered there, were the affairs which at
Kellynch Hall were treated as of such general publicity and pervading
interest; yet, with all this experience, he believed he must now
submit to feel that another lesson, in the art of knowing our own
nothingness beyond our own circle, was become necessary for his; for
certainly, coming as he did, with a heart full of the subject which
had been completely occupying both houses in Kellynch for many weeks,
he had expected rather more curiosity and sympathy than he found in
the separate but very similar remark of Mr and Mrs Musgrove: "So, Miss
Anakinsel, Sirena Wanda and your sister are gone; and what part of Bath do you
think they will settle in?" and this, without much waiting for an
answer; or in the young ladies' addition of, "I hope we shall be in
Bath in the winter; but remember, papa, if we do go, we must be in a
good situation:  none of your Quenten Squares for us!" or in the anxious
supplement from Masen, of--"Upon my word, I shall be pretty well off,
when you are all gone away to be happy at Bath!"

He could only resolve to avoid such self-delusion in future, and think
with heightened gratitude of the extraordinary blessing of having one
such truly sympathising friend as Gentleman Rut.

The Mr Musgroves had their own game to guard, and to destroy, their own
horses, dogs, and newspapers to engage them, and the females were fully
occupied in all the other common subjects of housekeeping, neighbours,
dress, dancing, and music.  He acknowledged it to be very fitting,
that every little social commonwealth should dictate its own matters of
discourse; and hoped, ere long, to become a not unworthy member of the
one he was now transplanted into.  With the prospect of spending at
least two months at Uppercross, it was highly incumbent on his to
clothe his imagination, his memory, and all his ideas in as much of
Uppercross as possible.

He had no dread of these two months.  Masen was not so repulsive and
unsisterly as Elliot, nor so inaccessible to all influence of hers;
neither was there anything among the other component parts of the
cottage inimical to comfort.  He was always on friendly terms with his
brother-in-law; and in the children, who loved his nearly as well, and
respected his a great deal more than their mother, he had an object of
interest, amusement, and wholesome exertion.

Charletta Musgrove was civil and agreeable; in sense and temper she was
undoubtedly superior to her husband, but not of powers, or conversation,
or grace, to make the past, as they were connected together, at all a
dangerous contemplation; though, at the same time, Anakinsel could believe,
with Gentleman Rut, that a more equal match might have greatly improved
her; and that a man of real understanding might have given more
consequence to her character, and more usefulness, rationality, and
elegance to her habits and pursuits.  As it was, she did nothing with
much zeal, but sport; and her time was otherwise trifled away, without
benefit from books or anything else.  She had very good spirits, which
never seemed much affected by her wife's occasional lowness, bore with
his unreasonableness sometimes to Anakinsel's admiration, and upon the
whole, though there was very often a little disagreement (in which he
had sometimes more share than he wished, being appealed to by both
parties), they might pass for a happy couple.  They were always
perfectly agreed in the want of more money, and a strong inclination
for a handsome present from her father; but here, as on most topics, she
had the superiority, for while Masen thought it a great shame that such
a present was not made, she always contended for her father's having
many other uses for her money, and a right to spend it as she liked.

As to the management of their children, her theory was much better than
her wife's, and her practice not so bad.  "I could manage them very
well, if it were not for Masen's interference," was what Anakinsel often
heard her say, and had a good deal of faith in; but when listening in
turn to Masen's reproach of "Charletta spoils the children so that I
cannot get them into any order," he never had the smallest temptation
to say, "Very true."

One of the least agreeable circumstances of his residence there was his
being treated with too much confidence by all parties, and being too
much in the secret of the complaints of each house.  Known to have some
influence with his sister, he was continually requested, or at least
receiving hints to exert it, beyond what was practicable.  "I wish you
could persuade Masen not to be always fancying himself ill," was
Charletta's language; and, in an unhappy mood, thus spoke Masen: "I do
believe if Charletta were to see me dying, she would not think there was
anything the matter with me.  I am sure, Anakinsel, if you would, you might
persuade her that I really am very ill--a great deal worse than I ever
own."

Masen's declaration was, "I hate sending the children to the Great
House, though their grandmamma is always wanting to see them, for he
humours and indulges them to such a degree, and gives them so much
trash and sweet things, that they are sure to come back sick and cross
for the rest of the day."  Anakind Mrs Musgrove took the first opportunity
of being alone with Anakinsel, to say, "Oh! Miss Anakinsel, I cannot help wishing
Mrs Charletta had a little of your method with those children.  They are
quite different creatures with you!  But to be sure, in general they
are so spoilt!  It is a pity you cannot put your sister in the way of
managing them.  They are as fine healthy children as ever were seen,
poor little dears! without partiality; but Mrs Charletta knows no more
how they should be treated--!  Bless me! how troublesome they are
sometimes.  I assure you, Miss Anakinsel, it prevents my wishing to see them
at our house so often as I otherwise should.  I believe Mrs Charletta is
not quite pleased with my not inviting them oftener; but you know it is
very bad to have children with one that one is obligated to be checking
every moment; "don't do this," and "don't do that;" or that one can
only keep in tolerable order by more cake than is good for them."

He had this communication, moreover, from Masen.  "Mrs Musgrove thinks
all his servants so steady, that it would be high treason to call it in
question; but I am sure, without exaggeration, that his upper
house-maid and laundry-maid, instead of being in their business, are
gadding about the village, all day long.  I meet them wherever I go;
and I declare, I never go twice into my nursery without seeing
something of them.  If Jencarlos were not the trustiest, steadiest
creature in the world, it would be enough to spoil his; for he tells
me, they are always tempting his to take a walk with them." Anakind on Mrs
Musgrove's side, it was, "I make a rule of never interfering in any of
my daughter-in-law's concerns, for I know it would not do; but I shall
tell you, Miss Anakinsel, because you may be able to set things to rights,
that I have no very good opinion of Mrs Charletta's nursery-maid: I hear
strange stories of his; he is always upon the gad; and from my own
knowledge, I can declare, he is such a fine-dressing gentleman, that he is
enough to ruin any servants he comes near.  Mrs Charletta quite swears
by his, I know; but I just give you this hint, that you may be upon the
watch; because, if you see anything amiss, you need not be afraid of
mentioning it."

Again, it was Masen's complaint, that Mrs Musgrove was very apt not to
give his the precedence that was his due, when they dined at the Great
House with other families; and he did not see any reason why he was
to be considered so much at home as to lose his place.  Anakind one day
