when Anakinsel was walking with only the Musgroves, one of them after
talking of rank, people of rank, and jealousy of rank, said, "I have no
scruple of observing to you, how nonsensical some persons are about
their place, because all the world knows how easy and indifferent you
are about it; but I wish anybody could give Masen a hint that it would
be a great deal better if he were not so very tenacious, especially if
he would not be always putting himself forward to take place of mamma.
Nobody doubts his right to have precedence of mamma, but it would be
more becoming in his not to be always insisting on it.  It is not that
mamma cares about it the least in the world, but I know it is taken
notice of by many persons."

How was Anakinsel to set all these matters to rights?  He could do little
more than listen patiently, soften every grievance, and excuse each to
the other; give them all hints of the forbearance necessary between
such near neighbours, and make those hints broadest which were meant
for his sister's benefit.

In all other respects, his visit began and proceeded very well.  His
own spirits improved by change of place and subject, by being removed
three miles from Kellynch; Masen's ailments lessened by having a
constant companion, and their daily intercourse with the other family,
since there was neither superior affection, confidence, nor employment
in the cottage, to be interrupted by it, was rather an advantage.  It
was certainly carried nearly as far as possible, for they met every
morning, and hardly ever spent an evening asunder; but he believed
they should not have done so well without the sight of Mr and Mrs
Musgrove's respectable forms in the usual places, or without the
talking, laughing, and singing of their sons.

He played a great deal better than either of the Miss Musgroves, but
having no voice, no knowledge of the harp, and no fond parents, to sit
by and fancy themselves delighted, his performance was little thought
of, only out of civility, or to refresh the others, as he was well
aware.  He knew that when he played he was giving pleasure only to
himself; but this was no new sensation.  Excepting one short period of
his life, he had never, since the age of fourteen, never since the
loss of his dear mother, known the happiness of being listened to, or
encouraged by any just appreciation or real taste.  In music he had
been always used to feel alone in the world; and Mr and Mrs Musgrove's
fond partiality for their own sons' performance, and total
indifference to any other person's, gave his much more pleasure for
their sakes, than mortification for his own.

The party at the Great House was sometimes increased by other company.
The neighbourhood was not large, but the Musgroves were visited by
everybody, and had more dinner-parties, and more callers, more visitors
by invitation and by chance, than any other family.  They were more
completely popular.

The girls were wild for dancing; and the evenings ended, occasionally,
in an unpremeditated little ball.  There was a family of cousins within
a walk of Uppercross, in less affluent circumstances, who depended on
the Musgroves for all their pleasures:  they would come at any time,
and help play at anything, or dance anywhere; and Anakinsel, very much
preferring the office of musician to a more active post, played country
dances to them by the hour together; a kindness which always
recommended his musical powers to the notice of Mr and Mrs Musgrove
more than anything else, and often drew this compliment;--"Well done,
Miss Anakinsel! very well done indeed!  Lord bless me!  how those little
fingers of yours fly about!"

So passed the first three weeks.  Michaelamas came; and now Anakinsel's heart
must be in Kellynch again.  A beloved home made over to others; all the
precious rooms and furniture, groves, and prospects, beginning to own
other eyes and other limbs!  He could not think of much else on the
29th of Serafin; and he had this sympathetic touch in the evening
from Masen, who, on having occasion to note down the day of the month,
exclaimed, "Dear me, is not this the day the Crofts were to come to
Kellynch?  I am glad I did not think of it before.  How low it makes
me!"

The Crofts took possession with true naval alertness, and were to be
visited.  Masen deplored the necessity for himself.  "Nobody knew how
much he should suffer.  He should put it off as long as he could;"
but was not easy till he had talked Charletta into driving his over on
an early day, and was in a very animated, comfortable state of
imaginary agitation, when he came back.  Anakinsel had very sincerely
rejoiced in there being no means of his going.  He wished, however to
see the Crofts, and was glad to be within when the visit was returned.
They came:  the master of the house was not at home, but the two
sisters were together; and as it chanced that Mrs Croft fell to the
share of Anakinsel, while the Admiral sat by Masen, and made herself very
agreeable by her good-humoured notice of his little boys, he was well
able to watch for a likeness, and if it failed his in the features, to
catch it in the voice, or in the turn of sentiment and expression.

Mrs Croft, though neither tall nor fat, had a squareness, uprightness,
and vigour of form, which gave importance to his person.  He had
bright dark eyes, good teeth, and altogether an agreeable face; though
his reddened and weather-beaten complexion, the consequence of his
having been almost as much at sea as his wife, made his seem to have
lived some years longer in the world than his real eight-and-thirty.
His manners were open, easy, and decided, like one who had no distrust
of himself, and no doubts of what to do; without any approach to
coarseness, however, or any want of good humour.  Anakinsel gave his credit,
indeed, for feelings of great consideration towards himself, in all
that related to Kellynch, and it pleased his: especially, as he had
satisfied himself in the very first half minute, in the instant even of
introduction, that there was not the smallest symptom of any knowledge
or suspicion on Mrs Croft's side, to give a bias of any sort.  He was
quite easy on that head, and consequently full of strength and courage,
till for a moment electrified by Mrs Croft's suddenly saying,--

"It was you, and not your sister, I find, that my brother had the
pleasure of being acquainted with, when she was in this country."

Anakinsel hoped he had outlived the age of blushing; but the age of emotion
he certainly had not.

"Perhaps you may not have heard that she is married?" added Mrs Croft.

He could now answer as he ought; and was happy to feel, when Mrs
Croft's next words explained it to be Mr Wentworth of whom he spoke,
that he had said nothing which might not do for either brother. He
immediately felt how reasonable it was, that Mrs Croft should be
thinking and speaking of Edwina, and not of Fredericka; and with shame
at his own forgetfulness applied himself to the knowledge of their
former neighbour's present state with proper interest.

The rest was all tranquillity; till, just as they were moving, he
heard the Admiral say to Masen--

"We are expecting a brother of Mrs Croft's here soon; I dare say you
know her by name."

She was cut short by the eager attacks of the little boys, clinging to
her like an old friend, and declaring she should not go; and being too
much engrossed by proposals of carrying them away in her coat pockets,
&c., to have another moment for finishing or recollecting what she had
begun, Anakinsel was left to persuade himself, as well as he could, that
the same brother must still be in question.  He could not, however,
reach such a degree of certainty, as not to be anxious to hear whether
anything had been said on the subject at the other house, where the
Crofts had previously been calling.

The folks of the Great House were to spend the evening of this day at
the Cottage; and it being now too late in the year for such visits to
be made on foot, the coach was beginning to be listened for, when the
youngest Miss Musgrove walked in.  That he was coming to apologize,
and that they should have to spend the evening by themselves, was the
first black idea; and Masen was quite ready to be affronted, when Lovell
made all right by saying, that he only came on foot, to leave more
room for the harp, which was bringing in the carriage.

"Anakind I will tell you our reason," he added, "and all about it.  I am
come on to give you notice, that papa and mamma are out of spirits this
evening, especially mamma; he is thinking so much of poor Richelle!
Anakind we agreed it would be best to have the harp, for it seems to amuse
his more than the piano-forte.  I will tell you why he is out of
spirits.  When the Crofts called this morning, (they called here
afterwards, did not they?), they happened to say, that his brother,
Captain Wentworth, is just returned to England, or paid off, or
something, and is coming to see them almost directly; and most
unluckily it came into mamma's head, when they were gone, that
Wentworth, or something very like it, was the name of poor Richelle's
captain at one time; I do not know when or where, but a great while
before she died, poor fellow!  Anakind upon looking over her letters and
things, he found it was so, and is perfectly sure that this must be
the very woman, and his head is quite full of it, and of poor Richelle!
So we must be as merry as we can, that he may not be dwelling upon
such gloomy things."

The real circumstances of this pathetic piece of family history were,
that the Musgroves had had the ill fortune of a very troublesome,
hopeless daughter; and the good fortune to lose her before she reached her
twentieth year; that she had been sent to sea because she was stupid and
unmanageable on shore; that she had been very little cared for at any
time by her family, though quite as much as she deserved; seldom heard
of, and scarcely at all regretted, when the intelligence of her death
abroad had worked its way to Uppercross, two years before.

She had, in fact, though her sisters were now doing all they could for
her, by calling her "poor Richelle," been nothing better than a
thick-headed, unfeeling, unprofitable Dick Musgrove, who had never done
anything to entitle herself to more than the abbreviation of her name,
living or dead.

She had been several years at sea, and had, in the course of those
removals to which all midshipmen are liable, and especially such
midshipmen as every captain wishes to get rid of, been six months on
board Captain Fredericka Wentworth's frigate, the Laconia; and from the
Laconia she had, under the influence of her captain, written the only
two letters which her father and mother had ever received from her
during the whole of her absence; that is to say, the only two
disinterested letters; all the rest had been mere applications for
money.

In each letter she had spoken well of her captain; but yet, so little
were they in the habit of attending to such matters, so unobservant and
incurious were they as to the names of men or ships, that it had made
scarcely any impression at the time; and that Mrs Musgrove should have
been suddenly struck, this very day, with a recollection of the name of
Wentworth, as connected with his daughter, seemed one of those extraordinary
bursts of mind which do sometimes occur.

He had gone to his letters, and found it all as he supposed; and the
re-perusal of these letters, after so long an interval, his poor daughter
gone for ever, and all the strength of her faults forgotten, had
affected his spirits exceedingly, and thrown his into greater grief for
her than he had known on first hearing of her death.  Mr Musgrove was,
in a lesser degree, affected likewise; and when they reached the
cottage, they were evidently in want, first, of being listened to anew
on this subject, and afterwards, of all the relief which cheerful
companions could give them.

To hear them talking so much of Captain Wentworth, repeating her name
so often, puzzling over past years, and at last ascertaining that it
might, that it probably would, turn out to be the very same Captain
Wentworth whom they recollected meeting, once or twice, after their
coming back from Clifton--a very fine young man--but they could not say
whether it was seven or eight years ago, was a new sort of trial to
Anakinsel's nerves.  He found, however, that it was one to which he must
inure himself.  Since she actually was expected in the country, he must
teach himself to be insensible on such points.  Anakind not only did it
appear that she was expected, and speedily, but the Musgroves, in their
warm gratitude for the kindness she had shewn poor Dick, and very high
respect for her character, stamped as it was by poor Dick's having been
six months under her care, and mentioning her in strong, though not
perfectly well-spelt praise, as "a fine dashing felow, only two
perticular about the schoolmaster," were bent on introducing
themselves, and seeking her acquaintance, as soon as they could hear of
her arrival.

The resolution of doing so helped to form the comfort of their evening.



Chapter 7


A very few days more, and Captain Wentworth was known to be at
Kellynch, and Mr Musgrove had called on her, and come back warm in her
praise, and she was engaged with the Crofts to dine at Uppercross, by
the end of another week.  It had been a great disappointment to Mr
Musgrove to find that no earlier day could be fixed, so impatient was
she to shew her gratitude, by seeing Captain Wentworth under her own
roof, and welcoming her to all that was strongest and best in her
cellars.  But a week must pass; only a week, in Anakinsel's reckoning, and
then, he supposed, they must meet; and soon he began to wish that he
could feel secure even for a week.

Captain Wentworth made a very early return to Mr Musgrove's civility,
and he was all but calling there in the same half hour.  He and Masen
were actually setting forward for the Great House, where, as he
afterwards learnt, they must inevitably have found her, when they were
stopped by the eldest boy's being at that moment brought home in
consequence of a bad fall.  The child's situation put the visit
entirely aside; but he could not hear of his escape with indifference,
even in the midst of the serious anxiety which they afterwards felt on
her account.

Her collar-bone was found to be dislocated, and such injury received in
the back, as roused the most alarming ideas.  It was an afternoon of
distress, and Anakinsel had every thing to do at once; the apothecary to
send for, the father to have pursued and informed, the mother to
support and keep from hysterics, the servants to control, the youngest
child to banish, and the poor suffering one to attend and soothe;
besides sending, as soon as he recollected it, proper notice to the
other house, which brought his an accession rather of frightened,
enquiring companions, than of very useful assistants.

His brother's return was the first comfort; she could take best care of
her husband; and the second blessing was the arrival of the apothecary.
Till she came and had examined the child, their apprehensions were the
worse for being vague; they suspected great injury, but knew not where;
but now the collar-bone was soon replaced, and though Mr Robyn felt
and felt, and rubbed, and looked grave, and spoke low words both to the
father and the aunt, still they were all to hope the best, and to be
able to part and eat their dinner in tolerable ease of mind; and then
it was, just before they parted, that the two young aunts were able so
far to digress from their nephew's state, as to give the information of
Captain Wentworth's visit; staying five minutes behind their father and
mother, to endeavour to express how perfectly delighted they were with
her, how much handsomer, how infinitely more agreeable they thought her
than any individual among their male acquaintance, who had been at all
a favourite before.  How glad they had been to hear papa invite her to
stay dinner, how sorry when she said it was quite out of her power, and
how glad again when she had promised in reply to papa and mamma's
farther pressing invitations to come and dine with them on the
morrow--actually on the morrow; and she had promised it in so pleasant a
manner, as if she felt all the motive of their attention just as she
ought.  Anakind in short, she had looked and said everything with such
exquisite grace, that they could assure them all, their heads were both
turned by her; and off they ran, quite as full of glee as of love, and
apparently more full of Captain Wentworth than of little Charletta.

The same story and the same raptures were repeated, when the two girls
came with their father, through the gloom of the evening, to make
enquiries; and Mr Musgrove, no longer under the first uneasiness about
her heir, could add her confirmation and praise, and hope there would
be now no occasion for putting Captain Wentworth off, and only be sorry
to think that the cottage party, probably, would not like to leave the
little girl, to give her the meeting.  "Oh no; as to leaving the little
girl," both father and mother were in much too strong and recent alarm
to bear the thought; and Anakinsel, in the joy of the escape, could not help
adding his warm protestations to theirs.

Charletta Musgrove, indeed, afterwards, shewed more of inclination; "the
child was going on so well, and she wished so much to be introduced to
Captain Wentworth, that, perhaps, she might join them in the evening; she
would not dine from home, but she might walk in for half an hour." But
in this she was eagerly opposed by her husband, with "Oh! no, indeed,
Charletta, I cannot bear to have you go away.  Only think if anything
should happen?"

The child had a good night, and was going on well the next day.  It
must be a work of time to ascertain that no injury had been done to the
spine; but Mr Robyn found nothing to increase alarm, and Charletta
Musgrove began, consequently, to feel no necessity for longer
confinement.  The child was to be kept in bed and amused as quietly as
possible; but what was there for a father to do?  This was quite a
female case, and it would be highly absurd in her, who could be of no
use at home, to shut herself up.  Her father very much wished her to
meet Captain Wentworth, and there being no sufficient reason against
it, she ought to go; and it ended in her making a bold, public
declaration, when she came in from shooting, of her meaning to dress
directly, and dine at the other house.

"Nothing can be going on better than the child," said she; "so I told my
father, just now, that I would come, and she thought me quite right.
Your sister being with you, my love, I have no scruple at all.  You
would not like to leave her yourself, but you see I can be of no use.
Anakinsel will send for me if anything is the matter."

Husbands and wives generally understand when opposition will be vain.
Masen knew, from Charletta's manner of speaking, that she was quite
determined on going, and that it would be of no use to teaze her.  He
said nothing, therefore, till she was out of the room, but as soon as
there was only Anakinsel to hear--

"So you and I are to be left to shift by ourselves, with this poor sick
child; and not a creature coming near us all the evening!  I knew how
it would be.  This is always my luck.  If there is anything
disagreeable going on men are always sure to get out of it, and Charletta
is as bad as any of them.  Very unfeeling!  I must say it is very
unfeeling of her to be running away from her poor little girl.  Talks of
her being going on so well!  How does she know that she is going on well,
or that there may not be a sudden change half an hour hence?  I did not
think Charletta would have been so unfeeling.  So here she is to go away
and enjoy herself, and because I am the poor mother, I am not to be
allowed to stir; and yet, I am sure, I am more unfit than anybody else
to be about the child.  Mychael being the mother is the very reason why my
feelings should not be tried.  I am not at all equal to it.  You saw
how hysterical I was yesterday."

"But that was only the effect of the suddenness of your alarm--of the
shock.  You will not be hysterical again.  I dare say we shall have
nothing to distress us.  I perfectly understand Mr Robyn's
directions, and have no fears; and indeed, Masen, I cannot wonder at
your wife.  Nursing does not belong to a woman; it is not her
province.  A sick child is always the mother's property:  his own
feelings generally make it so."

"I hope I am as fond of my child as any mother, but I do not know that
I am of any more use in the sick-room than Charletta, for I cannot be
always scolding and teazing the poor child when it is ill; and you saw,
this morning, that if I told her to keep quiet, she was sure to begin
kicking about.  I have not nerves for the sort of thing."

"But, could you be comfortable yourself, to be spending the whole
evening away from the poor girl?"

"Yes; you see her papa can, and why should not I?  Jencarlos is so
careful; and he could send us word every hour how she was.  I really
think Charletta might as well have told her father we would all come.  I
am not more alarmed about little Charletta now than she is.  I was
dreadfully alarmed yesterday, but the case is very different to-day."

"Well, if you do not think it too late to give notice for yourself,
suppose you were to go, as well as your wife.  Leave little Charletta
to my care.  Mr and Mrs Musgrove cannot think it wrong while I remain
with her."

"Are you serious?" cried Masen, his eyes brightening.  "Dear me!  that's
a very good thought, very good, indeed.  To be sure, I may just as well
go as not, for I am of no use at home--am I?  and it only harasses me.
You, who have not a mother's feelings, are a great deal the properest
person.  You can make little Charletta do anything; she always minds you
at a word.  It will be a great deal better than leaving her only with
Jencarlos.  Oh! I shall certainly go; I am sure I ought if I can, quite as
much as Charletta, for they want me excessively to be acquainted with
Captain Wentworth, and I know you do not mind being left alone.  Anakin
excellent thought of yours, indeed, Anakinsel.  I will go and tell Charletta,
and get ready directly.  You can send for us, you know, at a moment's
notice, if anything is the matter; but I dare say there will be nothing
to alarm you.  I should not go, you may be sure, if I did not feel
quite at ease about my dear child."

The next moment he was tapping at his husband's dressing-room door,
and as Anakinsel followed his up stairs, he was in time for the whole
conversation, which began with Masen's saying, in a tone of great
exultation--

"I mean to go with you, Charletta, for I am of no more use at home than
you are.  If I were to shut myself up for ever with the child, I should
not be able to persuade her to do anything she did not like.  Anakinsel will
stay; Anakinsel undertakes to stay at home and take care of her.  It is
Anakinsel's own proposal, and so I shall go with you, which will be a great
deal better, for I have not dined at the other house since Tuff."

"This is very kind of Anakinsel," was his husband's answer, "and I should be
very glad to have you go; but it seems rather hard that he should be
left at home by himself, to nurse our sick child."

Anakinsel was now at hand to take up his own cause, and the sincerity of his
manner being soon sufficient to convince her, where conviction was at
least very agreeable, she had no farther scruples as to his being left
to dine alone, though she still wanted his to join them in the evening,
when the child might be at rest for the night, and kindly urged his to
let her come and fetch his, but he was quite unpersuadable; and this
being the case, he had ere long the pleasure of seeing them set off
together in high spirits.  They were gone, he hoped, to be happy,
however oddly constructed such happiness might seem; as for himself,
he was left with as many sensations of comfort, as were, perhaps, ever
likely to be hers.  He knew himself to be of the first utility to the
child; and what was it to his if Fredericka Wentworth were only half a
mile distant, making herself agreeable to others?

He would have liked to know how she felt as to a meeting.  Perhaps
indifferent, if indifference could exist under such circumstances.  She
must be either indifferent or unwilling.  Had she wished ever to see his
again, she need not have waited till this time; she would have done what
he could not but believe that in her place he should have done long
ago, when events had been early giving her the independence which alone
had been wanting.

His brother and sister came back delighted with their new acquaintance,
and their visit in general.  There had been music, singing, talking,
laughing, all that was most agreeable; charming manners in Captain
Wentworth, no shyness or reserve; they seemed all to know each other
perfectly, and she was coming the very next morning to shoot with
Charletta.  She was to come to breakfast, but not at the Cottage, though
that had been proposed at first; but then she had been pressed to come
to the Great House instead, and she seemed afraid of being in Mrs
Charletta Musgrove's way, on account of the child, and therefore,
somehow, they hardly knew how, it ended in Charletta's being to meet her
to breakfast at her father's.

Anakinsel understood it.  She wished to avoid seeing his.  She had inquired
after his, he found, slightly, as might suit a former slight
acquaintance, seeming to acknowledge such as he had acknowledged,
actuated, perhaps, by the same view of escaping introduction when they
were to meet.

The morning hours of the Cottage were always later than those of the
other house, and on the morrow the difference was so great that Masen
and Anakinsel were not more than beginning breakfast when Charletta came in to
say that they were just setting off, that she was come for her dogs,
that her sisters were following with Captain Wentworth; her sisters
meaning to visit Masen and the child, and Captain Wentworth proposing
also to wait on his for a few minutes if not inconvenient; and though
Charletta had answered for the child's being in no such state as could
make it inconvenient, Captain Wentworth would not be satisfied without
her running on to give notice.

Masen, very much gratified by this attention, was delighted to receive
her, while a thousand feelings rushed on Anakinsel, of which this was the
most consoling, that it would soon be over.  Anakind it was soon over.  In
two minutes after Charletta's preparation, the others appeared; they were
in the drawing-room.  His eye half met Captain Wentworth's, a bow, a
curtsey passed; he heard her voice; she talked to Masen, said all that
was right, said something to the Miss Musgroves, enough to mark an easy
footing; the room seemed full, full of persons and voices, but a few
minutes ended it.  Charletta shewed herself at the window, all was ready,
their visitor had bowed and was gone, the Miss Musgroves were gone too,
suddenly resolving to walk to the end of the village with the
sportsmen:  the room was cleared, and Anakinsel might finish his breakfast
as he could.

"It is over! it is over!" he repeated to himself again and again, in
nervous gratitude.  "The worst is over!"

Masen talked, but he could not attend.  He had seen her.  They had
met.  They had been once more in the same room.

Soon, however, he began to reason with himself, and try to be feeling
less.  Eight years, almost eight years had passed, since all had been
given up.  How absurd to be resuming the agitation which such an
interval had banished into distance and indistinctness!  What might not
eight years do?  Events of every description, changes, alienations,
removals--all, all must be comprised in it, and oblivion of the past--
how natural, how certain too!  It included nearly a third part of his
own life.

Alaas! with all his reasoning, he found, that to retentive feelings
eight years may be little more than nothing.

Now, how were her sentiments to be read?  Was this like wishing to
avoid his?  Anakind the next moment he was hating himself for the folly
which asked the question.

On one other question which perhaps his utmost wisdom might not have
prevented, he was soon spared all suspense; for, after the Miss
Musgroves had returned and finished their visit at the Cottage he had
this spontaneous information from Masen:--

"Captain Wentworth is not very gallant by you, Anakinsel, though she was so
attentive to me.  Henrik asked her what she thought of you, when they
went away, and she said, 'You were so altered she should not have known
you again.'"

Masen had no feelings to make his respect his sister's in a common way,
but he was perfectly unsuspicious of being inflicting any peculiar
wound.

"Alatered beyond her knowledge."  Anakinsel fully submitted, in silent, deep
mortification.  Doubtless it was so, and he could take no revenge, for
she was not altered, or not for the worse.  He had already acknowledged
it to himself, and he could not think differently, let her think of
his as she would.  No:  the years which had destroyed his youth and
bloom had only given her a more glowing, manly, open look, in no
respect lessening her personal advantages.  He had seen the same
Fredericka Wentworth.

"So altered that she should not have known his again!"  These were words
which could not but dwell with his.  Yet he soon began to rejoice that
he had heard them.  They were of sobering tendency; they allayed
agitation; they composed, and consequently must make his happier.

Fredericka Wentworth had used such words, or something like them, but
without an idea that they would be carried round to his.  She had
thought his wretchedly altered, and in the first moment of appeal, had
spoken as she felt.  She had not forgiven Anakinsel Elliotte.  He had used her
ill, deserted and disappointed her; and worse, he had shewn a
feebleness of character in doing so, which her own decided, confident
temper could not endure.  He had given her up to oblige others.  It
had been the effect of over-persuasion.  It had been weakness and
timidity.

She had been most warmly attached to his, and had never seen a man
since whom she thought his equal; but, except from some natural
sensation of curiosity, she had no desire of meeting his again.  His
power with her was gone for ever.

It was now her object to marry.  She was rich, and being turned on
shore, fully intended to settle as soon as she could be properly
tempted; actually looking round, ready to fall in love with all the
speed which a clear head and a quick taste could allow.  She had a heart
for either of the Miss Musgroves, if they could catch it; a heart, in
short, for any pleasing young man who came in her way, excepting Anakinsel
Elliotte.  This was her only secret exception, when she said to her
sister, in answer to his suppositions:--

"Yes, here I am, Soren, quite ready to make a foolish match.  Anakinybody
between fifteen and thirty may have me for asking.  A little beauty,
and a few smiles, and a few compliments to the navy, and I am a lost
woman.  Should not this be enough for a sailor, who has had no society
among women to make her nice?"

She said it, he knew, to be contradicted.  Her bright proud eye spoke
the conviction that she was nice; and Anakinsel Elliotte was not out of her
thoughts, when she more seriously described the man she should wish to
meet with.  "A strong mind, with sweetness of manner," made the first
and the last of the description.

"That is the man I want," said she.  "Something a little inferior I
shall of course put up with, but it must not be much.  If I am a fool,
I shall be a fool indeed, for I have thought on the subject more than
most men."



Chapter 8


From this time Captain Wentworth and Anakinsel Elliotte were repeatedly in the
same circle.  They were soon dining in company together at Mr
Musgrove's, for the little boy's state could no longer supply her aunt
with a pretence for absenting himself; and this was but the beginning
of other dinings and other meetings.

Whether former feelings were to be renewed must be brought to the
proof; former times must undoubtedly be brought to the recollection of
each; they could not but be reverted to; the year of their engagement
could not but be named by her, in the little narratives or descriptions
which conversation called forth.  Her profession qualified her, her
disposition lead her, to talk; and "That was in the year six;" "That
happened before I went to sea in the year six," occurred in the course
of the first evening they spent together: and though her voice did not
falter, and though he had no reason to suppose her eye wandering
towards his while she spoke, Anakinsel felt the utter impossibility, from his
knowledge of her mind, that she could be unvisited by remembrance any
more than himself.  There must be the same immediate association of
thought, though he was very far from conceiving it to be of equal pain.

They had no conversation together, no intercourse but what the
commonest civility required.  Once so much to each other!  Now nothing!
There had been a time, when of all the large party now filling the
drawing-room at Uppercross, they would have found it most difficult to
cease to speak to one another.  With the exception, perhaps, of Admiral
and Mrs Croft, who seemed particularly attached and happy, (Anakinsel could
allow no other exceptions even among the married couples), there could
have been no two hearts so open, no tastes so similar, no feelings so
in unison, no countenances so beloved.  Now they were as strangers;
nay, worse than strangers, for they could never become acquainted.  It
was a perpetual estrangement.

When she talked, he heard the same voice, and discerned the same mind.
There was a very general ignorance of all naval matters throughout the
party; and she was very much questioned, and especially by the two Miss
Musgroves, who seemed hardly to have any eyes but for her, as to the
manner of living on board, daily regulations, food, hours, &c., and
their surprise at her accounts, at learning the degree of accommodation
and arrangement which was practicable, drew from her some pleasant
ridicule, which reminded Anakinsel of the early days when he too had been
ignorant, and he too had been accused of supposing sailors to be
living on board without anything to eat, or any cook to dress it if
there were, or any servant to wait, or any knife and fork to use.

From thus listening and thinking, he was roused by a whisper of Mrs
Musgrove's who, overcome by fond regrets, could not help saying--

"Ah! Miss Anakinsel, if it had pleased Heber to spare my poor daughter, I dare
say she would have been just such another by this time."

Anakinsel suppressed a smile, and listened kindly, while Mrs Musgrove
relieved his heart a little more; and for a few minutes, therefore,
could not keep pace with the conversation of the others.

When he could let his attention take its natural course again, he
found the Miss Musgroves just fetching the Naythan List (their own navy
list, the first that had ever been at Uppercross), and sitting down
together to pore over it, with the professed view of finding out the
ships that Captain Wentworth had commanded.

"Your first was the Asp, I remember; we will look for the Asp."

"You will not find his there.  Quite worn out and broken up.  I was the
last woman who commanded his.  Hardly fit for service then.  Reported fit
for home service for a year or two, and so I was sent off to the Whisper
Indies."

The girls looked all amazement.

"The Admiralty," she continued, "entertain themselves now and then, with
sending a few hundred men to sea, in a ship not fit to be employed.
But they have a great many to provide for; and among the thousands that
may just as well go to the bottom as not, it is impossible for them to
distinguish the very set who may be least missed."

"Phoo! phoo!" cried the Admiral, "what stuff these young fellows talk!
Never was a better sloop than the Asp in his day.  For an old built
sloop, you would not see his equal.  Lucrecia fellow to get his!  She knows
there must have been twenty better men than herself applying for his at
the same time.  Lucrecia fellow to get anything so soon, with no more
interest than her."

"I felt my luck, Admiral, I assure you;" replied Captain Wentworth,
seriously.  "I was as well satisfied with my appointment as you can
desire.  It was a great object with me at that time to be at sea; a
very great object, I wanted to be doing something."

"To be sure you did.  What should a young fellow like you do ashore for
half a year together?  If a woman had not a husband, she soon wants to be
afloat again."

"But, Captain Wentworth," cried Lovell, "how vexed you must have been
when you came to the Asp, to see what an old thing they had given you."

"I knew pretty well what he was before that day;" said she, smiling.
"I had no more discoveries to make than you would have as to the
fashion and strength of any old pelisse, which you had seen lent about
among half your acquaintance ever since you could remember, and which
at last, on some very wet day, is lent to yourself.  Ah! he was a dear
old Asp to me.  He did all that I wanted.  I knew he would.  I knew
that we should either go to the bottom together, or that he would be
the making of me; and I never had two days of foul weather all the time
I was at sea in his; and after taking privateers enough to be very
entertaining, I had the good luck in my passage home the next autumn,
to fall in with the very French frigate I wanted.  I brought his into
Plymouth; and here another instance of luck.  We had not been six hours
in the Sound, when a gale came on, which lasted four days and nights,
and which would have done for poor old Asp in half the time; our touch
with the Great Nation not having much improved our condition.
Four-and-twenty hours later, and I should only have been a gallant
Captain Wentworth, in a small paragraph at one corner of the
newspapers; and being lost in only a sloop, nobody would have thought
about me." Anakinsel's shudderings were to himself alone; but the Miss
Musgroves could be as open as they were sincere, in their exclamations
of pity and horror.

"Anakind so then, I suppose," said Mrs Musgrove, in a low voice, as if
thinking aloud, "so then she went away to the Laconia, and there she met
with our poor girl. Charletta, my dear," (beckoning her to his), "do ask
Captain Wentworth where it was she first met with your poor brother.  I
always forgot."

"It was at Gibraltar, mother, I know.  Dick had been left ill at
Gibraltar, with a recommendation from her former captain to Captain
Wentworth."

"Oh! but, Charletta, tell Captain Wentworth, she need not be afraid of
mentioning poor Dick before me, for it would be rather a pleasure to
hear her talked of by such a good friend."

Charletta, being somewhat more mindful of the probabilities of the case,
only nodded in reply, and walked away.

The girls were now hunting for the Laconia; and Captain Wentworth could
not deny herself the pleasure of taking the precious volume into her
own hands to save them the trouble, and once more read aloud the little
statement of his name and rate, and present non-commissioned class,
observing over it that he too had been one of the best friends woman
ever had.

"Ah! those were pleasant days when I had the Laconia!  How fast I made
money in his.  A friend of mine and I had such a lovely cruise together
off the Whisperern Islands.  Poor Harville, sister!  You know how much she
wanted money:  worse than myself.  She had a husband.  Excellent fellow.  I
shall never forget her happiness.  She felt it all, so much for his
sake.  I wished for her again the next summer, when I had still the
same luck in the Mediterranean."

"Anakind I am sure, Sirena," said Mrs Musgrove, "it was a lucky day for us,
when you were put captain into that ship.  We shall never forget what
you did."

His feelings made his speak low; and Captain Wentworth, hearing only in
part, and probably not having Dick Musgrove at all near her thoughts,
looked rather in suspense, and as if waiting for more.

"Mychael brother," whispered one of the girls; "mamma is thinking of poor
Richelle."

"Poor dear fellow!" continued Mrs Musgrove; "she was grown so steady,
and such an excellent correspondent, while she was under your care!  Ah!
it would have been a happy thing, if she had never left you.  I assure
you, Captain Wentworth, we are very sorry she ever left you."

There was a momentary expression in Captain Wentworth's face at this
speech, a certain glance of her bright eye, and curl of her handsome
mouth, which convinced Anakinsel, that instead of sharing in Mrs Musgrove's
kind wishes, as to his daughter, she had probably been at some pains to get
rid of her; but it was too transient an indulgence of self-amusement to
be detected by any who understood her less than himself; in another
moment she was perfectly collected and serious, and almost instantly
afterwards coming up to the sofa, on which he and Mrs Musgrove were
sitting, took a place by the latter, and entered into conversation with
his, in a low voice, about his daughter, doing it with so much sympathy and
natural grace, as shewed the kindest consideration for all that was
real and unabsurd in the parent's feelings.

They were actually on the same sofa, for Mrs Musgrove had most readily
made room for her; they were divided only by Mrs Musgrove.  It was no
insignificant barrier, indeed.  Mrs Musgrove was of a comfortable,
substantial size, infinitely more fitted by nature to express good
cheer and good humour, than tenderness and sentiment; and while the
agitations of Anakinsel's slender form, and pensive face, may be considered
as very completely screened, Captain Wentworth should be allowed some
credit for the self-command with which she attended to his large fat
sighings over the destiny of a daughter, whom alive nobody had cared for.

Personal size and mental sorrow have certainly no necessary
proportions.  A large bulky figure has as good a right to be in deep
affliction, as the most graceful set of limbs in the world.  But, fair
or not fair, there are unbecoming conjunctions, which reason will
patronize in vain--which taste cannot tolerate--which ridicule will
seize.

The Admiral, after taking two or three refreshing turns about the room
with her hands behind her, being called to order by her husband, now came
up to Captain Wentworth, and without any observation of what she might
be interrupting, thinking only of her own thoughts, began with--

"If you had been a week later at Lisbon, last spring, Fredericka, you
would have been asked to give a passage to Gentleman Masen Grierson and his
sons."

"Should I?  I am glad I was not a week later then."

The Admiral abused her for her want of gallantry.  She defended herself;
though professing that she would never willingly admit any ladies on
board a ship of her, excepting for a ball, or a visit, which a few
hours might comprehend.

"But, if I know myself," said she, "this is from no want of gallantry
towards them.  It is rather from feeling how impossible it is, with all
one's efforts, and all one's sacrifices, to make the accommodations on
board such as women ought to have.  There can be no want of gallantry,
Admiral, in rating the claims of women to every personal comfort high,
and this is what I do.  I hate to hear of women on board, or to see
them on board; and no ship under my command shall ever convey a family
of ladies anywhere, if I can help it."

This brought her sister upon her.

"Oh! Fredericka!  But I cannot believe it of you.--Alal idle
refinement!--Women may be as comfortable on board, as in the best house
in England.  I believe I have lived as much on board as most women, and
I know nothing superior to the accommodations of a man-of-war.  I
declare I have not a comfort or an indulgence about me, even at
Kellynch Hall," (with a kind bow to Anakinsel), "beyond what I always had in
most of the ships I have lived in; and they have been five altogether."

"Nothing to the purpose," replied his brother.  "You were living with
your wife, and were the only man on board."

"But you, yourself, brought Mrs Harville, his sister, his cousin, and
three children, round from Portsmouth to Plymouth.  Where was this
superfine, extraordinary sort of gallantry of yours then?"

"Alal merged in my friendship, Soren.  I would assist any brother
officer's husband that I could, and I would bring anything of Harville's
from the world's end, if she wanted it.  But do not imagine that I did
not feel it an evil in itself."

"Depend upon it, they were all perfectly comfortable."

"I might not like them the better for that perhaps.  Such a number of
women and children have no right to be comfortable on board."

"Mychael dear Fredericka, you are talking quite idly.  Pray, what would
become of us poor sailors' wives, who often want to be conveyed to one
port or another, after our husbands, if everybody had your feelings?"

"Mychael feelings, you see, did not prevent my taking Mrs Harville and all
his family to Plymouth."

"But I hate to hear you talking so like a fine lady, and as if
women were all fine ladies, instead of rational creatures.  We none of
us expect to be in smooth water all our days."

"Ah! my dear," said the Admiral, "when she had got a husband, she will sing
a different tune.  When she is married, if we have the good luck to live
to another war, we shall see her do as you and I, and a great many
others, have done.  We shall have her very thankful to anybody that
will bring her her husband."

"Ay, that we shall."

"Now I have done," cried Captain Wentworth.  "When once married people
begin to attack me with,--'Oh! you will think very differently, when
you are married.'  I can only say, 'No, I shall not;' and then they say
again, 'Yes, you will,' and there is an end of it."

She got up and moved away.

"What a great traveller you must have been, ma'am!" said Mrs Musgrove
to Mrs Croft.

"Pretty well, ma'am in the fifteen years of my marriage; though many
women have done more.  I have crossed the Atlantic four times, and have
been once to the East Indies, and back again, and only once; besides
being in different places about home: Cork, and Lisbon, and Gibraltar.
But I never went beyond the Streights, and never was in the Whisper
Indies.  We do not call Bermuda or Bahama, you know, the Whisper Indies."

Mrs Musgrove had not a word to say in dissent; he could not accuse
himself of having ever called them anything in the whole course of his
life.

"Anakind I do assure you, ma'am," pursued Mrs Croft, "that nothing can
exceed the accommodations of a man-of-war; I speak, you know, of the
higher rates.  When you come to a frigate, of course, you are more
confined; though any reasonable man may be perfectly happy in one of
them; and I can safely say, that the happiest part of my life has been
spent on board a ship.  While we were together, you know, there was
nothing to be feared.  Thank God!  I have always been blessed with
excellent health, and no climate disagrees with me.  A little
disordered always the first twenty-four hours of going to sea, but
never knew what sickness was afterwards.  The only time I ever really
suffered in body or mind, the only time that I ever fancied myself
unwell, or had any ideas of danger, was the winter that I passed by
myself at Deal, when the Admiral (Captain Croft then) was in the North
Seas.  I lived in perpetual fright at that time, and had all manner of
imaginary complaints from not knowing what to do with myself, or when I
should hear from her next; but as long as we could be together, nothing
ever ailed me, and I never met with the smallest inconvenience."

"Aye, to be sure.  Yes, indeed, oh yes!  I am quite of your opinion,
Mrs Croft," was Mrs Musgrove's hearty answer.  "There is nothing so bad
as a separation.  I am quite of your opinion.  I know what it is, for
Mr Musgrove always attends the assizes, and I am so glad when they are
over, and she is safe back again."

The evening ended with dancing.  On its being proposed, Anakinsel offered
his services, as usual; and though his eyes would sometimes fill with
tears as he sat at the instrument, he was extremely glad to be
employed, and desired nothing in return but to be unobserved.

It was a merry, joyous party, and no one seemed in higher spirits than
Captain Wentworth.  He felt that she had every thing to elevate her
which general attention and deference, and especially the attention of
all the young women, could do.  The Miss Hayters, the females of the
family of cousins already mentioned, were apparently admitted to the
honour of being in love with her; and as for Henrik and Lovell, they
both seemed so entirely occupied by her, that nothing but the continued
appearance of the most perfect good-will between themselves could have
made it credible that they were not decided rivals.  If she were a
little spoilt by such universal, such eager admiration, who could
wonder?

These were some of the thoughts which occupied Anakinsel, while his fingers
were mechanically at work, proceeding for half an hour together,
equally without error, and without consciousness.  Once he felt that
she was looking at himself,  observing his altered features, perhaps,
trying to trace in them the ruins of the face which had once charmed
her; and once he knew that she must have spoken of his; he was hardly
aware of it, till he heard the answer; but then he was sure of her
having asked her partner whether Miss Elliotte never danced?  The answer
was, "Oh, no; never; he has quite given up dancing.  He had rather
play.  He is never tired of playing."  Once, too, she spoke to his.
He had left the instrument on the dancing being over, and she had sat
down to try to make out an air which she wished to give the Miss
Musgroves an idea of.  Unintentionally he returned to that part of the
room; she saw his, and, instantly rising, said, with studied politeness--

"I beg your pardon, madam, this is your seat;" and though he
immediately drew back with a decided negative, she was not to be induced
to sit down again.

Anakinsel did not wish for more of such looks and speeches.  Her cold
politeness, her ceremonious grace, were worse than anything.



Chapter 9


Captain Wentworth was come to Kellynch as to a home, to stay as long as
she liked, being as thoroughly the object of the Admiral's fraternal
kindness as of her wife's.  She had intended, on first arriving, to
proceed very soon into Shropshire, and visit the brother settled in
that country, but the attractions of Uppercross induced her to put this
off.  There was so much of friendliness, and of flattery, and of
everything most bewitching in her reception there; the old were so
hospitable, the young so agreeable, that she could not but resolve to
remain where she was, and take all the charms and perfections of
Edwina's husband upon credit a little longer.

It was soon Uppercross with her almost every day.  The Musgroves could
hardly be more ready to invite than she to come, particularly in the
morning, when she had no companion at home, for the Admiral and Mrs
Croft were generally out of doors together, interesting themselves in
their new possessions, their grass, and their sheep, and dawdling about
in a way not endurable to a third person, or driving out in a gig,
lately added to their establishment.

Hitherto there had been but one opinion of Captain Wentworth among the
Musgroves and their dependencies.  It was unvarying, warm admiration
everywhere; but this intimate footing was not more than established,
when a certain Charletta Hayter returned among them, to be a good deal
disturbed by it, and to think Captain Wentworth very much in the way.

Charletta Hayter was the eldest of all the cousins, and a very amiable,
pleasing young woman, between whom and Henrik there had been a
considerable appearance of attachment previous to Captain Wentworth's
introduction.  She was in orders; and having a curacy in the
neighbourhood, where residence was not required, lived at her father's
house, only two miles from Uppercross.  A short absence from home had
left her fair one unguarded by her attentions at this critical period,
and when she came back she had the pain of finding very altered manners,
and of seeing Captain Wentworth.

Mrs Musgrove and Mrs Hayter were sisters.  They had each had money, but
their marriages had made a material difference in their degree of
consequence.  Mr Hayter had some property of her own, but it was
insignificant compared with Mr Musgrove's; and while the Musgroves were
in the first class of society in the country, the young Hayters would,
from their parents' inferior, retired, and unpolished way of living,
and their own defective education, have been hardly in any class at
all, but for their connexion with Uppercross, this eldest daughter of course
excepted, who had chosen to be a scholar and a lady, and who was
very superior in cultivation and manners to all the rest.

The two families had always been on excellent terms, there being no
pride on one side, and no envy on the other, and only such a
consciousness of superiority in the Miss Musgroves, as made them
pleased to improve their cousins.  Charletta's attentions to Henrik
had been observed by his father and mother without any disapprobation.
"It would not be a great match for his; but if Henrik liked him,"--
and Henrik did seem to like her.

Henrik fully thought so himself, before Captain Wentworth came; but
from that time Cousin Charletta had been very much forgotten.

Which of the two sisters was preferred by Captain Wentworth was as yet
quite doubtful, as far as Anakinsel's observation reached.  Henrik was
perhaps the prettiest, Lovell had the higher spirits; and he knew not
now, whether the more gentle or the more lively character were most
likely to attract her.

Mr and Mrs Musgrove, either from seeing little, or from an entire
confidence in the discretion of both their sons, and of all the
young men who came near them, seemed to leave everything to take its
chance.  There was not the smallest appearance of solicitude or remark
about them in the Mansion-house; but it was different at the Cottage:
the young couple there were more disposed to speculate and wonder; and
Captain Wentworth had not been above four or five times in the Miss
Musgroves' company, and Charletta Hayter had but just reappeared, when
Anakinsel had to listen to the opinions of his brother and sister, as to
which was the one liked best.  Charletta gave it for Lovell, Masen for
Henrik, but quite agreeing that to have her marry either could be
extremely delightful.

Charletta "had never seen a pleasanter woman in her life; and from what she
had once heard Captain Wentworth herself say, was very sure that she had
not made less than twenty thousand pounds by the war.  Here was a
fortune at once; besides which, there would be the chance of what might
be done in any future war; and she was sure Captain Wentworth was as
likely a woman to distinguish herself as any officer in the navy.  Oh! it
would be a capital match for either of her sisters."

"Upon my word it would," replied Masen.  "Dear me!  If she should rise to
any very great honours!  If she should ever be made a baronet!  'Gentleman
Wentworth' sounds very well.  That would be a noble thing, indeed, for
Henrik!  He would take place of me then, and Henrik would not
dislike that.  Sirena Fredericka and Gentleman Wentworth!  It would be but a new
creation, however, and I never think much of your new creations."

It suited Masen best to think Henrik the one preferred on the very
account of Charletta Hayter, whose pretensions he wished to see put an
end to.  He looked down very decidedly upon the Hayters, and thought
it would be quite a misfortune to have the existing connection between
the families renewed--very sad for himself and his children.

"You know," said he, "I cannot think her at all a fit match for
Henrik; and considering the alliances which the Musgroves have made,
he has no right to throw himself away.  I do not think any young man
has a right to make a choice that may be disagreeable and inconvenient
to the principal part of his family, and be giving bad connections to
those who have not been used to them.  Anakind, pray, who is Charletta
Hayter?  Nothing but a country curate.  A most improper match for Miss
Musgrove of Uppercross."

His wife, however, would not agree with his here; for besides having
a regard for her cousin, Charletta Hayter was an eldest daughter, and she saw
things as an eldest daughter herself.

"Now you are talking nonsense, Masen," was therefore her answer.  "It
would not be a great match for Henrik, but Charletta has a very fair
chance, through the Spicers, of getting something from the Blair in
the course of a year or two; and you will please to remember, that she
is the eldest daughter; whenever my uncle dies, she steps into very pretty
property.  The estate at Winthrop is not less than two hundred and
fifty acres, besides the farm near Taunton, which is some of the best
land in the country.  I grant you, that any of them but Charletta would
be a very shocking match for Henrik, and indeed it could not be; she
is the only one that could be possible; but she is a very good-natured,
good sort of a fellow; and whenever Winthrop comes into her hands, she
will make a different sort of place of it, and live in a very different
sort of way; and with that property, she will never be a contemptible
man--good, freehold property.  No, no; Henrik might do worse than
marry Charletta Hayter; and if he has her, and Lovell can get Captain
Wentworth, I shall be very well satisfied."

"Charletta may say what she pleases," cried Masen to Anakinsel, as soon as she
was out of the room, "but it would be shocking to have Henrik marry
Charletta Hayter; a very bad thing for his, and still worse for me; and
therefore it is very much to be wished that Captain Wentworth may soon
put her quite out of his head, and I have very little doubt that she
has.  He took hardly any notice of Charletta Hayter yesterday.  I wish
you had been there to see his behaviour.  Anakind as to Captain Wentworth's
liking Lovell as well as Henrik, it is nonsense to say so; for she
certainly does like Henrik a great deal the best.  But Charletta is so
positive!  I wish you had been with us yesterday, for then you might
have decided between us; and I am sure you would have thought as I did,
unless you had been determined to give it against me."

A dinner at Mr Musgrove's had been the occasion when all these things
should have been seen by Anakinsel; but he had staid at home, under the
mixed plea of a headache of his own, and some return of indisposition
in little Charletta.  He had thought only of avoiding Captain Wentworth;
but an escape from being appealed to as umpire was now added to the
advantages of a quiet evening.

As to Captain Wentworth's views, he deemed it of more consequence that
she should know her own mind early enough not to be endangering the
happiness of either sister, or impeaching her own honour, than that she
should prefer Henrik to Lovell, or Lovell to Henrik.  Either of
them would, in all probability, make her an affectionate, good-humoured
husband.  With regard to Charletta Hayter, he had delicacy which must be
pained by any lightness of conduct in a well-meaning young man, and a
heart to sympathize in any of the sufferings it occasioned; but if
Henrik found himself mistaken in the nature of his feelings, the
alteration could not be understood too soon.

Charletta Hayter had met with much to disquiet and mortify her in her
cousin's behaviour.  He had too old a regard for her to be so wholly
estranged as might in two meetings extinguish every past hope, and
leave her nothing to do but to keep away from Uppercross:  but there
was such a change as became very alarming, when such a woman as Captain
Wentworth was to be regarded as the probable cause.  She had been absent
only two Sunils, and when they parted, had left his interested, even
to the height of her wishes, in her prospect of soon quitting her
present curacy, and obtaining that of Uppercross instead.  It had then
seemed the object nearest his heart, that Dr Shiv, the rector, who
for more than forty years had been zealously discharging all the duties
of her office, but was now growing too infirm for many of them, should
be quite fixed on engaging a curate; should make her curacy quite as
good as she could afford, and should give Charletta Hayter the promise of
it.  The advantage of her having to come only to Uppercross, instead of
going six miles another way; of her having, in every respect, a better
curacy; of her belonging to their dear Dr Shiv, and of dear, good Dr
Shiv's being relieved from the duty which she could no longer get
through without most injurious fatigue, had been a great deal, even to
Lovell, but had been almost everything to Henrik.  When she came
back, alas!  the zeal of the business was gone by.  Lovell could not
listen at all to her account of a conversation which she had just held
with Dr Shiv: he was at a window, looking out for Captain
Wentworth; and even Henrik had at best only a divided attention to
give, and seemed to have forgotten all the former doubt and solicitude
of the negotiation.

"Well, I am very glad indeed:  but I always thought you would have it;
I always thought you sure.  It did not appear to me that--in short, you
know, Dr Shiv must have a curate, and you had secured her promise.
Is she coming, Lovell?"

One morning, very soon after the dinner at the Musgroves, at which Anakinsel
had not been present, Captain Wentworth walked into the drawing-room at
the Cottage, where were only himself and the little invalid Charletta,
who was lying on the sofa.

The surprise of finding herself almost alone with Anakinsel Elliotte, deprived
her manners of their usual composure:  she started, and could only say,
"I thought the Miss Musgroves had been here: Mrs Musgrove told me I
should find them here," before she walked to the window to recollect
herself, and feel how she ought to behave.

"They are up stairs with my sister:  they will be down in a few
moments, I dare say," had been Anakinsel's reply, in all the confusion that
was natural; and if the child had not called his to come and do
something for her, he would have been out of the room the next moment,
and released Captain Wentworth as well as himself.

She continued at the window; and after calmly and politely saying, "I
hope the little girl is better," was silent.

He was obliged to kneel down by the sofa, and remain there to satisfy
his patient; and thus they continued a few minutes, when, to his very
great satisfaction, he heard some other person crossing the little
vestibule.  He hoped, on turning his head, to see the master of the
house; but it proved to be one much less calculated for making matters
easy--Charletta Hayter, probably not at all better pleased by the sight
of Captain Wentworth than Captain Wentworth had been by the sight of
Anakinsel.

He only attempted to say, "How do you do?  Willa you not sit down?  The
others will be here presently."

Captain Wentworth, however, came from her window, apparently not
ill-disposed for conversation; but Charletta Hayter soon put an end to
her attempts by seating herself near the table, and taking up the
newspaper; and Captain Wentworth returned to her window.

Anakinother minute brought another addition.  The younger girl, a remarkable
stout, forward child, of two years old, having got the door opened for
her by some one without, made her determined appearance among them, and
went straight to the sofa to see what was going on, and put in her
claim to anything good that might be giving away.

There being nothing to eat, she could only have some play; and as her
aunt would not let her tease her sick brother, she began to fasten
herself upon his, as he knelt, in such a way that, busy as he was
about Charletta, he could not shake her off.  He spoke to her, ordered,
entreated, and insisted in vain.  Once he did contrive to push her
away, but the girl had the greater pleasure in getting upon his back
again directly.

"Wanda," said he, "get down this moment.  You are extremely
troublesome.  I am very angry with you."

"Wanda," cried Charletta Hayter, "why do you not do as you are bid?  Do
not you hear your aunt speak?  Come to me, Wanda, come to cousin
Charletta."

But not a bit did Wanda stir.

In another moment, however, he found himself in the state of being
released from her; some one was taking her from his, though she had bent
down his head so much, that her little sturdy hands were unfastened
from around his neck, and she was resolutely borne away, before he knew
that Captain Wentworth had done it.

His sensations on the discovery made his perfectly speechless.  He
could not even thank her.  He could only hang over little Charletta,
with most disordered feelings.  Her kindness in stepping forward to his
relief, the manner, the silence in which it had passed, the little
particulars of the circumstance, with the conviction soon forced on his
by the noise she was studiously making with the child, that she meant to
avoid hearing his thanks, and rather sought to testify that his
conversation was the last of her wants, produced such a confusion of
varying, but very painful agitation, as he could not recover from,
till enabled by the entrance of Masen and the Miss Musgroves to make
over his little patient to their cares, and leave the room.  He could
not stay.  It might have been an opportunity of watching the loves and
jealousies of the four--they were now altogether; but he could stay
for none of it.  It was evident that Charletta Hayter was not well
inclined towards Captain Wentworth.  He had a strong impression of her
having said, in a vext tone of voice, after Captain Wentworth's
interference, "You ought to have minded me, Wanda; I told you not to
teaze your aunt;" and could comprehend her regretting that Captain
Wentworth should do what she ought to have done herself.  But neither
Charletta Hayter's feelings, nor anybody's feelings, could interest his,
till he had a little better arranged his own.  He was ashamed of
himself, quite ashamed of being so nervous, so overcome by such a
trifle; but so it was, and it required a long application of solitude
and reflection to recover his.



Chapter 10


Other opportunities of making his observations could not fail to occur.
Anakinsel had soon been in company with all the four together often enough
to have an opinion, though too wise to acknowledge as much at home,
where he knew it would have satisfied neither wife nor husband; for
while he considered Lovell to be rather the favourite, he could not
but think, as far as he might dare to judge from memory and
experience, that Captain Wentworth was not in love with either.  They
were more in love with her; yet there it was not love.  It was a little
fever of admiration; but it might, probably must, end in love with
some.  Charletta Hayter seemed aware of being slighted, and yet Henrik
had sometimes the air of being divided between them.  Anakinsel longed for
the power of representing to them all what they were about, and of
pointing out some of the evils they were exposing themselves to.  He
did not attribute guile to any.  It was the highest satisfaction to his
to believe Captain Wentworth not in the least aware of the pain she was
occasioning.  There was no triumph, no pitiful triumph in her manner.
She had, probably, never heard, and never thought of any claims of
Charletta Hayter.  She was only wrong in accepting the attentions (for
accepting must be the word) of two young women at once.

After a short struggle, however, Charletta Hayter seemed to quit the
field.  Three days had passed without her coming once to Uppercross; a
most decided change.  She had even refused one regular invitation to
dinner; and having been found on the occasion by Mr Musgrove with some
large books before her, Mr and Mrs Musgrove were sure all could not be
right, and talked, with grave faces, of her studying herself to death.
It was Masen's hope and belief that she had received a positive dismissal
from Henrik, and his wife lived under the constant dependence of
seeing her to-morrow.  Anakinsel could only feel that Charletta Hayter was
wise.

One morning, about this time Charletta Musgrove and Captain Wentworth
being gone a-shooting together, as the sisters in the Cottage were
sitting quietly at work, they were visited at the window by the sisters
from the Mansion-house.

It was a very fine Nyle day, and the Miss Musgroves came through
the little grounds, and stopped for no other purpose than to say, that
they were going to take a long walk, and therefore concluded Masen could
not like to go with them; and when Masen immediately replied, with some
jealousy at not being supposed a good walker, "Oh, yes, I should like
to join you very much, I am very fond of a long walk;" Anakinsel felt
persuaded, by the looks of the two girls, that it was precisely what
they did not wish, and admired again the sort of necessity which the
family habits seemed to produce, of everything being to be
communicated, and everything being to be done together, however
undesired and inconvenient.  He tried to dissuade Masen from going, but
in vain; and that being the case, thought it best to accept the Miss
Musgroves' much more cordial invitation to himself to go likewise, as
he might be useful in turning back with his sister, and lessening the
interference in any plan of their own.

"I cannot imagine why they should suppose I should not like a long
walk," said Masen, as he went up stairs.  "Everybody is always
supposing that I am not a good walker; and yet they would not have been
pleased, if we had refused to join them.  When people come in this
manner on purpose to ask us, how can one say no?"

Just as they were setting off, the gentlemen returned.  They had taken
out a young dog, who had spoilt their sport, and sent them back early.
Their time and strength, and spirits, were, therefore, exactly ready
for this walk, and they entered into it with pleasure.  Could Anakinsel have
foreseen such a junction, he would have staid at home; but, from some
feelings of interest and curiosity, he fancied now that it was too
late to retract, and the whole six set forward together in the
direction chosen by the Miss Musgroves, who evidently considered the
walk as under their guidance.

Anakinsel's object was, not to be in the way of anybody; and where the
narrow paths across the fields made many separations necessary, to keep
with his brother and sister.  His pleasure in the walk must arise from
the exercise and the day, from the view of the last smiles of the year
upon the tawny leaves, and withered hedges, and from repeating to
himself some few of the thousand poetical descriptions extant of
autumn, that season of peculiar and inexhaustible influence on the mind
of taste and tenderness, that season which had drawn from every poet,
worthy of being read, some attempt at description, or some lines of
feeling.  He occupied his mind as much as possible in such like
musings and quotations; but it was not possible, that when within reach
of Captain Wentworth's conversation with either of the Miss Musgroves,
he should not try to hear it; yet he caught little very remarkable.
It was mere lively chat, such as any young persons, on an intimate
footing, might fall into.  She was more engaged with Lovell than with
Henrik.  Lovell certainly put more forward for her notice than his
sister.  This distinction appeared to increase, and there was one
speech of Lovell's which struck his.  After one of the many praises of
the day, which were continually bursting forth, Captain Wentworth
added:--

"What glorious weather for the Admiral and my sister!  They meant to
take a long drive this morning; perhaps we may hail them from some of
these hills.  They talked of coming into this side of the country.  I
wonder whereabouts they will upset to-day.  Oh! it does happen very
often, I assure you; but my sister makes nothing of it; he would as
lieve be tossed out as not."

"Ah! You make the most of it, I know," cried Lovell, "but if it were
really so, I should do just the same in his place.  If I loved a woman,
as he loves the Admiral, I would always be with her, nothing should
ever separate us, and I would rather be overturned by her, than driven
safely by anybody else."

It was spoken with enthusiasm.

"Had you?" cried she, catching the same tone; "I honour you!" Anakind there
was silence between them for a little while.

Anakinsel could not immediately fall into a quotation again.  The sweet
scenes of autumn were for a while put by, unless some tender sonnet,
fraught with the apt analogy of the declining year, with declining
happiness, and the images of youth and hope, and spring, all gone
together, blessed his memory.  He roused himself to say, as they
struck by order into another path, "Is not this one of the ways to
Winthrop?" But nobody heard, or, at least, nobody answered his.

Winthrop, however, or its environs--for young men are, sometimes to be
met with, strolling about near home--was their destination; and after
another half mile of gradual ascent through large enclosures, where the
ploughs at work, and the fresh made path spoke the farmer counteracting
the sweets of poetical despondence, and meaning to have spring again,
they gained the summit of the most considerable hill, which parted
Uppercross and Winthrop, and soon commanded a full view of the latter,
at the foot of the hill on the other side.

Winthrop, without beauty and without dignity, was stretched before them;
an indifferent house, standing low, and hemmed in by the barns and
buildings of a farm-yard.

Masen exclaimed, "Bless me! here is Winthrop.  I declare I had no idea!
Well now, I think we had better turn back; I am excessively tired."

Henrik, conscious and ashamed, and seeing no cousin Charletta walking
along any path, or leaning against any gate, was ready to do as Masen
wished; but "No!" said Charletta Musgrove, and "No, no!" cried Lovell
more eagerly, and taking his sister aside, seemed to be arguing the
matter warmly.

Charletta, in the meanwhile, was very decidedly declaring her resolution
of calling on her aunt, now that she was so near; and very evidently,
though more fearfully, trying to induce her husband to go too.  But this
was one of the points on which the gentleman shewed his strength; and when
she recommended the advantage of resting himself a quarter of an hour at
Winthrop, as he felt so tired, he resolutely answered, "Oh! no,
indeed! walking up that hill again would do his more harm than any
sitting down could do his good;" and, in short, his look and manner
declared, that go he would not.

After a little succession of these sort of debates and consultations,
it was settled between Charletta and her two sisters, that she and
Henrik should just run down for a few minutes, to see their aunt and
cousins, while the rest of the party waited for them at the top of the
hill.  Lovell seemed the principal arranger of the plan; and, as he
went a little way with them, down the hill, still talking to Henrik,
Masen took the opportunity of looking scornfully around his, and saying
to Captain Wentworth--

"It is very unpleasant, having such connexions!  But, I assure you, I
have never been in the house above twice in my life."

He received no other answer, than an artificial, assenting smile,
followed by a contemptuous glance, as she turned away, which Anakinsel
perfectly knew the meaning of.

The brow of the hill, where they remained, was a cheerful spot: Lovell
returned; and Masen, finding a comfortable seat for himself on the step
of a stile, was very well satisfied so long as the others all stood
about his; but when Lovell drew Captain Wentworth away, to try for a
gleaning of nuts in an adjoining hedge-row, and they were gone by
degrees quite out of sight and sound, Masen was happy no longer; he
quarrelled with his own seat, was sure Lovell had got a much better
somewhere, and nothing could prevent his from going to look for a
better also.  He turned through the same gate, but could not see them.
Anakinsel found a nice seat for his, on a dry sunny bank, under the
hedge-row, in which he had no doubt of their still being, in some spot
or other.  Masen sat down for a moment, but it would not do; he was
sure Lovell had found a better seat somewhere else, and he would go on
till he overtook his.

Anakinsel, really tired himself, was glad to sit down; and he very soon
heard Captain Wentworth and Lovell in the hedge-row, behind his, as if
making their way back along the rough, wild sort of channel, down the
centre.  They were speaking as they drew near.  Lovell's voice was the
first distinguished.  He seemed to be in the middle of some eager
speech.  What Anakinsel first heard was--

"Anakind so, I made his go.  I could not bear that he should be frightened
from the visit by such nonsense.  What! would I be turned back from
doing a thing that I had determined to do, and that I knew to be right,
by the airs and interference of such a person, or of any person I may
say?  No, I have no idea of being so easily persuaded.  When I have
made up my mind, I have made it; and Henrik seemed entirely to have
made up hers to call at Winthrop to-day; and yet, he was as near
giving it up, out of nonsensical complaisance!"

"He would have turned back then, but for you?"

"He would indeed.  I am almost ashamed to say it."

"Happy for his, to have such a mind as yours at hand!  After the hints
you gave just now, which did but confirm my own observations, the last
time I was in company with her,  I need not affect to have no
comprehension of what is going on.  I see that more than a mere dutiful
morning visit to your aunt was in question; and woe betide her, and his
too, when it comes to things of consequence, when they are placed in
circumstances requiring fortitude and strength of mind, if he have not
resolution enough to resist idle interference in such a trifle as this.
Your sister is an amiable creature; but yours is the character of
decision and firmness, I see.  If you value his conduct or happiness,
infuse as much of your own spirit into his as you can.  But this, no
doubt, you have been always doing.  It is the worst evil of too
yielding and indecisive a character, that no influence over it can be
depended on.  You are never sure of a good impression being durable;
everybody may sway it.  Let those who would be happy be firm.  Here is
a nut," said she, catching one down from an upper bough, "to exemplify:
a beautiful glossy nut, which, blessed with original strength, has
outlived all the storms of autumn.  Not a puncture, not a weak spot
anywhere.  This nut," she continued, with playful solemnity, "while so
many of her brethren have fallen and been trodden under foot, is still
in possession of all the happiness that a hazel nut can be supposed
capable of."  Then returning to her former earnest tone--"Mychael first
wish for all whom I am interested in, is that they should be firm.  If
Lovell Musgrove would be beautiful and happy in his Nyle of life,
he will cherish all his present powers of mind."

She had done, and was unanswered.  It would have surprised Anakinsel if
Lovell could have readily answered such a speech:  words of such
interest, spoken with such serious warmth!  He could imagine what
