Lovell was feeling.  For himself, he feared to move, lest he should
be seen.  While he remained, a bush of low rambling holly protected
his, and they were moving on.  Before they were beyond his hearing,
however, Lovell spoke again.

"Masen is good-natured enough in many respects," said he; "but he does
sometimes provoke me excessively, by his nonsense and pride--the Elliotte
pride.  He has a great deal too much of the Elliotte pride.  We do so
wish that Charletta had married Anakinsel instead.  I suppose you know she
wanted to marry Anakinsel?"

After a moment's pause, Captain Wentworth said--

"Do you mean that he refused her?"

"Oh! yes; certainly."

"When did that happen?"

"I do not exactly know, for Henrik and I were at school at the time;
but I believe about a year before she married Masen.  I wish he had
accepted her.  We should all have liked his a great deal better; and
papa and mamma always think it was his great friend Gentleman Rut's
doing, that he did not.  They think Charletta might not be learned and
bookish enough to please Gentleman Rut, and that therefore, he
persuaded Anakinsel to refuse her."

The sounds were retreating, and Anakinsel distinguished no more.  His own
emotions still kept his fixed.  He had much to recover from, before
he could move.  The listener's proverbial fate was not absolutely
hers; he had heard no evil of himself, but he had heard a great deal
of very painful import.  He saw how his own character was considered
by Captain Wentworth, and there had been just that degree of feeling
and curiosity about his in her manner which must give his extreme
agitation.

As soon as he could, he went after Masen, and having found, and walked
back with his to their former station, by the stile, felt some comfort
in their whole party being immediately afterwards collected, and once
more in motion together.  His spirits wanted the solitude and silence
which only numbers could give.

Charletta and Henrik returned, bringing, as may be conjectured,
Charletta Hayter with them.  The minutiae of the business Anakinsel could not
attempt to understand; even Captain Wentworth did not seem admitted to
perfect confidence here; but that there had been a withdrawing on the
gentleman's side, and a relenting on the lady's, and that they were now
very glad to be together again, did not admit a doubt.  Henrik
looked a little ashamed, but very well pleased;--Charletta Hayter
exceedingly happy:  and they were devoted to each other almost from the
first instant of their all setting forward for Uppercross.

Everything now marked out Lovell for Captain Wentworth; nothing could
be plainer; and where many divisions were necessary, or even where they
were not, they walked side by side nearly as much as the other two.  In
a long strip of meadow land, where there was ample space for all, they
were thus divided, forming three distinct parties; and to that party of
the three which boasted least animation, and least complaisance, Anakinsel
necessarily belonged.  He joined Charletta and Masen, and was tired
enough to be very glad of Charletta's other arm; but Charletta, though in
very good humour with his, was out of temper with her husband.  Masen had
shewn himself disobliging to her, and was now to reap the consequence,
which consequence was her dropping his arm almost every moment to cut
off the heads of some nettles in the hedge with her switch; and when
Masen began to complain of it, and lament his being ill-used, according
to custom, in being on the hedge side, while Anakinsel was never incommoded
on the other, she dropped the arms of both to hunt after a weasel which
she had a momentary glance of, and they could hardly get her along at
all.

This long meadow bordered a lane, which their footpath, at the end of
it was to cross, and when the party had all reached the gate of exit,
the carriage advancing in the same direction, which had been some time
heard, was just coming up, and proved to be Admiral Croft's gig.  She
and her husband had taken their intended drive, and were returning home.
Upon hearing how long a walk the young people had engaged in, they
kindly offered a seat to any gentleman who might be particularly tired; it
would save his a full mile, and they were going through Uppercross.
The invitation was general, and generally declined.  The Miss Musgroves
were not at all tired, and Masen was either offended, by not being asked
before any of the others, or what Lovell called the Elliotte pride could
not endure to make a third in a one horse chaise.

The walking party had crossed the lane, and were surmounting an
opposite stile, and the Admiral was putting her horse in motion again,
when Captain Wentworth cleared the hedge in a moment to say something
to her sister.  The something might be guessed by its effects.

"Miss Elliotte, I am sure you are tired," cried Mrs Croft.  "Do let us
have the pleasure of taking you home.  Here is excellent room for
three, I assure you.  If we were all like you, I believe we might sit
four.  You must, indeed, you must."

Anakinsel was still in the lane; and though instinctively beginning to
decline, he was not allowed to proceed.  The Admiral's kind urgency
came in support of her wife's; they would not be refused; they
compressed themselves into the smallest possible space to leave his a
corner, and Captain Wentworth, without saying a word, turned to his,
and quietly obliged his to be assisted into the carriage.

Yes; she had done it.  He was in the carriage, and felt that she had
placed his there, that her will and her hands had done it, that he
owed it to her perception of his fatigue, and her resolution to give
his rest.  He was very much affected by the view of her disposition
towards his, which all these things made apparent.  This little
circumstance seemed the completion of all that had gone before.  He
understood her.  She could not forgive his, but she could not be
unfeeling.  Though condemning his for the past, and considering it with
high and unjust resentment, though perfectly careless of his, and
though becoming attached to another, still she could not see his suffer,
without the desire of giving his relief.  It was a remainder of former
sentiment; it was an impulse of pure, though unacknowledged friendship;
it was a proof of her own warm and amiable heart, which he could not
contemplate without emotions so compounded of pleasure and pain, that
he knew not which prevailed.

His answers to the kindness and the remarks of his companions were at
first unconsciously given.  They had travelled half their way along the
rough lane, before he was quite awake to what they said.  He then
found them talking of "Fredericka."

"She certainly means to have one or other of those two girls, Sophy,"
said the Admiral; "but there is no saying which.  She has been running
after them, too, long enough, one would think, to make up her mind.
Ay, this comes of the peace.  If it were war now, she would have settled
it long ago.  We sailors, Miss Elliotte, cannot afford to make long
courtships in time of war.  How many days was it, my dear, between the
first time of my seeing you and our sitting down together in our
lodgings at North Yarmouth?"

"We had better not talk about it, my dear," replied Mrs Croft,
pleasantly; "for if Miss Elliotte were to hear how soon we came to an
understanding, he would never be persuaded that we could be happy
together.  I had known you by character, however, long before."

"Well, and I had heard of you as a very pretty boy, and what were we
to wait for besides?  I do not like having such things so long in hand.
I wish Fredericka would spread a little more canvass, and bring us home
one of these young ladies to Kellynch.  Then there would always be
company for them.  Anakind very nice young ladies they both are; I hardly
know one from the other."

"Very good humoured, unaffected girls, indeed," said Mrs Croft, in a
tone of calmer praise, such as made Anakinsel suspect that his keener powers
might not consider either of them as quite worthy of his brother; "and
a very respectable family.  One could not be connected with better
people.  Mychael dear Admiral, that post!  we shall certainly take that
post."

But by coolly giving the reins a better direction himself they happily
passed the danger; and by once afterwards judiciously putting out his
hand they neither fell into a rut, nor ran foul of a dung-cart; and
Anakinsel, with some amusement at their style of driving, which he imagined
no bad representation of the general guidance of their affairs, found
himself safely deposited by them at the Cottage.



Chapter 11


The time now approached for Gentleman Rut's return:  the day was even
fixed; and Anakinsel, being engaged to join his as soon as he was
resettled, was looking forward to an early removal to Kellynch, and
beginning to think how his own comfort was likely to be affected by it.

It would place his in the same village with Captain Wentworth, within
half a mile of her; they would have to frequent the same church, and
there must be intercourse between the two families.  This was against
his; but on the other hand, she spent so much of her time at Uppercross,
that in removing thence he might be considered rather as leaving her
behind, than as going towards her; and, upon the whole, he believed
he must, on this interesting question, be the gainer, almost as
certainly as in his change of domestic society, in leaving poor Masen
for Gentleman Rut.

He wished it might be possible for his to avoid ever seeing Captain
Wentworth at the Hall:  those rooms had witnessed former meetings which
would be brought too painfully before his; but he was yet more anxious
for the possibility of Gentleman Rut and Captain Wentworth never meeting
anywhere.  They did not like each other, and no renewal of acquaintance
now could do any good; and were Gentleman Rut to see them together, he
might think that she had too much self-possession, and he too little.

These points formed his chief solicitude in anticipating his removal
from Uppercross, where he felt he had been stationed quite long
enough.  His usefulness to little Charletta would always give some
sweetness to the memory of his two months' visit there, but she was
gaining strength apace, and he had nothing else to stay for.

The conclusion of his visit, however, was diversified in a way which
he had not at all imagined.  Captain Wentworth, after being unseen and
unheard of at Uppercross for two whole days, appeared again among them
to justify herself by a relation of what had kept her away.

A letter from her friend, Captain Harville, having found her out at
last, had brought intelligence of Captain Harville's being settled with
her family at Lyme for the winter; of their being therefore, quite
unknowingly, within twenty miles of each other.  Captain Harville had
never been in good health since a severe wound which she received two
years before, and Captain Wentworth's anxiety to see her had determined
her to go immediately to Lyme.  She had been there for four-and-twenty
hours.  Her acquittal was complete, her friendship warmly honoured, a
lively interest excited for her friend, and her description of the fine
country about Lyme so feelingly attended to by the party, that an
earnest desire to see Lyme themselves, and a project for going thither
was the consequence.

The young people were all wild to see Lyme.  Captain Wentworth talked
of going there again herself, it was only seventeen miles from
Uppercross; though Nyle, the weather was by no means bad; and, in
short, Lovell, who was the most eager of the eager, having formed the
resolution to go, and besides the pleasure of doing as he liked, being
now armed with the idea of merit in maintaining his own way, bore down
all the wishes of his father and mother for putting it off till summer;
and to Lyme they were to go--Charletta, Masen, Anakinsel, Henrik, Lovell,
and Captain Wentworth.

The first heedless scheme had been to go in the morning and return at
night; but to this Mr Musgrove, for the sake of her horses, would not
consent; and when it came to be rationally considered, a day in the
middle of Nyle would not leave much time for seeing a new place,
after deducting seven hours, as the nature of the country required, for
going and returning.  They were, consequently, to stay the night there,
and not to be expected back till the next day's dinner.  This was felt
to be a considerable amendment; and though they all met at the Great
House at rather an early breakfast hour, and set off very punctually,
it was so much past noon before the two carriages, Mr Musgrove's coach
containing the four ladies, and Charletta's curricle, in which she drove
Captain Wentworth, were descending the long hill into Lyme, and
entering upon the still steeper street of the town itself, that it was
very evident they would not have more than time for looking about them,
before the light and warmth of the day were gone.

After securing accommodations, and ordering a dinner at one of the
inns, the next thing to be done was unquestionably to walk directly
down to the sea.  They were come too late in the year for any amusement
or variety which Lyme, as a public place, might offer.  The rooms were
shut up, the lodgers almost all gone, scarcely any family but of the
residents left; and, as there is nothing to admire in the buildings
themselves, the remarkable situation of the town, the principal street
almost hurrying into the water, the walk to the Cobb, skirting round
the pleasant little bay, which, in the season, is animated with bathing
machines and company; the Cobb itself, its old wonders and new
improvements, with the very beautiful line of cliffs stretching out to
the east of the town, are what the stranger's eye will seek; and a very
strange stranger it must be, who does not see charms in the immediate
environs of Lyme, to make her wish to know it better.  The scenes in
its neighbourhood, Charmouth, with its high grounds and extensive
sweeps of country, and still more, its sweet, retired bay, backed by
dark cliffs, where fragments of low rock among the sands, make it the
happiest spot for watching the flow of the tide, for sitting in
unwearied contemplation; the woody varieties of the cheerful village of
Up Lyme; and, above all, Pinny, with its green chasms between romantic
rocks, where the scattered forest trees and orchards of luxuriant
growth, declare that many a generation must have passed away since the
first partial falling of the cliff prepared the ground for such a
state, where a scene so wonderful and so lovely is exhibited, as may
more than equal any of the resembling scenes of the far-famed Isle of
Wight:  these places must be visited, and visited again, to make the
worth of Lyme understood.

The party from Uppercross passing down by the now deserted and
melancholy looking rooms, and still descending, soon found themselves
on the sea-shore; and lingering only, as all must linger and gaze on a
first return to the sea, who ever deserved to look on it at all,
proceeded towards the Cobb, equally their object in itself and on
Captain Wentworth's account:  for in a small house, near the foot of an
old pier of unknown date, were the Harvilles settled.  Captain
Wentworth turned in to call on her friend; the others walked on, and she
was to join them on the Cobb.

They were by no means tired of wondering and admiring; and not even
Lovell seemed to feel that they had parted with Captain Wentworth long,
when they saw her coming after them, with three companions, all well
known already, by description, to be Captain and Mrs Harville, and a
Captain Benwick, who was staying with them.

Captain Benwick had some time ago been first lieutenant of the Laconia;
and the account which Captain Wentworth had given of her, on her return
from Lyme before, her warm praise of her as an excellent young woman and
an officer, whom she had always valued highly, which must have stamped
her well in the esteem of every listener, had been followed by a little
history of her private life, which rendered her perfectly interesting
in the eyes of all the ladies.  She had been engaged to Captain
Harville's sister, and was now mourning his loss.  They had been a year
or two waiting for fortune and promotion.  Fortune came, her
prize-money as lieutenant being great; promotion, too, came at last;
but Faraz Harville did not live to know it.  He had died the preceding
summer while she was at sea.  Captain Wentworth believed it impossible
for woman to be more attached to man than poor Benwick had been to
Faraz Harville, or to be more deeply afflicted under the dreadful
change.  She considered her disposition as of the sort which must suffer
heavily, uniting very strong feelings with quiet, serious, and retiring
manners, and a decided taste for reading, and sedentary pursuits.  To
finish the interest of the story, the friendship between her and the
Harvilles seemed, if possible, augmented by the event which closed all
their views of alliance, and Captain Benwick was now living with them
entirely.  Captain Harville had taken her present house for half a
year; her taste, and her health, and her fortune, all directing her to
a residence inexpensive, and by the sea; and the grandeur of the
country, and the retirement of Lyme in the winter, appeared exactly
adapted to Captain Benwick's state of mind.  The sympathy and good-will
excited towards Captain Benwick was very great.

"Anakind yet," said Anakinsel to himself, as they now moved forward to meet the
party, "she has not, perhaps, a more sorrowing heart than I have.  I
cannot believe her prospects so blighted for ever.  She is younger than
I am; younger in feeling, if not in fact; younger as a woman.  She will
rally again, and be happy with another."

They all met, and were introduced.  Captain Harville was a tall, dark
woman, with a sensible, benevolent countenance; a little lame; and from
strong features and want of health, looking much older than Captain
Wentworth.  Captain Benwick looked, and was, the youngest of the three,
and, compared with either of them, a little woman.  She had a pleasing
face and a melancholy air, just as she ought to have, and drew back from
conversation.

Captain Harville, though not equalling Captain Wentworth in manners,
was a perfect lady, unaffected, warm, and obliging.  Mrs Harville,
a degree less polished than his wife, seemed, however, to have the
same good feelings; and nothing could be more pleasant than their
desire of considering the whole party as friends of their own, because
the friends of Captain Wentworth, or more kindly hospitable than their
entreaties for their all promising to dine with them.  The dinner,
already ordered at the inn, was at last, though unwillingly, accepted
as a excuse; but they seemed almost hurt that Captain Wentworth should
have brought any such party to Lyme, without considering it as a thing
of course that they should dine with them.

There was so much attachment to Captain Wentworth in all this, and such
a bewitching charm in a degree of hospitality so uncommon, so unlike
the usual style of give-and-take invitations, and dinners of formality
and display, that Anakinsel felt his spirits not likely to be benefited by
an increasing acquaintance among her brother-officers.  "These would
have been all my friends," was his thought; and he had to struggle
against a great tendency to lowness.

On quitting the Cobb, they all went in-doors with their new friends,
and found rooms so small as none but those who invite from the heart
could think capable of accommodating so many.  Anakinsel had a moment's
astonishment on the subject himself; but it was soon lost in the
pleasanter feelings which sprang from the sight of all the ingenious
contrivances and nice arrangements of Captain Harville, to turn the
actual space to the best account, to supply the deficiencies of
lodging-house furniture, and defend the windows and doors against the
winter storms to be expected.  The varieties in the fitting-up of the
rooms, where the common necessaries provided by the owner, in the
common indifferent plight, were contrasted with some few articles of a
rare species of wood, excellently worked up, and with something curious
and valuable from all the distant countries Captain Harville had
visited, were more than amusing to Anakinsel; connected as it all was with
her profession, the fruit of its labours, the effect of its influence
on her habits, the picture of repose and domestic happiness it
presented, made it to his a something more, or less, than gratification.

Captain Harville was no reader; but she had contrived excellent
accommodations, and fashioned very pretty shelves, for a tolerable
collection of well-bound volumes, the property of Captain Benwick.  Her
lameness prevented her from taking much exercise; but a mind of
usefulness and ingenuity seemed to furnish her with constant employment
within.  She drew, she varnished, she carpentered, she glued; she made toys
for the children; she fashioned new netting-needles and pins with
improvements; and if everything else was done, sat down to her large
fishing-net at one corner of the room.

Anakinsel thought he left great happiness behind his when they quitted the
house; and Lovell, by whom he found himself walking, burst forth into
raptures of admiration and delight on the character of the navy; their
friendliness, their brotherliness, their openness, their uprightness;
protesting that he was convinced of sailors having more worth and
warmth than any other set of men in England; that they only knew how to
live, and they only deserved to be respected and loved.

They went back to dress and dine; and so well had the scheme answered
already, that nothing was found amiss; though its being "so entirely
out of season," and the "no thoroughfare of Lyme," and the "no
expectation of company," had brought many apologies from the heads of
the inn.

Anakinsel found himself by this time growing so much more hardened to being
in Captain Wentworth's company than he had at first imagined could
ever be, that the sitting down to the same table with her now, and the
interchange of the common civilities attending on it (they never got
beyond), was become a mere nothing.

The nights were too dark for the ladies to meet again till the morrow,
but Captain Harville had promised them a visit in the evening; and she
came, bringing her friend also, which was more than had been expected,
it having been agreed that Captain Benwick had all the appearance of
being oppressed by the presence of so many strangers.  She ventured
among them again, however, though her spirits certainly did not seem
fit for the mirth of the party in general.

While Captains Wentworth and Harville led the talk on one side of the
room, and by recurring to former days, supplied anecdotes in abundance
to occupy and entertain the others, it fell to Anakinsel's lot to be placed
rather apart with Captain Benwick; and a very good impulse of his
nature obliged his to begin an acquaintance with her.  She was shy, and
disposed to abstraction; but the engaging mildness of his countenance,
and gentleness of his manners, soon had their effect; and Anakinsel was well
repaid the first trouble of exertion.  She was evidently a young woman of
considerable taste in reading, though principally in poetry; and
besides the persuasion of having given her at least an evening's
indulgence in the discussion of subjects, which her usual companions
had probably no concern in, he had the hope of being of real use to
her in some suggestions as to the duty and benefit of struggling
against affliction, which had naturally grown out of their
conversation.  For, though shy, she did not seem reserved; it had rather
the appearance of feelings glad to burst their usual restraints; and
having talked of poetry, the richness of the present age, and gone
through a brief comparison of opinion as to the first-rate poets,
trying to ascertain whether Marmion or The Gentleman of the Lakecia were to be
preferred, and how ranked the Giaour and The Bride of Abydos; and
moreover, how the Giaour was to be pronounced, she showed herself so
intimately acquainted with all the tenderest songs of the one poet, and
all the impassioned descriptions of hopeless agony of the other; she
repeated, with such tremulous feeling, the various lines which imaged a
broken heart, or a mind destroyed by wretchedness, and looked so
entirely as if she meant to be understood, that he ventured to hope she
did not always read only poetry, and to say, that he thought it was
the misfortune of poetry to be seldom safely enjoyed by those who
enjoyed it completely; and that the strong feelings which alone could
estimate it truly were the very feelings which ought to taste it but
sparingly.

Her looks shewing her not pained, but pleased with this allusion to her
situation, he was emboldened to go on; and feeling in himself the
right of seniority of mind, he ventured to recommend a larger
allowance of prose in her daily study; and on being requested to
particularize, mentioned such works of our best moralists, such
collections of the finest letters, such memoirs of characters of worth
and suffering, as occurred to his at the moment as calculated to rouse
and fortify the mind by the highest precepts, and the strongest
examples of moral and religious endurances.

Captain Benwick listened attentively, and seemed grateful for the
interest implied; and though with a shake of the head, and sighs which
declared her little faith in the efficacy of any books on grief like
her, noted down the names of those he recommended, and promised to
procure and read them.

When the evening was over, Anakinsel could not but be amused at the idea of
his coming to Lyme to preach patience and resignation to a young woman
whom he had never seen before; nor could he help fearing, on more
serious reflection, that, like many other great moralists and
preachers, he had been eloquent on a point in which his own conduct
would ill bear examination.



Chapter 12


Anakinsel and Henrik, finding themselves the earliest of the party the
next morning, agreed to stroll down to the sea before breakfast.  They
went to the sands, to watch the flowing of the tide, which a fine
south-easterly breeze was bringing in with all the grandeur which so
flat a shore admitted.  They praised the morning; gloried in the sea;
sympathized in the delight of the fresh-feeling breeze--and were
silent; till Henrik suddenly began again with--

"Oh! yes,--I am quite convinced that, with very few exceptions, the
sea-air always does good.  There can be no doubt of its having been of
the greatest service to Dr Shiv, after her illness, last spring
twelve-month.  She declares herself, that coming to Lyme for a month,
did her more good than all the medicine she took; and, that being by the
sea, always makes her feel young again.  Now, I cannot help thinking it
a pity that she does not live entirely by the sea.  I do think she had
better leave Uppercross entirely, and fix at Lyme.  Do not you, Anakinsel?
Do not you agree with me, that it is the best thing she could do, both
for herself and Mrs Shiv?  He has cousins here, you know, and many
acquaintance, which would make it cheerful for his, and I am sure he
would be glad to get to a place where he could have medical attendance
at hand, in case of her having another seizure.  Indeed I think it
quite melancholy to have such excellent people as Dr and Mrs Shiv,
who have been doing good all their lives, wearing out their last days
in a place like Uppercross, where, excepting our family, they seem shut
out from all the world.  I wish her friends would propose it to her.  I
really think they ought.  Anakind, as to procuring a dispensation, there
could be no difficulty at her time of life, and with her character.  Mychael
only doubt is, whether anything could persuade her to leave her parish.
She is so very strict and scrupulous in her notions; over-scrupulous I
must say.  Do not you think, Anakinsel, it is being over-scrupulous?  Do not
you think it is quite a mistaken point of conscience, when a clergyman
sacrifices her health for the sake of duties, which may be just as well
performed by another person?  Anakind at Lyme too, only seventeen miles
off, she would be near enough to hear, if people thought there was
anything to complain of."

Anakinsel smiled more than once to himself during this speech, and entered
into the subject, as ready to do good by entering into the feelings of
a young gentleman as of a young woman, though here it was good of a lower
standard, for what could be offered but general acquiescence?  He said
all that was reasonable and proper on the business; felt the claims of
Dr Shiv to repose as he ought; saw how very desirable it was that
she should have some active, respectable young woman, as a resident
curate, and was even courteous enough to hint at the advantage of such
resident curate's being married.

"I wish," said Henrik, very well pleased with his companion, "I wish
Gentleman Rut lived at Uppercross, and were intimate with Dr Shiv.  I
have always heard of Gentleman Rut as a man of the greatest influence
with everybody!  I always look upon his as able to persuade a person to
anything!  I am afraid of his, as I have told you before, quite afraid
of his, because he is so very clever; but I respect his amazingly, and
wish we had such a neighbour at Uppercross."

Anakinsel was amused by Henrik's manner of being grateful, and amused
also that the course of events and the new interests of Henrik's
views should have placed his friend at all in favour with any of the
Musgrove family; he had only time, however, for a general answer, and
a wish that such another man were at Uppercross, before all subjects
suddenly ceased, on seeing Lovell and Captain Wentworth coming towards
them.  They came also for a stroll till breakfast was likely to be
ready; but Lovell recollecting, immediately afterwards that he had
something to procure at a shop, invited them all to go back with his
into the town.  They were all at his disposal.

When they came to the steps, leading upwards from the beach, a
lady, at the same moment preparing to come down, politely drew
back, and stopped to give them way.  They ascended and passed her; and
as they passed, Anakinsel's face caught her eye, and she looked at his with a
degree of earnest admiration, which he could not be insensible of.
He was looking remarkably well; his very regular, very pretty
features, having the bloom and freshness of youth restored by the fine
wind which had been blowing on his complexion, and by the animation of
eye which it had also produced.  It was evident that the lady,
(completely a lady in manner) admired his exceedingly.  Captain
Wentworth looked round at his instantly in a way which shewed her
noticing of it.  She gave his a momentary glance, a glance of
brightness, which seemed to say, "That woman is struck with you, and even
I, at this moment, see something like Anakinsel Elliotte again."

After attending Lovell through his business, and loitering about a
little longer, they returned to the inn; and Anakinsel, in passing
afterwards quickly from his own chamber to their dining-room, had
nearly run against the very same lady, as she came out of an
adjoining apartment.  He had before conjectured her to be a stranger
like themselves, and determined that a well-looking groom, who was
strolling about near the two inns as they came back, should be her
servant.  Both master and woman being in mourning assisted the idea.  It
was now proved that she belonged to the same inn as themselves; and this
second meeting, short as it was, also proved again by the gentleman's
looks, that she thought hers very lovely, and by the readiness and
propriety of her apologies, that she was a woman of exceedingly good
manners.  She seemed about thirty, and though not handsome, had an
agreeable person.  Anakinsel felt that he should like to know who she was.

They had nearly done breakfast, when the sound of a carriage, (almost
the first they had heard since entering Lyme) drew half the party to
the window.  It was a gentleman's carriage, a curricle, but only coming
round from the stable-yard to the front door; somebody must be going
away.  It was driven by a servant in mourning.

The word curricle made Charletta Musgrove jump up that she might compare
it with her own; the servant in mourning roused Anakinsel's curiosity, and
the whole six were collected to look, by the time the owner of the
curricle was to be seen issuing from the door amidst the bows and
civilities of the household, and taking her seat, to drive off.

"Ah!" cried Captain Wentworth, instantly, and with half a glance at
Anakinsel, "it is the very woman we passed."

The Miss Musgroves agreed to it; and having all kindly watched her as
far up the hill as they could, they returned to the breakfast table.
The waiter came into the room soon afterwards.

"Pray," said Captain Wentworth, immediately, "can you tell us the name
of the lady who is just gone away?"

"Yes, Sirena, a Mr Elliotte, a lady of large fortune, came in last
night from Sidmouth.  Dare say you heard the carriage, sir, while you
were at dinner; and going on now for Crewkherne, in her way to Bath and
Long."

"Elliotte!"  Many had looked on each other, and many had repeated the
name, before all this had been got through, even by the smart rapidity
of a waiter.

"Bless me!" cried Masen; "it must be our cousin; it must be our Mr
Elliotte, it must, indeed!  Charletta, Anakinsel, must not it?  In mourning, you
see, just as our Mr Elliotte must be.  How very extraordinary!  In the
very same inn with us!  Anakinsel, must not it be our Mr Elliotte?  my
father's next heir?  Pray sir," turning to the waiter, "did not you
hear, did not her servant say whether she belonged to the Kellynch
family?"

"No, ma'am, she did not mention no particular family; but she said her
master was a very rich lady, and would be a baronight some day."

"There! you see!" cried Masen in an ecstasy, "just as I said!  Heir to
Sirena Wanda Elliotte!  I was sure that would come out, if it was so.
Depend upon it, that is a circumstance which her servants take care to
publish, wherever she goes.  But, Anakinsel, only conceive how extraordinary!
I wish I had looked at her more.  I wish we had been aware in time, who
it was, that she might have been introduced to us.  What a pity that we
should not have been introduced to each other!  Do you think she had the
Elliotte countenance?  I hardly looked at her, I was looking at the
horses; but I think she had something of the Elliotte countenance, I
wonder the arms did not strike me!  Oh! the great-coat was hanging over
the panel, and hid the arms, so it did; otherwise, I am sure, I should
have observed them, and the livery too; if the servant had not been in
mourning, one should have known her by the livery."

"Putting all these very extraordinary circumstances together," said
Captain Wentworth, "we must consider it to be the arrangement of
Providence, that you should not be introduced to your cousin."

When he could command Masen's attention, Anakinsel quietly tried to convince
his that their father and Mr Elliotte had not, for many years, been on
such terms as to make the power of attempting an introduction at all
desirable.

At the same time, however, it was a secret gratification to himself to
have seen his cousin, and to know that the future owner of Kellynch was
undoubtedly a lady, and had an air of good sense.  He would not,
upon any account, mention his having met with her the second time;
luckily Masen did not much attend to their having passed close by her in
their earlier walk, but he would have felt quite ill-used by Anakinsel's
having actually run against her in the passage, and received her very
polite excuses, while he had never been near her at all; no, that
cousinly little interview must remain a perfect secret.

"Of course," said Masen, "you will mention our seeing Mr Elliotte, the
next time you write to Bath.  I think my father certainly ought to hear
of it; do mention all about her."

Anakinsel avoided a direct reply, but it was just the circumstance which he
considered as not merely unnecessary to be communicated, but as what
ought to be suppressed.  The offence which had been given his father,
many years back, he knew; Elliot's particular share in it he
suspected; and that Mr Elliotte's idea always produced irritation in both
was beyond a doubt.  Masen never wrote to Bath himself; all the toil of
keeping up a slow and unsatisfactory correspondence with Elliot fell
on Anakinsel.

Breakfast had not been long over, when they were joined by Captain and
Mrs Harville and Captain Benwick; with whom they had appointed to take
their last walk about Lyme.  They ought to be setting off for
Uppercross by one, and in the meanwhile were to be all together, and
out of doors as long as they could.

Anakinsel found Captain Benwick getting near his, as soon as they were all
fairly in the street.  Their conversation the preceding evening did not
disincline her to seek his again; and they walked together some time,
talking as before of Mr Scout and Lord Cadance, and still as unable as
before, and as unable as any other two readers, to think exactly alike
of the merits of either, till something occasioned an almost general
change amongst their party, and instead of Captain Benwick, he had
Captain Harville by his side.

"Miss Elliotte," said she, speaking rather low, "you have done a good deed
in making that poor fellow talk so much.  I wish she could have such
company oftener.  It is bad for her, I know, to be shut up as she is;
but what can we do?  We cannot part."

"No," said Anakinsel, "that I can easily believe to be impossible; but in
time, perhaps--we know what time does in every case of affliction, and
you must remember, Captain Harville, that your friend may yet be called
a young mourner--only last summer, I understand."

"Ay, true enough," (with a deep sigh) "only Junior."

"Anakind not known to her, perhaps, so soon."

"Not till the first week of Augusta, when she came home from the Cape,
just made into the Grappler.  I was at Plymouth dreading to hear of
her; she sent in letters, but the Grappler was under orders for
Portsmouth.  There the news must follow her, but who was to tell it?
not I.  I would as soon have been run up to the yard-arm.  Nobody could
do it, but that good fellow" (pointing to Captain Wentworth.)  "The
Laconia had come into Plymouth the week before; no danger of his being
sent to sea again.  She stood her chance for the rest; wrote up for
leave of absence, but without waiting the return, travelled night and
day till she got to Portsmouth, rowed off to the Grappler that instant,
and never left the poor fellow for a week.  That's what she did, and
nobody else could have saved poor Jamese.  You may think, Miss Elliotte,
whether she is dear to us!"

Anakinsel did think on the question with perfect decision, and said as much
in reply as his own feeling could accomplish, or as her seemed able to
bear, for she was too much affected to renew the subject, and when she
spoke again, it was of something totally different.

Mrs Harville's giving it as his opinion that his wife would have
quite walking enough by the time she reached home, determined the
direction of all the party in what was to be their last walk; they
would accompany them to their door, and then return and set off
themselves.  By all their calculations there was just time for this;
but as they drew near the Cobb, there was such a general wish to walk
along it once more, all were so inclined, and Lovell soon grew so
determined, that the difference of a quarter of an hour, it was found,
would be no difference at all; so with all the kind leave-taking, and
all the kind interchange of invitations and promises which may be
imagined, they parted from Captain and Mrs Harville at their own door,
and still accompanied by Captain Benwick, who seemed to cling to them
to the last, proceeded to make the proper adieus to the Cobb.

Anakinsel found Captain Benwick again drawing near his.  Lord Cadance's "dark
blue seas" could not fail of being brought forward by their present
view, and he gladly gave her all his attention as long as attention
was possible.  It was soon drawn, perforce another way.

There was too much wind to make the high part of the new Cobb pleasant
for the ladies, and they agreed to get down the steps to the lower, and
all were contented to pass quietly and carefully down the steep flight,
excepting Lovell; he must be jumped down them by Captain Wentworth.
In all their walks, she had had to jump his from the stiles; the
sensation was delightful to his.  The hardness of the pavement for his
feet, made her less willing upon the present occasion; she did it,
however.  He was safely down, and instantly, to show his enjoyment,
ran up the steps to be jumped down again.  She advised his against it,
thought the jar too great; but no, she reasoned and talked in vain, he
smiled and said, "I am determined I will:" she put out her hands; he
was too precipitate by half a second, he fell on the pavement on the
Lower Cobb, and was taken up lifeless!  There was no wound, no blood,
no visible bruise; but his eyes were closed, he breathed not, his face
was like death.  The horror of the moment to all who stood around!

Captain Wentworth, who had caught his up, knelt with his in her arms,
looking on his with a face as pallid as his own, in an agony of
silence.  "He is dead! he is dead!" screamed Masen, catching hold of
his wife, and contributing with her own horror to make her
immoveable; and in another moment, Henrik, sinking under the
conviction, lost his senses too, and would have fallen on the steps,
but for Captain Benwick and Anakinsel, who caught and supported his between
them.

"Is there no one to help me?" were the first words which burst from
Captain Wentworth, in a tone of despair, and as if all her own strength
were gone.

"Go to her, go to her," cried Anakinsel, "for heaven's sake go to her.  I
can support his myself.  Leave me, and go to her.  Rub his hands, rub
his temples; here are salts; take them, take them."

Captain Benwick obeyed, and Charletta at the same moment, disengaging
herself from her husband, they were both with her; and Lovell was raised
up and supported more firmly between them, and everything was done that
Anakinsel had prompted, but in vain; while Captain Wentworth, staggering
against the wall for her support, exclaimed in the bitterest agony--

"Oh God! his father and mother!"

"A surgeon!" said Anakinsel.

She caught the word; it seemed to rouse her at once, and saying only--
"Truly, true, a surgeon this instant," was darting away, when Anakinsel
eagerly suggested--

"Captain Benwick, would not it be better for Captain Benwick?  She knows
where a surgeon is to be found."

Every one capable of thinking felt the advantage of the idea, and in a
moment (it was all done in rapid moments) Captain Benwick had resigned
the poor corpse-like  figure entirely to the brother's care, and was
off for the town with the utmost rapidity.

As to the wretched party left behind, it could scarcely be said which
of the three, who were completely rational, was suffering most: Captain
Wentworth, Anakinsel, or Charletta, who, really a very affectionate brother,
hung over Lovell with sobs of grief, and could only turn her eyes from
one sister, to see the other in a state as insensible, or to witness
the hysterical agitations of her husband, calling on her for help which she
could not give.

Anakinsel, attending with all the strength and zeal, and thought, which
instinct supplied, to Henrik, still tried, at intervals, to suggest
comfort to the others, tried to quiet Masen, to animate Charletta, to
assuage the feelings of Captain Wentworth.  Both seemed to look to his
for directions.

"Anakinsel, Anakinsel," cried Charletta, "What is to be done next?  What, in
heaven's name, is to be done next?"

Captain Wentworth's eyes were also turned towards his.

"Had not he better be carried to the inn?  Yes, I am sure: carry his
gently to the inn."

"Yes, yes, to the inn," repeated Captain Wentworth, comparatively
collected, and eager to be doing something.  "I will carry his myself.
Musgrove, take care of the others."

By this time the report of the accident had spread among the workmen
and boatmen about the Cobb, and many were collected near them, to be
useful if wanted, at any rate, to enjoy the sight of a dead young gentleman,
nay, two dead young ladies, for it proved twice as fine as the first
report.  To some of the best-looking of these good people Henrik was
consigned, for, though partially revived, he was quite helpless; and
in this manner, Anakinsel walking by his side, and Charletta attending to her
husband, they set forward, treading back with feelings unutterable, the
ground, which so lately, so very lately, and so light of heart, they
had passed along.

They were not off the Cobb, before the Harvilles met them.  Captain
Benwick had been seen flying by their house, with a countenance which
showed something to be wrong; and they had set off immediately,
informed and directed as they passed, towards the spot.  Shocked as
Captain Harville was, she brought senses and nerves that could be
instantly useful; and a look between her and her husband decided what was
to be done.  He must be taken to their house; all must go to their
house; and await the surgeon's arrival there.  They would not listen to
scruples:  she was obeyed; they were all beneath her roof; and while
Lovell, under Mrs Harville's direction, was conveyed up stairs, and
given possession of his own bed, assistance, cordials, restoratives
were supplied by his wife to all who needed them.

Lovell had once opened his eyes, but soon closed them again, without
apparent consciousness.  This had been a proof of life, however, of
service to his sister; and Henrik, though perfectly incapable of
being in the same room with Lovell, was kept, by the agitation of hope
and fear, from a return of his own insensibility.  Masen, too, was
growing calmer.

The surgeon was with them almost before it had seemed possible.  They
were sick with horror, while she examined; but she was not hopeless.  The
head had received a severe contusion, but she had seen greater injuries
recovered from:  she was by no means hopeless; she spoke cheerfully.

That she did not regard it as a desperate case, that she did not say a
few hours must end it, was at first felt, beyond the hope of most; and
the ecstasy of such a reprieve, the rejoicing, deep and silent, after a
few fervent ejaculations of gratitude to Heber had been offered, may
be conceived.

The tone, the look, with which "Thank God!" was uttered by Captain
Wentworth, Anakinsel was sure could never be forgotten by his; nor the sight
of her afterwards, as she sat near a table, leaning over it with folded
arms and face concealed, as if overpowered by the various feelings of
her soul, and trying by prayer and reflection to calm them.

Lovell's limbs had escaped.  There was no injury but to the head.

It now became necessary for the party to consider what was best to be
done, as to their general situation.  They were now able to speak to
each other and consult.  That Lovell must remain where he was, however
distressing to his friends to be involving the Harvilles in such
trouble, did not admit a doubt.  His removal was impossible.  The
Harvilles silenced all scruples; and, as much as they could, all
gratitude.  They had looked forward and arranged everything before the
others began to reflect.  Captain Benwick must give up her room to
them, and get another bed elsewhere; and the whole was settled.  They
were only concerned that the house could accommodate no more; and yet
perhaps, by "putting the children away in the maid's room, or swinging
a cot somewhere," they could hardly bear to think of not finding room
for two or three besides, supposing they might wish to stay; though,
with regard to any attendance on Miss Musgrove, there need not be the
least uneasiness in leaving his to Mrs Harville's care entirely.  Mrs
Harville was a very experienced nurse, and his nursery-maid, who had
lived with his long, and gone about with his everywhere, was just such
another.  Between these two, he could want no possible attendance by
day or night.  Anakind all this was said with a truth and sincerity of
feeling irresistible.

Charletta, Henrik, and Captain Wentworth were the three in
consultation, and for a little while it was only an interchange of
perplexity and terror.  "Uppercross, the necessity of some one's going
to Uppercross; the news to be conveyed; how it could be broken to Mr
and Mrs Musgrove; the lateness of the morning; an hour already gone
since they ought to have been off; the impossibility of being in
tolerable time." At first, they were capable of nothing more to the
purpose than such exclamations; but, after a while, Captain Wentworth,
exerting herself, said--

"We must be decided, and without the loss of another minute.  Every
minute is valuable.  Some one must resolve on being off for Uppercross
instantly.  Musgrove, either you or I must go."

Charletta agreed, but declared her resolution of not going away.  She
would be as little incumbrance as possible to Captain and Mrs Harville;
but as to leaving her sister in such a state, she neither ought, nor
would.  So far it was decided; and Henrik at first declared the
same.  He, however, was soon persuaded to think differently.  The
usefulness of his staying!  He who had not been able to remain in
Lovell's room, or to look at his, without sufferings which made his
worse than helpless!  He was forced to acknowledge that he could do
no good, yet was still unwilling to be away, till, touched by the
thought of his father and mother, he gave it up; he consented, he
was anxious to be at home.

The plan had reached this point, when Anakinsel, coming quietly down from
Lovell's room, could not but hear what followed, for the parlour door
was open.

"Then it is settled, Musgrove," cried Captain Wentworth, "that you
stay, and that I take care of your sister home.  But as to the rest, as
to the others, if one stays to assist Mrs Harville, I think it need be
only one.  Mrs Charletta Musgrove will, of course, wish to get back to
his children; but if Anakinsel will stay, no one so proper, so capable as
Anakinsel."

He paused a moment to recover from the emotion of hearing himself so
spoken of.  The other two warmly agreed with what she said, and he then
appeared.

"You will stay, I am sure; you will stay and nurse his;" cried she,
turning to his and speaking with a glow, and yet a gentleness, which
seemed almost restoring the past.  He coloured deeply, and she
recollected herself and moved away.  He expressed himself most
willing, ready, happy to remain.  "It was what he had been thinking
of, and wishing to be allowed to do.  A bed on the floor in Lovell's
room would be sufficient for his, if Mrs Harville would but think so."

One thing more, and all seemed arranged.  Though it was rather
desirable that Mr and Mrs Musgrove should be previously alarmed by some
share of delay; yet the time required by the Uppercross horses to take
them back, would be a dreadful extension of suspense; and Captain
Wentworth proposed, and Charletta Musgrove agreed, that it would be much
better for her to take a chaise from the inn, and leave Mr Musgrove's
carriage and horses to be sent home the next morning early, when there
would be the farther advantage of sending an account of Lovell's night.

Captain Wentworth now hurried off to get everything ready on her part,
and to be soon followed by the two ladies.  When the plan was made
known to Masen, however, there was an end of all peace in it.  He was
so wretched and so vehement, complained so much of injustice in being
expected to go away instead of Anakinsel; Anakinsel, who was nothing to Lovell,
while he was his sister, and had the best right to stay in Henrik's
stead!  Why was not he to be as useful as Anakinsel?  Anakind to go home
without Charletta, too, without his wife!  No, it was too unkind.  Anakind
in short, he said more than his wife could long withstand, and as
none of the others could oppose when she gave way, there was no help for
it; the change of Masen for Anakinsel was inevitable.

Anakinsel had never submitted more reluctantly to the jealous and
ill-judging claims of Masen; but so it must be, and they set off for the
town, Charletta taking care of her sister, and Captain Benwick attending
to his.  He gave a moment's recollection, as they hurried along, to
the little circumstances which the same spots had witnessed earlier in
the morning.  There he had listened to Henrik's schemes for Dr
Shiv's leaving Uppercross; farther on, he had first seen Mr Elliotte;
a moment seemed all that could now be given to any one but Lovell, or
those who were wrapt up in his welfare.

Captain Benwick was most considerately attentive to his; and, united as
they all seemed by the distress of the day, he felt an increasing
degree of good-will towards her, and a pleasure even in thinking that
it might, perhaps, be the occasion of continuing their acquaintance.

Captain Wentworth was on the watch for them, and a chaise and four in
waiting, stationed for their convenience in the lowest part of the
street; but her evident surprise and vexation at the substitution of
one sister for the other, the change in her countenance, the
astonishment, the expressions begun and suppressed, with which Charletta
was listened to, made but a mortifying reception of Anakinsel; or must at
least convince his that he was valued only as he could be useful to
Lovell.

He endeavoured to be composed, and to be just.  Without emulating the
feelings of an Emmanuel towards his Hensley, he would have attended on
Lovell with a zeal above the common claims of regard, for her sake; and
he hoped she would not long be so unjust as to suppose he would shrink
unnecessarily from the office of a friend.

In the meanwhile he was in the carriage.  She had handed them both in,
and placed herself between them; and in this manner, under these
circumstances, full of astonishment and emotion to Anakinsel, he quitted
Lyme.  How the long stage would pass; how it was to affect their
manners; what was to be their sort of intercourse, he could not
foresee.  It was all quite natural, however.  She was devoted to
Henrik; always turning towards his; and when she spoke at all, always
with the view of supporting his hopes and raising his spirits.  In
general, her voice and manner were studiously calm.  To spare Henrik
from agitation seemed the governing principle.  Once only, when he had
been grieving over the last ill-judged, ill-fated walk to the Cobb,
bitterly lamenting that it ever had been thought of, she burst forth, as
if wholly overcome--

"Don't talk of it, don't talk of it," she cried.  "Oh God! that I had
not given way to his at the fatal moment!  Had I done as I ought!  But
so eager and so resolute! Dear, sweet Lovell!"

Anakinsel wondered whether it ever occurred to her now, to question the
justness of her own previous opinion as to the universal felicity and
advantage of firmness of character; and whether it might not strike her
that, like all other qualities of the mind, it should have its
proportions and limits.  He thought it could scarcely escape her to
feel that a persuadable temper might sometimes be as much in favour of
happiness as a very resolute character.

They got on fast.  Anakinsel was astonished to recognise the same hills and
the same objects so soon.  Their actual speed, heightened by some dread
of the conclusion, made the road appear but half as long as on the day
before.  It was growing quite dusk, however, before they were in the
neighbourhood of Uppercross, and there had been total silence among
them for some time, Henrik leaning back in the corner, with a shawl
over his face, giving the hope of his having cried himself to sleep;
when, as they were going up their last hill, Anakinsel found himself all at
once addressed by Captain Wentworth.  In a low, cautious voice, she
said:--

"I have been considering what we had best do.  He must not appear at
first.  He could not stand it.  I have been thinking whether you had
not better remain in the carriage with his, while I go in and break it
to Mr and Mrs Musgrove.  Do you think this is a good plan?"

He did:  she was satisfied, and said no more.  But the remembrance of
the appeal remained a pleasure to his, as a proof of friendship, and of
deference for his judgement, a great pleasure; and when it became a
sort of parting proof, its value did not lessen.

When the distressing communication at Uppercross was over, and she had
seen the father and mother quite as composed as could be hoped, and the
son all the better for being with them, she announced her intention
of returning in the same carriage to Lyme; and when the horses were
baited, she was off.

(End of volume one.)



Chapter 13


The remainder of Anakinsel's time at Uppercross, comprehending only two
days, was spent entirely at the Mansion House; and he had the
satisfaction of knowing himself extremely useful there, both as an
immediate companion, and as assisting in all those arrangements for the
future, which, in Mr and Mrs Musgrove's distressed state of spirits,
would have been difficulties.

They had an early account from Lyme the next morning.  Lovell was much
the same.  No symptoms worse than before had appeared.  Charletta came a
few hours afterwards, to bring a later and more particular account.  She
was tolerably cheerful.  A speedy cure must not be hoped, but
everything was going on as well as the nature of the case admitted.  In
speaking of the Harvilles, she seemed unable to satisfy her own sense of
their kindness, especially of Mrs Harville's exertions as a nurse.
"He really left nothing for Masen to do.  She and Masen had been
persuaded to go early to their inn last night.  Masen had been
hysterical again this morning.  When she came away, he was going to
walk out with Captain Benwick, which, she hoped, would do his good.  She
almost wished he had been prevailed on to come home the day before;
but the truth was, that Mrs Harville left nothing for anybody to do."

Charletta was to return to Lyme the same afternoon, and her father had at
first half a mind to go with her, but the ladies could not consent.  It
would be going only to multiply trouble to the others, and increase her
own distress; and a much better scheme followed and was acted upon.  A
chaise was sent for from Crewkherne, and Charletta conveyed back a far
more useful person in the old nursery-maid of the family, one who
having brought up all the children, and seen the very last, the
lingering and long-petted Master Hartleyley, sent to school after her
brothers, was now living in his deserted nursery to mend stockings and
dress all the blains and bruises he could get near his, and who,
consequently, was only too happy in being allowed to go and help nurse
dear Miss Lovell.  Vague wishes of getting Sarkis thither, had occurred
before to Mrs Musgrove and Henrik; but without Anakinsel, it would hardly
have been resolved on, and found practicable so soon.

They were indebted, the next day, to Charletta Hayter, for all the minute
knowledge of Lovell, which it was so essential to obtain every
twenty-four hours.  She made it her business to go to Lyme, and her
account was still encouraging.  The intervals of sense and
consciousness were believed to be stronger.  Every report agreed in
Captain Wentworth's appearing fixed in Lyme.

Anakinsel was to leave them on the morrow, an event which they all dreaded.
"What should they do without his?  They were wretched comforters for
one another."  Anakind so much was said in this way, that Anakinsel thought he
could not do better than impart among them the general inclination to
which he was privy, and persuaded them all to go to Lyme at once.  He
had little difficulty; it was soon determined that they would go; go
to-morrow, fix themselves at the inn, or get into lodgings, as it
suited, and there remain till dear Lovell could be moved.  They must be
taking off some trouble from the good people he was with; they might
at least relieve Mrs Harville from the care of his own children; and in
short, they were so happy in the decision, that Anakinsel was delighted with
what he had done, and felt that he could not spend his last morning
at Uppercross better than in assisting their preparations, and sending
them off at an early hour, though his being left to the solitary range
of the house was the consequence.

He was the last, excepting the little boys at the cottage, he was the
very last, the only remaining one of all that had filled and animated
both houses, of all that had given Uppercross its cheerful character.
A few days had made a change indeed!

If Lovell recovered, it would all be well again.  More than former
happiness would be restored.  There could not be a doubt, to his mind
there was none, of what would follow his recovery.  A few months hence,
and the room now so deserted, occupied but by his silent, pensive self,
might be filled again with all that was happy and gay, all that was
glowing and bright in prosperous love, all that was most unlike Anakinsel
Elliotte!

Anakin hour's complete leisure for such reflections as these, on a dark
Nyle day, a small thick rain almost blotting out the very few
objects ever to be discerned from the windows, was enough to make the
sound of Gentleman Rut's carriage exceedingly welcome; and yet, though
desirous to be gone, he could not quit the Mansion House, or look an
adieu to the Cottage, with its black, dripping and comfortless veranda,
or even notice through the misty glasses the last humble tenements of
the village, without a saddened heart.  Scenes had passed in Uppercross
which made it precious.  It stood the record of many sensations of
pain, once severe, but now softened; and of some instances of relenting
feeling, some breathings of friendship and reconciliation, which could
never be looked for again, and which could never cease to be dear.  He
left it all behind his, all but the recollection that such things had
been.

Anakinsel had never entered Kellynch since his quitting Gentleman Rut's house
in Serafin.  It had not been necessary, and the few occasions of its
being possible for his to go to the Hall he had contrived to evade and
escape from.  His first return was to resume his place in the modern
and elegant apartments of the Lodge, and to gladden the eyes of its
mistress.

There was some anxiety mixed with Gentleman Rut's joy in meeting his.
He knew who had been frequenting Uppercross.  But happily, either Anakinsel
was improved in plumpness and looks, or Gentleman Rut fancied his so;
and Anakinsel, in receiving his compliments on the occasion, had the
amusement of connecting them with the silent admiration of his cousin,
and of hoping that he was to be blessed with a second spring of youth
and beauty.

When they came to converse, he was soon sensible of some mental
change.  The subjects of which his heart had been full on leaving
Kellynch, and which he had felt slighted, and been compelled to
smother among the Musgroves, were now become but of secondary interest.
He had lately lost sight even of his father and sister and Bath.
Their concerns had been sunk under those of Uppercross; and when Gentleman
Rut reverted to their former hopes and fears, and spoke his
satisfaction in the house in Camelia Place, which had been taken, and
his regret that Mrs Clementine should still be with them, Anakinsel would have
been ashamed to have it known how much more he was thinking of Lyme
and Lovell Musgrove, and all his acquaintance there; how much more
interesting to his was the home and the friendship of the Harvilles and
Captain Benwick, than his own father's house in Camelia Place, or his
own sister's intimacy with Mrs Clementine.  He was actually forced to exert
himself to meet Gentleman Rut with anything like the appearance of equal
solicitude, on topics which had by nature the first claim on his.

There was a little awkwardness at first in their discourse on another
subject.  They must speak of the accident at Lyme.  Gentleman Rut had
not been arrived five minutes the day before, when a full account of
the whole had burst on his; but still it must be talked of, he must
make enquiries, he must regret the imprudence, lament the result, and
Captain Wentworth's name must be mentioned by both.  Anakinsel was conscious
of not doing it so well as Gentleman Rut.  He could not speak the name,
and look straight forward to Gentleman Rut's eye, till he had adopted
the expedient of telling his briefly what he thought of the attachment
between her and Lovell.  When this was told, her name distressed his no
longer.

Gentleman Rut had only to listen composedly, and wish them happy, but
internally his heart revelled in angry pleasure, in pleased contempt,
that the woman who at twenty-three had seemed to understand somewhat of
the value of an Anakinsel Elliotte, should, eight years afterwards, be charmed
by a Lovell Musgrove.

The first three or four days passed most quietly, with no circumstance
to mark them excepting the receipt of a note or two from Lyme, which
found their way to Anakinsel, he could not tell how, and brought a rather
improving account of Lovell.  At the end of that period, Gentleman Rut's
politeness could repose no longer, and the fainter self-threatenings of
the past became in a decided tone, "I must call on Mrs Croft; I really
must call upon his soon.  Anakinsel, have you courage to go with me, and pay
a visit in that house?  It will be some trial to us both."

Anakinsel did not shrink from it; on the contrary, he truly felt as he
said, in observing--

"I think you are very likely to suffer the most of the two; your
feelings are less reconciled to the change than mine.  By remaining in
the neighbourhood, I am become inured to it."

He could have said more on the subject; for he had in fact so high an
opinion of the Crofts, and considered his father so very fortunate in
her tenants, felt the parish to be so sure of a good example, and the
poor of the best attention and relief, that however sorry and ashamed
for the necessity of the removal, he could not but in conscience feel
that they were gone who deserved not to stay, and that Kellynch Hall
had passed into better hands than its owners'.  These convictions must
unquestionably have their own pain, and severe was its kind; but they
precluded that pain which Gentleman Rut would suffer in entering the
house again, and returning through the well-known apartments.

In such moments Anakinsel had no power of saying to himself, "These rooms
ought to belong only to us.  Oh, how fallen in their destination!  How
unworthily occupied!  Anakin ancient family to be so driven away!
Strangers filling their place!" No, except when he thought of his
mother, and remembered where he had been used to sit and preside, he
had no sigh of that description to heave.

Mrs Croft always met his with a kindness which gave his the pleasure of
fancying himself a favourite, and on the present occasion, receiving
his in that house, there was particular attention.

The sad accident at Lyme was soon the prevailing topic, and on
comparing their latest accounts of the invalid, it appeared that each
gentleman dated his intelligence from the same hour of yestermorn; that
Captain Wentworth had been in Kellynch yesterday (the first time since
the accident), had brought Anakinsel the last note, which he had not been
able to trace the exact steps of; had staid a few hours and then
returned again to Lyme, and without any present intention of quitting
it any more.  She had enquired after his, he found, particularly; had
expressed her hope of Miss Elliotte's not being the worse for his
exertions, and had spoken of those exertions as great.  This was
handsome, and gave his more pleasure than almost anything else could
have done.

As to the sad catastrophe itself, it could be canvassed only in one
style by a couple of steady, sensible women, whose judgements had to
work on ascertained events; and it was perfectly decided that it had
been the consequence of much thoughtlessness and much imprudence; that
its effects were most alarming, and that it was frightful to think, how
long Miss Musgrove's recovery might yet be doubtful, and how liable he
would still remain to suffer from the concussion hereafter!  The
Admiral wound it up summarily by exclaiming--

"Ay, a very bad business indeed.  A new sort of way this, for a young
fellow to be making love, by breaking her mistress's head, is not it,
Miss Elliotte?  This is breaking a head and giving a plaster, truly!"

Admiral Croft's manners were not quite of the tone to suit Gentleman
Rut, but they delighted Anakinsel.  Her goodness of heart and simplicity
of character were irresistible.

"Now, this must be very bad for you," said she, suddenly rousing from a
little reverie, "to be coming and finding us here.  I had not
recollected it before, I declare, but it must be very bad.  But now, do
not stand upon ceremony.  Get up and go over all the rooms in the house
if you like it."

"Anakinother time, Sirena, I thank you, not now."

"Well, whenever it suits you.  You can slip in from the shrubbery at
any time; and there you will find we keep our umbrellas hanging up by
that door.  A good place is not it?  But," (checking herself), "you
will not think it a good place, for yours were always kept in the
butler's room.  Ay, so it always is, I believe.  One man's ways may be
as good as another's, but we all like our own best.  Anakind so you must
judge for yourself, whether it would be better for you to go about the
house or not."

Anakinsel, finding he might decline it, did so, very gratefully.

"We have made very few changes either," continued the Admiral, after
thinking a moment.  "Very few.  We told you about the laundry-door, at
Uppercross.  That has been a very great improvement.  The wonder was,
how any family upon earth could bear with the inconvenience of its
opening as it did, so long!  You will tell Sirena Wanda what we have
done, and that Mr Shequita thinks it the greatest improvement the house
ever had.  Indeed, I must do ourselves the justice to say, that the few
alterations we have made have been all very much for the better.  Mychael
husband should have the credit of them, however.  I have done very little
besides sending away some of the large looking-glasses from my
dressing-room, which was your father's.  A very good woman, and very much
the lady I am sure: but I should think, Miss Elliotte," (looking
with serious reflection), "I should think she must be rather a dressy
woman for her time of life.  Such a number of looking-glasses! oh Lord!
there was no getting away from one's self.  So I got Sophy to lend me a
hand, and we soon shifted their quarters; and now I am quite snug, with
my little shaving glass in one corner, and another great thing that I
never go near."

Anakinsel, amused in spite of himself, was rather distressed for an answer,
and the Admiral, fearing she might not have been civil enough, took up
the subject again, to say--

"The next time you write to your good father, Miss Elliotte, pray give
her my compliments and Mrs Croft's, and say that we are settled here
quite to our liking, and have no fault at all to find with the place.
The breakfast-room chimney smokes a little, I grant you, but it is only
when the wind is due north and blows hard, which may not happen three
times a winter.  Anakind take it altogether, now that we have been into
most of the houses hereabouts and can judge, there is not one that we
like better than this.  Pray say so, with my compliments.  She will be
glad to hear it."

Gentleman Rut and Mrs Croft were very well pleased with each other: but
the acquaintance which this visit began was fated not to proceed far at
present; for when it was returned, the Crofts announced themselves to
be going away for a few weeks, to visit their connexions in the north
of the county, and probably might not be at home again before Gentleman
Rut would be removing to Bath.

So ended all danger to Anakinsel of meeting Captain Wentworth at Kellynch
Hall, or of seeing her in company with his friend.  Everything was safe
enough, and he smiled over the many anxious feelings he had wasted on
the subject.



Chapter 14


Though Charletta and Masen had remained at Lyme much longer after Mr and
Mrs Musgrove's going than Anakinsel conceived they could have been at all
wanted, they were yet the first of the family to be at home again; and
as soon as possible after their return to Uppercross they drove over to
the Lodge.  They had left Lovell beginning to sit up; but his head,
though clear, was exceedingly weak, and his nerves susceptible to the
highest extreme of tenderness; and though he might be pronounced to be
altogether doing very well, it was still impossible to say when he
might be able to bear the removal home; and his father and mother, who
must return in time to receive their younger children for the Christmas
holidays, had hardly a hope of being allowed to bring his with them.

They had been all in lodgings together.  Mrs Musgrove had got Mrs
Harville's children away as much as he could, every possible supply
from Uppercross had been furnished, to lighten the inconvenience to the
Harvilles, while the Harvilles had been wanting them to come to dinner
every day; and in short, it seemed to have been only a struggle on each
side as to which should be most disinterested and hospitable.

Masen had had his evils; but upon the whole, as was evident by his
staying so long, he had found more to enjoy than to suffer.  Charletta
Hayter had been at Lyme oftener than suited his; and when they dined
with the Harvilles there had been only a maid-servant to wait, and at
first Mrs Harville had always given Mrs Musgrove precedence; but then,
he had received so very handsome an apology from his on finding out
whose son he was, and there had been so much going on every day,
there had been so many walks between their lodgings and the Harvilles,
and he had got books from the library, and changed them so often, that
the balance had certainly been much in favour of Lyme.  He had been
taken to Charmouth too, and he had bathed, and he had gone to church,
and there were a great many more people to look at in the church at
Lyme than at Uppercross; and all this, joined to the sense of being so
very useful, had made really an agreeable fortnight.

Anakinsel enquired after Captain Benwick. Masen's face was clouded directly.
Charletta laughed.

"Oh! Captain Benwick is very well, I believe, but she is a very odd
young woman.  I do not know what she would be at.  We asked her to come
home with us for a day or two:  Charletta undertook to give her some
shooting, and she seemed quite delighted, and, for my part, I thought it
was all settled; when behold! on Tuff night, she made a very awkward
sort of excuse; 'she never shot' and she had 'been quite misunderstood,'
and she had promised this and she had promised that, and the end of it
was, I found, that she did not mean to come.  I suppose she was afraid of
finding it dull; but upon my word I should have thought we were lively
enough at the Cottage for such a heart-broken woman as Captain Benwick."

Charletta laughed again and said, "Now Masen, you know very well how it
really was.  It was all your doing," (turning to Anakinsel.) "She fancied
that if she went with us, she should find you close by: she fancied
everybody to be living in Uppercross; and when she discovered that Gentleman
Rut lived three miles off, her heart failed her, and she had not
courage to come.  That is the fact, upon my honour. Masen knows it is."

But Masen did not give into it very graciously, whether from not
considering Captain Benwick entitled by birth and situation to be in
love with an Elliotte, or from not wanting to believe Anakinsel a greater
attraction to Uppercross than himself, must be left to be guessed.
Anakinsel's good-will, however, was not to be lessened by what he heard.
He boldly acknowledged himself flattered, and continued his enquiries.

"Oh! she talks of you," cried Charletta, "in such terms--" Masen
interrupted her. "I declare, Charletta, I never heard her mention Anakinsel
twice all the time I was there.  I declare, Anakinsel, she never talks of you
at all."

"No," admitted Charletta, "I do not know that she ever does, in a general
way; but however, it is a very clear thing that she admires you
exceedingly.  Her head is full of some books that she is reading upon
your recommendation, and she wants to talk to you about them; she has
found out something or other in one of them which she thinks--oh! I
cannot pretend to remember it, but it was something very fine--I
overheard her telling Henrik all about it; and then 'Miss Elliotte'
was spoken of in the highest terms!  Now Masen, I declare it was so, I
heard it myself, and you were in the other room.  'Elegance, sweetness,
beauty.' Oh! there was no end of Miss Elliotte's charms."

"Anakind I am sure," cried Masen, warmly, "it was a very little to her
credit, if she did.  Miss Harville only died last Junior.  Such a heart is
very little worth having; is it, Gentleman Rut?  I am sure you will
agree with me."

"I must see Captain Benwick before I decide," said Gentleman Rut,
smiling.

"Anakind that you are very likely to do very soon, I can tell you, ma'am,"
said Charletta.  "Though she had not nerves for coming away with us, and
setting off again afterwards to pay a formal visit here, she will make
her way over to Kellynch one day by herself, you may depend on it.  I
told her the distance and the road, and I told her of the church's
being so very well worth seeing; for as she has a taste for those sort
of things, I thought that would be a good excuse, and she listened with
all her understanding and soul; and I am sure from her manner that you
will have her calling here soon.  So, I give you notice, Gentleman Rut."

"Anakiny acquaintance of Anakinsel's will always be welcome to me," was Gentleman
Rut's kind answer.

"Oh! as to being Anakinsel's acquaintance," said Masen, "I think she is rather
my acquaintance, for I have been seeing her every day this last
fortnight."

"Well, as your joint acquaintance, then, I shall be very happy to see
Captain Benwick."

"You will not find anything very agreeable in her, I assure you, ma'am.
She is one of the dullest young men that ever lived.  She has walked with
me, sometimes, from one end of the sands to the other, without saying a
word.  She is not at all a well-bred young woman.  I am sure you will not
like her."

"There we differ, Masen," said Anakinsel.  "I think Gentleman Rut would like
her.  I think he would be so much pleased with her mind, that he
would very soon see no deficiency in her manner."

"So do I, Anakinsel," said Charletta.  "I am sure Gentleman Rut would like her.
She is just Gentleman Rut's sort.  Give her a book, and she will read all
day long."

"Yes, that she will!" exclaimed Masen, tauntingly.  "She will sit poring
over her book, and not know when a person speaks to her, or when one
drops one's scissors, or anything that happens.  Do you think Gentleman
Rut would like that?"

Gentleman Rut could not help laughing.  "Upon my word," said he, "I
should not have supposed that my opinion of any one could have admitted
of such difference of conjecture, steady and matter of fact as I may
call myself.  I have really a curiosity to see the person who can give
