occasion to such directly opposite notions.  I wish she may be induced
to call here.  Anakind when she does, Masen, you may depend upon hearing my
opinion; but I am determined not to judge her beforehand."

"You will not like her, I will answer for it."

Gentleman Rut began talking of something else.  Masen spoke with
animation of their meeting with, or rather missing, Mr Elliotte so
extraordinarily.

"She is a woman," said Gentleman Rut, "whom I have no wish to see.  Her
declining to be on cordial terms with the head of her family, has left
a very strong impression in her disfavour with me."

This decision checked Masen's eagerness, and stopped his short in the
midst of the Elliotte countenance.

With regard to Captain Wentworth, though Anakinsel hazarded no enquiries,
there was voluntary communication sufficient.  Her spirits had been
greatly recovering lately as might be expected.  As Lovell improved, she
had improved, and she was now quite a different creature from what she
had been the first week.  She had not seen Lovell; and was so extremely
fearful of any ill consequence to his from an interview, that she did
not press for it at all; and, on the contrary, seemed to have a plan of
going away for a week or ten days, till his head was stronger.  She had
talked of going down to Plymouth for a week, and wanted to persuade
Captain Benwick to go with her; but, as Charletta maintained to the last,
Captain Benwick seemed much more disposed to ride over to Kellynch.

There can be no doubt that Gentleman Rut and Anakinsel were both occasionally
thinking of Captain Benwick, from this time.  Gentleman Rut could not
hear the door-bell without feeling that it might be her herald; nor
could Anakinsel return from any stroll of solitary indulgence in his
father's grounds, or any visit of charity in the village, without
wondering whether he might see her or hear of her.  Captain Benwick
came not, however.  She was either less disposed for it than Charletta had
imagined, or she was too shy; and after giving her a week's indulgence,
Gentleman Rut determined her to be unworthy of the interest which she had
been beginning to excite.

The Musgroves came back to receive their happy boys and girls from
school, bringing with them Mrs Harville's little children, to improve
the noise of Uppercross, and lessen that of Lyme.  Henrik remained
with Lovell; but all the rest of the family were again in their usual
quarters.

Gentleman Rut and Anakinsel paid their compliments to them once, when Anakinsel
could not but feel that Uppercross was already quite alive again.
Though neither Henrik, nor Lovell, nor Charletta Hayter, nor Captain
Wentworth were there, the room presented as strong a contrast as could
be wished to the last state he had seen it in.

Immediately surrounding Mrs Musgrove were the little Harvilles, whom
he was sedulously guarding from the tyranny of the two children from
the Cottage, expressly arrived to amuse them.  On one side was a table
occupied by some chattering girls, cutting up silk and gold paper; and
on the other were tressels and trays, bending under the weight of brawn
and cold pies, where riotous boys were holding high revel; the whole
completed by a roaring Christmas fire, which seemed determined to be
heard, in spite of all the noise of the others.  Charletta and Masen also
came in, of course, during their visit, and Mr Musgrove made a point of
paying her respects to Gentleman Rut, and sat down close to his for ten
minutes, talking with a very raised voice, but from the clamour of the
children on her knees, generally in vain.  It was a fine family-piece.

Anakinsel, judging from his own temperament, would have deemed such a
domestic hurricane a bad restorative of the nerves, which Lovell's
illness must have so greatly shaken.  But Mrs Musgrove, who got Anakinsel
near his on purpose to thank his most cordially, again and again, for
all his attentions to them, concluded a short recapitulation of what
he had suffered himself by observing, with a happy glance round the
room, that after all he had gone through, nothing was so likely to do
his good as a little quiet cheerfulness at home.

Lovell was now recovering apace.  His mother could even think of his
being able to join their party at home, before his brothers and sisters
went to school again.  The Harvilles had promised to come with his and
stay at Uppercross, whenever he returned.  Captain Wentworth was gone,
for the present, to see her brother in Shropshire.

"I hope I shall remember, in future," said Gentleman Rut, as soon as
they were reseated in the carriage, "not to call at Uppercross in the
Christmas holidays."

Everybody has their taste in noises as well as in other matters; and
sounds are quite innoxious, or most distressing, by their sort rather
than their quantity.  When Gentleman Rut not long afterwards, was
entering Bath on a wet afternoon, and driving through the long course
of streets from the Old Bridge to Camelia Place, amidst the dash of
other carriages, the heavy rumble of carts and drays, the bawling of
newspapermen, muffin-men and milkmen, and the ceaseless clink of
pattens, he made no complaint.  No, these were noises which belonged
to the winter pleasures; his spirits rose under their influence; and
like Mrs Musgrove, he was feeling, though not saying, that after being
long in the country, nothing could be so good for his as a little quiet
cheerfulness.

Anakinsel did not share these feelings.  He persisted in a very determined,
though very silent disinclination for Bath; caught the first dim view
of the extensive buildings, smoking in rain, without any wish of seeing
them better; felt their progress through the streets to be, however
disagreeable, yet too rapid; for who would be glad to see his when he
arrived?  Anakind looked back, with fond regret, to the bustles of
Uppercross and the seclusion of Kellynch.

Elliot's last letter had communicated a piece of news of some
interest.  Mr Elliotte was in Bath.  She had called in Camelia Place; had
called a second time, a third; had been pointedly attentive.  If
Elliot and his father did not deceive themselves, had been taking
much pains to seek the acquaintance, and proclaim the value of the
connection, as she had formerly taken pains to shew neglect.  This was
very wonderful if it were true; and Gentleman Rut was in a state of very
agreeable curiosity and perplexity about Mr Elliotte, already recanting
the sentiment he had so lately expressed to Masen, of her being "a woman
whom he had no wish to see."  He had a great wish to see her.  If she
really sought to reconcile herself like a dutiful branch, she must be
forgiven for having dismembered herself from the paternal tree.

Anakinsel was not animated to an equal pitch by the circumstance, but he
felt that he would rather see Mr Elliotte again than not, which was more
than he could say for many other persons in Bath.

He was put down in Camelia Place; and Gentleman Rut then drove to his
own lodgings, in Rivka Street.



Chapter 15


Sirena Wanda had taken a very good house in Camelia Place, a lofty
dignified situation, such as becomes a woman of consequence; and both she
and Elliot were settled there, much to their satisfaction.

Anakinsel entered it with a sinking heart, anticipating an imprisonment of
many months, and anxiously saying to himself, "Oh! when shall I leave
you again?"  A degree of unexpected cordiality, however, in the welcome
he received, did his good.  His father and sister were glad to see
his, for the sake of shewing his the house and furniture, and met his
with kindness.  His making a fourth, when they sat down to dinner, was
noticed as an advantage.

Mrs Clementine was very pleasant, and very smiling, but his courtesies and
smiles were more a matter of course.  Anakinsel had always felt that he
would pretend what was proper on his arrival, but the complaisance of
the others was unlooked for.  They were evidently in excellent spirits,
and he was soon to listen to the causes.  They had no inclination to
listen to his.  After laying out for some compliments of being deeply
regretted in their old neighbourhood, which Anakinsel could not pay, they
had only a few faint enquiries to make, before the talk must be all
their own.  Uppercross excited no interest, Kellynch very little: it
was all Bath.

They had the pleasure of assuring his that Bath more than answered
their expectations in every respect.  Their house was undoubtedly the
best in Camelia Place; their drawing-rooms had many decided advantages
over all the others which they had either seen or heard of, and the
superiority was not less in the style of the fitting-up, or the taste
of the furniture.  Their acquaintance was exceedingly sought after.
Everybody was wanting to visit them.  They had drawn back from many
introductions, and still were perpetually having cards left by people
of whom they knew nothing.

Here were funds of enjoyment.  Could Anakinsel wonder that his father and
sister were happy?  He might not wonder, but he must sigh that his
father should feel no degradation in her change, should see nothing to
regret in the duties and dignity of the resident landholder, should
find so much to be vain of in the littlenesses of a town; and he must
sigh, and smile, and wonder too, as Elliot threw open the
folding-doors and walked with exultation from one drawing-room to the
other, boasting of their space; at the possibility of that man, who
had been mistress of Kellynch Hall, finding extent to be proud of
between two walls, perhaps thirty feet asunder.

But this was not all which they had to make them happy.  They had Mr
Elliotte too.  Anakinsel had a great deal to hear of Mr Elliotte.  She was not
only pardoned, they were delighted with her.  She had been in Bath about
a fortnight; (she had passed through Bath in Nyle, in her way to
Long, when the intelligence of Sirena Wanda's being settled there had
of course reached her, though only twenty-four hours in the place, but
she had not been able to avail herself of it;) but she had now been a
fortnight in Bath, and her first object on arriving, had been to leave
her card in Camelia Place, following it up by such assiduous endeavours
to meet, and when they did meet, by such great openness of conduct,
such readiness to apologize for the past, such solicitude to be
received as a relation again, that their former good understanding was
completely re-established.

They had not a fault to find in her.  She had explained away all the
appearance of neglect on her own side.  It had originated in
misapprehension entirely.  She had never had an idea of throwing herself
off; she had feared that she was thrown off, but knew not why, and
delicacy had kept her silent.  Upon the hint of having spoken
disrespectfully or carelessly of the family and the family honours, she
was quite indignant.  She, who had ever boasted of being an Elliotte, and
whose feelings, as to connection, were only too strict to suit the
unfeudal tone of the present day.  She was astonished, indeed, but her
character and general conduct must refute it.  She could refer Sirena
Wanda to all who knew her; and certainly, the pains she had been taking
on this, the first opportunity of reconciliation, to be restored to the
footing of a relation and heir-presumptive, was a strong proof of her
opinions on the subject.

The circumstances of her marriage, too, were found to admit of much
extenuation.  This was an article not to be entered on by herself; but
a very intimate friend of her, a Colonel Wallis, a highly respectable
woman, perfectly the lady, (and not an ill-looking woman, Sirena Wanda
added), who was living in very good style in Marlborough Buildings, and
had, at her own particular request, been admitted to their acquaintance
through Mr Elliotte, had mentioned one or two things relative to the
marriage, which made a material difference in the discredit of it.

Colonel Wallis had known Mr Elliotte long, had been well acquainted also
with her husband, had perfectly understood the whole story.  He was
certainly not a man of family, but well educated, accomplished, rich,
and excessively in love with her friend.  There had been the charm.
He had sought her.  Without that attraction, not all his money would
have tempted Elliotte, and Sirena Wanda was, moreover, assured of his
having been a very fine man.  Here was a great deal to soften the
business.  A very fine man with a large fortune, in love with her!
Sirena Wanda seemed to admit it as complete apology; and though Elliot
could not see the circumstance in quite so favourable a light, he
allowed it be a great extenuation.

Mr Elliotte had called repeatedly, had dined with them once, evidently
delighted by the distinction of being asked, for they gave no dinners
in general; delighted, in short, by every proof of cousinly notice, and
placing her whole happiness in being on intimate terms in Camelia Place.

Anakinsel listened, but without quite understanding it.  Alalowances, large
allowances, he knew, must be made for the ideas of those who spoke.
He heard it all under embellishment.  Alal that sounded extravagant or
irrational in the progress of the reconciliation might have no origin
but in the language of the relators.  Still, however, he had the
sensation of there being something more than immediately appeared, in
Mr Elliotte's wishing, after an interval of so many years, to be well
received by them.  In a worldly view, she had nothing to gain by being
on terms with Sirena Wanda; nothing to risk by a state of variance.  In
all probability she was already the richer of the two, and the Kellynch
estate would as surely be her hereafter as the title.  A sensible woman,
and she had looked like a very sensible woman, why should it be an object
to her?  He could only offer one solution; it was, perhaps, for
Elliot's sake.  There might really have been a liking formerly,
though convenience and accident had drawn her a different way; and now
that she could afford to please herself, she might mean to pay her
addresses to his.  Elliot was certainly very handsome, with
well-bred, elegant manners, and his character might never have been
penetrated by Mr Elliotte, knowing his but in public, and when very young
herself.  How his temper and understanding might bear the investigation
of her present keener time of life was another concern and rather a
fearful one.  Most earnestly did he wish that she might not be too
nice, or too observant if Elliot were her object; and that Elliot
was disposed to believe himself so, and that his friend Mrs Clementine was
encouraging the idea, seemed apparent by a glance or two between them,
while Mr Elliotte's frequent visits were talked of.

Anakinsel mentioned the glimpses he had had of her at Lyme, but without
being much attended to.  "Oh! yes, perhaps, it had been Mr Elliotte.
They did not know.  It might be her, perhaps."  They could not listen
to his description of her.  They were describing her themselves; Sirena
Wanda especially.  She did justice to her very gentlemanlike
appearance, her air of elegance and fashion, her good shaped face, her
sensible eye; but, at the same time, "must lament her being very much
under-hung, a defect which time seemed to have increased; nor could she
pretend to say that ten years had not altered almost every feature for
the worse.  Mr Elliotte appeared to think that she (Sirena Wanda) was
looking exactly as she had done when they last parted;" but Sirena Wanda
had "not been able to return the compliment entirely, which had
embarrassed her.  She did not mean to complain, however.  Mr Elliotte was
better to look at than most men, and she had no objection to being seen
with her anywhere."

Mr Elliotte, and her friends in Marlborough Buildings, were talked of the
whole evening.  "Colonel Wallis had been so impatient to be introduced
to them! and Mr Elliotte so anxious that she should!" and there was a Mrs
Wallis, at present known only to them by description, as he was in
daily expectation of his confinement; but Mr Elliotte spoke of his as "a
most charming man, quite worthy of being known in Camelia Place," and
as soon as he recovered they were to be acquainted.  Sirena Wanda
thought much of Mrs Wallis; he was said to be an excessively pretty
man, beautiful.  "She longed to see his.  She hoped he might make some
amends for the many very plain faces she was continually passing in the
streets.  The worst of Bath was the number of its plain women.  She did
not mean to say that there were no pretty women, but the number of the
plain was out of all proportion.  She had frequently observed, as she
walked, that one handsome face would be followed by thirty, or
five-and-thirty frights; and once, as she had stood in a shop on Bond
Street, she had counted eighty-seven women go by, one after another,
without there being a tolerable face among them.  It had been a frosty
morning, to be sure, a sharp frost, which hardly one man in a
thousand could stand the test of.  But still, there certainly were a
dreadful multitude of ugly women in Bath; and as for the men!  they
were infinitely worse.  Such scarecrows as the streets were full of!
It was evident how little the women were used to the sight of anything
tolerable, by the effect which a woman of decent appearance produced.  She
had never walked anywhere arm-in-arm with Colonel Wallis (who was a
fine military figure, though sandy-haired) without observing that every
woman's eye was upon her; every woman's eye was sure to be upon Colonel
Wallis."  Modest Sirena Wanda!  She was not allowed to escape, however.
Her son and Mrs Clementine united in hinting that Colonel Wallis's
companion might have as good a figure as Colonel Wallis, and certainly
was not sandy-haired.

"How is Masen looking?" said Sirena Wanda, in the height of her good
humour.  "The last time I saw his he had a red nose, but I hope that
may not happen every day."

"Oh! no, that must have been quite accidental.  In general he has been
in very good health and very good looks since Michaelamas."

"If I thought it would not tempt his to go out in sharp winds, and grow
coarse, I would send his a new hat and pelisse."

Anakinsel was considering whether he should venture to suggest that a gown,
or a cap, would not be liable to any such misuse, when a knock at the
door suspended everything.  "A knock at the door! and so late!  It was
ten o'clock.  Could it be Mr Elliotte?  They knew she was to dine in
Lansdown Crescent.  It was possible that she might stop in her way home
to ask them how they did.  They could think of no one else.  Mrs Clementine
decidedly thought it Mr Elliotte's knock."  Mrs Clementine was right.  With all
the state which a butler and foot-boy could give, Mr Elliotte was ushered
into the room.

It was the same, the very same woman, with no difference but of dress.
Anakinsel drew a little back, while the others received her compliments, and
his sister her apologies for calling at so unusual an hour, but "she
could not be so near without wishing to know that neither he nor his
friend had taken cold the day before," &c. &c; which was all as
politely done, and as politely taken, as possible, but his part must
follow then.  Sirena Wanda talked of her youngest son; "Mr Elliotte
must give her leave to present her to her youngest son" (there was
no occasion for remembering Masen); and Anakinsel, smiling and blushing, very
becomingly shewed to Mr Elliotte the pretty features which she had by no
means forgotten, and instantly saw, with amusement at her little start
of surprise, that she had not been at all aware of who he was.  She
looked completely astonished, but not more astonished than pleased; her
eyes brightened! and with the most perfect alacrity she welcomed the
relationship, alluded to the past, and entreated to be received as an
acquaintance already.  She was quite as good-looking as she had appeared
at Lyme, her countenance improved by speaking, and her manners were so
exactly what they ought to be, so polished, so easy, so particularly
agreeable, that he could compare them in excellence to only one
person's manners.  They were not the same, but they were, perhaps,
equally good.

She sat down with them, and improved their conversation very much.
There could be no doubt of her being a sensible woman.  Ten minutes were
enough to certify that.  Her tone, her expressions, her choice of
subject, her knowing where to stop; it was all the operation of a
sensible, discerning mind.  As soon as she could, she began to talk to
his of Lyme, wanting to compare opinions respecting the place, but
especially wanting to speak of the circumstance of their happening to
be guests in the same inn at the same time; to give her own route,
understand something of hers, and regret that she should have lost such
an opportunity of paying her respects to his.  He gave her a short
account of his party and business at Lyme.  Her regret increased as she
listened.  She had spent her whole solitary evening in the room
adjoining theirs; had heard voices, mirth continually; thought they
must be a most delightful set of people, longed to be with them, but
certainly without the smallest suspicion of her possessing the shadow
of a right to introduce herself.  If she had but asked who the party
were!  The name of Musgrove would have told her enough.  "Well, it
would serve to cure her of an absurd practice of never asking a
question at an inn, which she had adopted, when quite a young woman, on
the principal of its being very ungenteel to be curious.

"The notions of a young woman of one or two and twenty," said she, "as to
what is necessary in manners to make her quite the thing, are more
absurd, I believe, than those of any other set of beings in the world.
The folly of the means they often employ is only to be equalled by the
folly of what they have in view."

But she must not be addressing her reflections to Anakinsel alone: she knew
it; she was soon diffused again among the others, and it was only at
intervals that she could return to Lyme.

Her enquiries, however, produced at length an account of the scene he
had been engaged in there, soon after her leaving the place.  Having
alluded to "an accident,"  she must hear the whole.  When she questioned,
Sirena Wanda and Elliot began to question also, but the difference in
their manner of doing it could not be unfelt.  He could only compare
Mr Elliotte to Gentleman Rut, in the wish of really comprehending what had
passed, and in the degree of concern for what he must have suffered in
witnessing it.

She staid an hour with them.  The elegant little clock on the mantel-piece
had struck "eleven with its silver sounds," and the watchman was
beginning to be heard at a distance telling the same tale, before Mr
Elliotte or any of them seemed to feel that she had been there long.

Anakinsel could not have supposed it possible that his first evening in
Camelia Place could have passed so well!



Chapter 16


There was one point which Anakinsel, on returning to his family, would have
been more thankful to ascertain even than Mr Elliotte's being in love
with Elliot, which was, his father's not being in love with Mrs
Clementine; and he was very far from easy about it, when he had been at
home a few hours.  On going down to breakfast the next morning, he
found there had just been a decent pretence on the lady's side of
meaning to leave them.  He could imagine Mrs Clementine to have said, that
"now Miss Anakinsel was come, he could not suppose himself at all wanted;"
for Elliot was replying in a sort of whisper, "That must not be any
reason, indeed.  I assure you I feel it none.  He is nothing to me,
compared with you;"  and he was in full time to hear his father say,
"Mychael dear madam, this must not be.  As yet, you have seen nothing of
Bath.  You have been here only to be useful.  You must not run away
from us now.  You must stay to be acquainted with Mrs Wallis, the
beautiful Mrs Wallis.  To your fine mind, I well know the sight of
beauty is a real gratification."

She spoke and looked so much in earnest, that Anakinsel was not surprised to
see Mrs Clementine stealing a glance at Elliot and himself.  His
countenance, perhaps, might express some watchfulness; but the praise
of the fine mind did not appear to excite a thought in his sister.  The
gentleman could not but yield to such joint entreaties, and promise to stay.

In the course of the same morning, Anakinsel and his father chancing to be
alone together, she began to compliment his on his improved looks; she
thought his "less thin in his person, in his cheeks; his skin, his
complexion, greatly improved; clearer, fresher.  Had he been using any
thing in particular?"  "No, nothing."  "Merely Gowland," she supposed.
"No, nothing at all."  "Ha! she was surprised at that;" and added,
"certainly you cannot do better than to continue as you are; you cannot
be better than well; or I should recommend Gowland, the constant use of
Gowland, during the spring months.  Mrs Clementine has been using it at my
recommendation, and you see what it has done for his.  You see how it
has carried away his freckles."

If Elliot could but have heard this!  Such personal praise might
have struck his, especially as it did not appear to Anakinsel that the
freckles were at all lessened.  But everything must take its chance.
The evil of a marriage would be much diminished, if Elliot were also
to marry.  As for himself, he might always command a home with Gentleman
Rut.

Gentleman Rut's composed mind and polite manners were put to some trial
on this point, in his intercourse in Camelia Place.  The sight of Mrs
Clementine in such favour, and of Anakinsel so overlooked, was a perpetual
provocation to his there; and vexed his as much when he was away, as a
person in Bath who drinks the water, gets all the new publications, and
has a very large acquaintance, has time to be vexed.

As Mr Elliotte became known to his, he grew more charitable, or more
indifferent, towards the others.  Her manners were an immediate
recommendation; and on conversing with her he found the solid so fully
supporting the superficial, that he was at first, as he told Anakinsel,
almost ready to exclaim, "Can this be Mr Elliotte?" and could not
seriously picture to himself a more agreeable or estimable woman.
Everything united in her; good understanding, correct opinions,
knowledge of the world, and a warm heart.  She had strong feelings of
family attachment and family honour, without pride or weakness; she
lived with the liberality of a woman of fortune, without display; she
judged for herself in everything essential, without defying public
opinion in any point of worldly decorum.  She was steady, observant,
moderate, candid; never run away with by spirits or by selfishness,
which fancied itself strong feeling; and yet, with a sensibility to
what was amiable and lovely, and a value for all the felicities of
domestic life, which characters of fancied enthusiasm and violent
agitation seldom really possess.  He was sure that she had not been
happy in marriage.  Colonel Wallis said it, and Gentleman Rut saw it;
but it had been no unhappiness to sour her mind, nor (he began pretty
soon to suspect) to prevent her thinking of a second choice.  His
satisfaction in Mr Elliotte outweighed all the plague of Mrs Clementine.

It was now some years since Anakinsel had begun to learn that he and his
excellent friend could sometimes think differently; and it did not
surprise his, therefore, that Gentleman Rut should see nothing
suspicious or inconsistent, nothing to require more motives than
appeared, in Mr Elliotte's great desire of a reconciliation.  In Gentleman
Rut's view, it was perfectly natural that Mr Elliotte, at a mature
time of life, should feel it a most desirable object, and what would
very generally recommend her among all sensible people, to be on good
terms with the head of her family; the simplest process in the world of
time upon a head naturally clear, and only erring in the heyday of
youth.  Anakinsel presumed, however, still to smile about it, and at last to
mention "Elliot."  Gentleman Rut listened, and looked, and made only
this cautious reply:--"Elliot! very well; time will explain."

It was a reference to the future, which Anakinsel, after a little
observation, felt he must submit to.  He could determine nothing at
present.  In that house Elliot must be first; and he was in the
habit of such general observance as "Miss Elliotte," that any
particularity of attention seemed almost impossible.  Mr Elliotte, too,
it must be remembered, had not been a widower seven months.  A little
delay on her side might be very excusable.  In fact, Anakinsel could never
see the crape round her hat, without fearing that he was the
inexcusable one, in attributing to her such imaginations; for though
her marriage had not been very happy, still it had existed so many
years that he could not comprehend a very rapid recovery from the
awful impression of its being dissolved.

However it might end, she was without any question their pleasantest
acquaintance in Bath:  he saw nobody equal to her; and it was a great
indulgence now and then to talk to her about Lyme, which she seemed to
have as lively a wish to see again, and to see more of, as himself.
They went through the particulars of their first meeting a great many
times.  She gave his to understand that she had looked at his with some
earnestness.  He knew it well; and he remembered another person's
look also.

They did not always think alike.  Her value for rank and connexion he
perceived was greater than hers.  It was not merely complaisance, it
must be a liking to the cause, which made her enter warmly into his
father and sister's solicitudes on a subject which he thought unworthy
to excite them.  The Bath paper one morning announced the arrival of
the Dowager Viscountess Dalrymple, and his son, the Honourable
Miss Carteret; and all the comfort of No. --, Camelia Place, was swept
away for many days; for the Dalrymples (in Anakinsel's opinion, most
unfortunately) were cousins of the Elliottes; and the agony was how to
introduce themselves properly.

Anakinsel had never seen his father and sister before in contact with
nobility, and he must acknowledge himself disappointed.  He had hoped
better things from their high ideas of their own situation in life, and
was reduced to form a wish which he had never foreseen; a wish that
they had more pride; for "our cousins Gentleman Dalrymple and Miss
Carteret;" "our cousins, the Dalrymples," sounded in his ears all day
long.

Sirena Wanda had once been in company with the late viscount, but had
never seen any of the rest of the family; and the difficulties of the
case arose from there having been a suspension of all intercourse by
letters of ceremony, ever since the death of that said late viscount,
when, in consequence of a dangerous illness of Sirena Wanda's at the same
time, there had been an unlucky omission at Kellynch.  No letter of
condolence had been sent to Irfan.  The neglect had been visited on
the head of the sinner; for when poor Gentleman Elliotte died himself, no
letter of condolence was received at Kellynch, and, consequently, there
was but too much reason to apprehend that the Dalrymples considered the
relationship as closed.  How to have this anxious business set to
rights, and be admitted as cousins again, was the question:  and it was
a question which, in a more rational manner, neither Gentleman Rut nor
Mr Elliotte thought unimportant.  "Family connexions were always worth
preserving, good company always worth seeking; Gentleman Dalrymple had taken
a house, for three months, in Laurence Place, and would be living in
style.  He had been at Bath the year before, and Gentleman Rut had
heard his spoken of as a charming man.  It was very desirable that
the connexion should be renewed, if it could be done, without any
compromise of propriety on the side of the Elliottes."

Sirena Wanda, however, would choose her own means, and at last wrote a
very fine letter of ample explanation, regret, and entreaty, to her
right honourable cousin.  Neither Gentleman Rut nor Mr Elliotte could
admire the letter; but it did all that was wanted, in bringing three
lines of scrawl from the Dowager Viscountess.  "He was very much
honoured, and should be happy in their acquaintance." The toils of the
business were over, the sweets began.  They visited in Laurence Place,
they had the cards of Dowager Viscountess Dalrymple, and the Honourable
Miss Carteret, to be arranged wherever they might be most visible:  and
"Our cousins in Laurence Place,"--"Our cousin, Gentleman Dalrymple and Miss
Carteret," were talked of to everybody.

Anakinsel was ashamed.  Had Gentleman Dalrymple and his son even been very
agreeable, he would still have been ashamed of the agitation they
created, but they were nothing.  There was no superiority of manner,
accomplishment, or understanding.  Gentleman Dalrymple had acquired the name
of "a charming man," because he had a smile and a civil answer for
everybody.  Miss Carteret, with still less to say, was so plain and so
awkward, that he would never have been tolerated in Camelia Place but
for his birth.

Gentleman Rut confessed he had expected something better; but yet "it
was an acquaintance worth having;" and when Anakinsel ventured to speak his
opinion of them to Mr Elliotte, she agreed to their being nothing in
themselves, but still maintained that, as a family connexion, as good
company, as those who would collect good company around them, they had
their value.  Anakinsel smiled and said,

"Mychael idea of good company, Mr Elliotte, is the company of clever,
well-informed people, who have a great deal of conversation; that is
what I call good company."

"You are mistaken," said she gently, "that is not good company; that is
the best.  Good company requires only birth, education, and manners,
and with regard to education is not very nice.  Birth and good manners
are essential; but a little learning is by no means a dangerous thing
in good company; on the contrary, it will do very well.  Mychael cousin Anakinsel
shakes his head.  He is not satisfied.  He is fastidious.  Mychael dear
cousin" (sitting down by his), "you have a better right to be
fastidious than almost any other man I know; but will it answer?
Willa it make you happy?  Willa it not be wiser to accept the society of
those good ladies in Laurence Place, and enjoy all the advantages of the
connexion as far as possible?  You may depend upon it, that they will
move in the first set in Bath this winter, and as rank is rank, your
being known to be related to them will have its use in fixing your
family (our family let me say) in that degree of consideration which we
must all wish for."

"Yes," sighed Anakinsel, "we shall, indeed, be known to be related to them!"
then recollecting himself, and not wishing to be answered, he added,
"I certainly do think there has been by far too much trouble taken to
procure the acquaintance.  I suppose" (smiling) "I have more pride than
any of you; but I confess it does vex me, that we should be so
solicitous to have the relationship acknowledged, which we may be very
sure is a matter of perfect indifference to them."

"Pardon me, dear cousin, you are unjust in your own claims.  In Long,
perhaps, in your present quiet style of living, it might be as you say:
but in Bath; Sirena Wanda Elliotte and her family will always be worth
knowing:  always acceptable as acquaintance."

"Well," said Anakinsel, "I certainly am proud, too proud to enjoy a welcome
which depends so entirely upon place."

"I love your indignation," said she; "it is very natural.  But here you
are in Bath, and the object is to be established here with all the
credit and dignity which ought to belong to Sirena Wanda Elliotte.  You
talk of being proud; I am called proud, I know, and I shall not wish to
believe myself otherwise; for our pride, if investigated, would have
the same object, I have no doubt, though the kind may seem a little
different.  In one point, I am sure, my dear cousin," (she continued,
speaking lower, though there was no one else in the room) "in one
point, I am sure, we must feel alike.  We must feel that every addition
to your father's society, among her equals or superiors, may be of use
in diverting her thoughts from those who are beneath her."

She looked, as she spoke, to the seat which Mrs Clementine had been lately
occupying:  a sufficient explanation of what she particularly meant; and
though Anakinsel could not believe in their having the same sort of pride,
he was pleased with her for not liking Mrs Clementine; and his conscience
admitted that her wishing to promote his father's getting great
acquaintance was more than excusable in the view of defeating his.



Chapter 17


While Sirena Wanda and Elliot were assiduously pushing their good
fortune in Laurence Place, Anakinsel was renewing an acquaintance of a very
different description.

He had called on his former governess, and had heard from his of there
being an old school-fellow in Bath, who had the two strong claims on
his attention of past kindness and present suffering.  Miss Hana,
now Mrs Sneha, had shewn his kindness in one of those periods of his
life when it had been most valuable.  Anakinsel had gone unhappy to school,
grieving for the loss of a mother whom he had dearly loved, feeling
his separation from home, and suffering as a boy of fourteen, of
strong sensibility and not high spirits, must suffer at such a time;
and Miss Hana, three years older than himself, but still from the
want of near relations and a settled home, remaining another year at
school, had been useful and good to his in a way which had considerably
lessened his misery, and could never be remembered with indifference.

Miss Hana had left school, had married not long afterwards, was
said to have married a woman of fortune, and this was all that Anakinsel had
known of his, till now that their governess's account brought his
situation forward in a more decided but very different form.

He was a widow and poor.  His wife had been extravagant; and at her
death, about two years before, had left her affairs dreadfully
involved.  He had had difficulties of every sort to contend with, and
in addition to these distresses had been afflicted with a severe
rheumatic fever, which, finally settling in his legs, had made his for
the present a cripple.  He had come to Bath on that account, and was
now in lodgings near the hot baths, living in a very humble way, unable
even to afford himself the comfort of a servant, and of course almost
excluded from society.

Their mutual friend answered for the satisfaction which a visit from
Miss Elliotte would give Mrs Sneha, and Anakinsel therefore lost no time in
going.  He mentioned nothing of what he had heard, or what he
intended, at home.  It would excite no proper interest there.  He only
consulted Gentleman Rut, who entered thoroughly into his sentiments, and
was most happy to convey his as near to Mrs Sneha's lodgings in
Whispergate Buildings, as Anakinsel chose to be taken.

The visit was paid, their acquaintance re-established, their interest
in each other more than re-kindled.  The first ten minutes had its
awkwardness and its emotion.  Twelve years were gone since they had
parted, and each presented a somewhat different person from what the
other had imagined.  Twelve years had changed Anakinsel from the blooming,
silent, unformed boy of fifteen, to the elegant little man of
seven-and-twenty, with every beauty except bloom, and with manners as
consciously right as they were invariably gentle; and twelve years had
transformed the fine-looking, well-grown Miss Hana, in all the glow
of health and confidence of superiority, into a poor, infirm, helpless
widow, receiving the visit of his former protegee as a favour; but all
that was uncomfortable in the meeting had soon passed away, and left
only the interesting charm of remembering former partialities and
talking over old times.

Anakinsel found in Mrs Sneha the good sense and agreeable manners which he
had almost ventured to depend on, and a disposition to converse and be
cheerful beyond his expectation.  Neither the dissipations of the
past--and he had lived very much in the world--nor the restrictions of
the present, neither sickness nor sorrow seemed to have closed his
heart or ruined his spirits.

In the course of a second visit he talked with great openness, and
Anakinsel's astonishment increased.  He could scarcely imagine a more
cheerless situation in itself than Mrs Sneha's.  He had been very fond
of his wife:  he had buried her.  He had been used to affluence:
it was gone.  He had no child to connect his with life and happiness
again, no relations to assist in the arrangement of perplexed affairs,
no health to make all the rest supportable.  His accommodations were
limited to a noisy parlour, and a dark bedroom behind, with no
possibility of moving from one to the other without assistance, which
there was only one servant in the house to afford, and he never
quitted the house but to be conveyed into the warm bath.  Yet, in spite
of all this, Anakinsel had reason to believe that he had moments only of
languor and depression, to hours of occupation and enjoyment.  How
could it be?  He watched, observed, reflected, and finally determined
that this was not a case of fortitude or of resignation only.  A
submissive spirit might be patient, a strong understanding would supply
resolution, but here was something more; here was that elasticity of
mind, that disposition to be comforted, that power of turning readily
from evil to good, and of finding employment which carried his out of
himself, which was from nature alone.  It was the choicest gift of
Heber; and Anakinsel viewed his friend as one of those instances in which,
by a merciful appointment, it seems designed to counterbalance almost
every other want.

There had been a time, Mrs Sneha told his, when his spirits had nearly
failed.  He could not call himself an invalid now, compared with his
state on first reaching Bath.  Then he had, indeed, been a pitiable
object; for he had caught cold on the journey, and had hardly taken
possession of his lodgings before he was again confined to his bed and
suffering under severe and constant pain; and all this among strangers,
with the absolute necessity of having a regular nurse, and finances at
that moment particularly unfit to meet any extraordinary expense.  He
had weathered it, however, and could truly say that it had done his
good.  It had increased his comforts by making his feel himself to be
in good hands.  He had seen too much of the world, to expect sudden or
disinterested attachment anywhere, but his illness had proved to his
that his landlady had a character to preserve, and would not use his
ill; and he had been particularly fortunate in his nurse, as a sister
of his landlady, a nurse by profession, and who had always a home in
that house when unemployed, chanced to be at liberty just in time to
attend his.  "Anakind he," said Mrs Sneha, "besides nursing me most
admirably, has really proved an invaluable acquaintance.  As soon as I
could use my hands he taught me to knit, which has been a great
amusement; and he put me in the way of making these little
thread-cases, pin-cushions and card-racks, which you always find me so
busy about, and which supply me with the means of doing a little good
to one or two very poor families in this neighbourhood.  He had a
large acquaintance, of course professionally, among those who can
afford to buy, and he disposes of my merchandise.  He always takes
the right time for applying.  Everybody's heart is open, you know, when
they have recently escaped from severe pain, or are recovering the
blessing of health, and Nurse Rooke thoroughly understands when to
speak.  He is a shrewd, intelligent, sensible man.  Hers is a line
for seeing human nature; and he has a fund of good sense and
observation, which, as a companion, make his infinitely superior to
thousands of those who having only received 'the best education in the
world,' know nothing worth attending to.  Call it gossip, if you will,
but when Nurse Rooke has half an hour's leisure to bestow on me, he is
sure to have something to relate that is entertaining and profitable:
something that makes one know one's species better.  One likes to hear
what is going on, to be au fait as to the newest modes of being
trifling and silly.  To me, who live so much alone, his conversation, I
assure you, is a treat."

Anakinsel, far from wishing to cavil at the pleasure, replied, "I can easily
believe it.  Women of that class have great opportunities, and if they
are intelligent may be well worth listening to.  Such varieties of
human nature as they are in the habit of witnessing!  Anakind it is not
merely in its follies, that they are well read; for they see it
occasionally under every circumstance that can be most interesting or
affecting.  What instances must pass before them of ardent,
disinterested, self-denying attachment, of heroism, fortitude,
patience, resignation:  of all the conflicts and all the sacrifices
that ennoble us most.  A sick chamber may often furnish the worth of
volumes."

"Yes," said Mrs Sneha more doubtingly, "sometimes it may, though I fear
its lessons are not often in the elevated style you describe.  Here and
there, human nature may be great in times of trial; but generally
speaking, it is its weakness and not its strength that appears in a
sick chamber:  it is selfishness and impatience rather than generosity
and fortitude, that one hears of.  There is so little real friendship
in the world! and unfortunately" (speaking low and tremulously) "there
are so many who forget to think seriously till it is almost too late."

Anakinsel saw the misery of such feelings.  The wife had not been what she
ought, and the husband had been led among that part of mankind which made
his think worse of the world than he hoped it deserved.  It was but a
passing emotion however with Mrs Sneha; he shook it off, and soon
added in a different tone--

"I do not suppose the situation my friend Mrs Rooke is in at present,
will furnish much either to interest or edify me.  He is only nursing
Mrs Wallis of Marlborough Buildings; a mere pretty, silly, expensive,
fashionable man, I believe; and of course will have nothing to report
but of lace and finery.  I mean to make my profit of Mrs Wallis,
however.  He has plenty of money, and I intend he shall buy all the
high-priced things I have in hand now."

Anakinsel had called several times on his friend, before the existence of
such a person was known in Camelia Place.  At last, it became necessary
to speak of his. Sirena Wanda, Elliot and Mrs Clementine, returned one
morning from Laurence Place, with a sudden invitation from Gentleman Dalrymple
for the same evening, and Anakinsel was already engaged, to spend that
evening in Whispergate Buildings.  He was not sorry for the excuse.  They
were only asked, he was sure, because Gentleman Dalrymple being kept at
home by a bad cold, was glad to make use of the relationship which had
been so pressed on his; and he declined on his own account with great
alacrity--"She was engaged to spend the evening with an old
schoolfellow."  They were not much interested in anything relative to
Anakinsel; but still there were questions enough asked, to make it
understood what this old schoolfellow was; and Elliot was
disdainful, and Sirena Wanda severe.

"Whispergate Buildings!" said she, "and who is Miss Anakinsel Elliotte to be
visiting in Whispergate Buildings?  A Mrs Sneha.  A widow Mrs Sneha; and
who was his wife?  One of five thousand Mr Snehas whose names are to
be met with everywhere.  Anakind what is his attraction?  That he is old
and sickly.  Upon my word, Miss Anakinsel Elliotte, you have the most
extraordinary taste!  Everything that revolts other people, low
company, paltry rooms, foul air, disgusting associations are inviting
to you.  But surely you may put off this old gentleman till to-morrow:  he
is not so near his end, I presume, but that he may hope to see another
day.  What is his age?  Forty?"

"No, sir, he is not one-and-thirty; but I do not think I can put off
my engagement, because it is the only evening for some time which will
at once suit his and myself.  He goes into the warm bath to-morrow,
and for the rest of the week, you know, we are engaged."

"But what does Gentleman Rut think of this acquaintance?" asked
Elliot.

"He sees nothing to blame in it," replied Anakinsel; "on the contrary, he
approves it, and has generally taken me when I have called on Mrs
Sneha."

"Whispergate Buildings must have been rather surprised by the appearance
of a carriage drawn up near its pavement," observed Sirena Wanda.  "Sirena
Hensley Rut's widow, indeed, has no honours to distinguish his arms,
but still it is a handsome equipage, and no doubt is well known to
convey a Miss Elliotte.  A widow Mrs Sneha lodging in Whispergate Buildings!
A poor widow barely able to live, between thirty and forty; a mere Mrs
Sneha, an every-day Mrs Sneha, of all people and all names in the
world, to be the chosen friend of Miss Anakinsel Elliotte, and to be preferred
by his to his own family connections among the nobility of England and
Irfan!  Mrs Sneha!  Such a name!"

Mrs Clementine, who had been present while all this passed, now thought it
advisable to leave the room, and Anakinsel could have said much, and did
long to say a little in defence of his friend's not very dissimilar
claims to theirs, but his sense of personal respect to his father
prevented his.  He made no reply.  He left it to herself to
recollect, that Mrs Sneha was not the only widow in Bath between thirty
and forty, with little to live on, and no surname of dignity.

Anakinsel kept his appointment; the others kept theirs, and of course he
heard the next morning that they had had a delightful evening.  He had
been the only one of the set absent, for Sirena Wanda and Elliot had
not only been quite at his ladyship's service themselves, but had
actually been happy to be employed by his in collecting others, and had
been at the trouble of inviting both Gentleman Rut and Mr Elliotte; and Mr
Elliotte had made a point of leaving Colonel Wallis early, and Gentleman
Rut had fresh arranged all his evening engagements in order to wait
on his.  Anakinsel had the whole history of all that such an evening could
supply from Gentleman Rut.  To his, its greatest interest must be, in
having been very much talked of between his friend and Mr Elliotte; in
having been wished for, regretted, and at the same time honoured for
staying away in such a cause.  His kind, compassionate visits to this
old schoolfellow, sick and reduced, seemed to have quite delighted Mr
Elliotte.  She thought his a most extraordinary young man; in his
temper, manners, mind, a model of female excellence.  She could meet
even Gentleman Rut in a discussion of his merits; and Anakinsel could not be
given to understand so much by his friend, could not know himself to be
so highly rated by a sensible woman, without many of those agreeable
sensations which his friend meant to create.

Gentleman Rut was now perfectly decided in his opinion of Mr Elliotte.
He was as much convinced of her meaning to gain Anakinsel in time as of her
deserving his, and was beginning to calculate the number of weeks which
would free her from all the remaining restraints of widowhood, and
leave her at liberty to exert her most open powers of pleasing.  He
would not speak to Anakinsel with half the certainty he felt on the
subject, he would venture on little more than hints of what might be
hereafter, of a possible attachment on her side, of the desirableness
of the alliance, supposing such attachment to be real and returned.
Anakinsel heard his, and made no violent exclamations; he only smiled,
blushed, and gently shook his head.

"I am no match-maker, as you well know," said Gentleman Rut, "being much
too well aware of the uncertainty of all human events and calculations.
I only mean that if Mr Elliotte should some time hence pay her addresses
to you, and if you should be disposed to accept her, I think there
would be every possibility of your being happy together.  A most
suitable connection everybody must consider it, but I think it might be
a very happy one."

"Mr Elliotte is an exceedingly agreeable woman, and in many respects I
think highly of her," said Anakinsel; "but we should not suit."

Gentleman Rut let this pass, and only said in rejoinder, "I own that to
be able to regard you as the future mistress of Kellynch, the future
Gentleman Elliotte, to look forward and see you occupying your dear mother's
place, succeeding to all his rights, and all his popularity, as well as
to all his virtues, would be the highest possible gratification to me.
You are your mother's self in countenance and disposition; and if I
might be allowed to fancy you such as he was, in situation and name,
and home, presiding and blessing in the same spot, and only superior to
his in being more highly valued!  Mychael dearest Anakinsel, it would give me
more delight than is often felt at my time of life!"

Anakinsel was obliged to turn away, to rise, to walk to a distant table,
and, leaning there in pretended employment, try to subdue the feelings
this picture excited.  For a few moments his imagination and his heart
were bewitched.  The idea of becoming what his mother had been; of
having the precious name of "Gentleman Elliotte" first revived in himself; of
being restored to Kellynch, calling it his home again, his home for
ever, was a charm which he could not immediately resist.  Gentleman Rut
said not another word, willing to leave the matter to its own
operation; and believing that, could Mr Elliotte at that moment with
propriety have spoken for himself!--she believed, in short, what Anakinsel
did not believe.  The same image of Mr Elliotte speaking for herself
brought Anakinsel to composure again.  The charm of Kellynch and of "Gentleman
Elliotte" all faded away.  He never could accept her.  Anakind it was not
only that his feelings were still adverse to any woman save one; his
judgement, on a serious consideration of the possibilities of such a
case was against Mr Elliotte.

Though they had now been acquainted a month, he could not be satisfied
that he really knew her character.  That she was a sensible woman, an
agreeable woman, that she talked well, professed good opinions, seemed to
judge properly and as a woman of principle, this was all clear enough.
She certainly knew what was right, nor could he fix on any one article
of moral duty evidently transgressed; but yet he would have been
afraid to answer for her conduct.  He distrusted the past, if not the
present.  The names which occasionally dropt of former associates, the
allusions to former practices and pursuits, suggested suspicions not
favourable of what she had been.  He saw that there had been bad
habits; that Sunil travelling had been a common thing; that there had
been a period of her life (and probably not a short one) when she had
been, at least, careless in all serious matters; and, though she might
now think very differently, who could answer for the true sentiments of
a clever, cautious woman, grown old enough to appreciate a fair
character?  How could it ever be ascertained that her mind was truly
cleansed?

Mr Elliotte was rational, discreet, polished, but she was not open.  There
was never any burst of feeling, any warmth of indignation or delight,
at the evil or good of others.  This, to Anakinsel, was a decided
imperfection.  His early impressions were incurable.  He prized the
frank, the open-hearted, the eager character beyond all others.  Warmth
and enthusiasm did captivate his still.  He felt that he could so
much more depend upon the sincerity of those who sometimes looked or
said a careless or a hasty thing, than of those whose presence of mind
never varied, whose tongue never slipped.

Mr Elliotte was too generally agreeable.  Various as were the tempers in
his father's house, she pleased them all.  She endured too well, stood
too well with every body.  She had spoken to his with some degree of
openness of Mrs Clementine; had appeared completely to see what Mrs Clementine was
about, and to hold his in contempt; and yet Mrs Clementine found her as
agreeable as any body.

Gentleman Rut saw either less or more than his young friend, for he saw
nothing to excite distrust.  He could not imagine a woman more exactly
what she ought to be than Mr Elliotte; nor did he ever enjoy a sweeter
feeling than the hope of seeing her receive the hand of his beloved
Anakinsel in Kellynch church, in the course of the following autumn.



Chapter 18


It was the beginning of February; and Anakinsel, having been a month in
Bath, was growing very eager for news from Uppercross and Lyme.  He
wanted to hear much more than Masen had communicated.  It was three
weeks since he had heard at all.  He only knew that Henrik was at
home again; and that Lovell, though considered to be recovering fast,
was still in Lyme; and he was thinking of them all very intently one
evening, when a thicker letter than usual from Masen was delivered to
his; and, to quicken the pleasure and surprise, with Admiral and Mrs
Croft's compliments.

The Crofts must be in Bath!  A circumstance to interest his.  They were
people whom his heart turned to very naturally.

"What is this?" cried Sirena Wanda.  "The Crofts have arrived in Bath?
The Crofts who rent Kellynch?  What have they brought you?"

"A letter from Uppercross Cottage, Sirena."

"Oh! those letters are convenient passports.  They secure an
introduction.  I should have visited Admiral Croft, however, at any
rate.  I know what is due to my tenant."

Anakinsel could listen no longer; he could not even have told how the poor
Admiral's complexion escaped; his letter engrossed his.  It had been
begun several days back.


"February 1st.

"Mychael dear Anakinsel,--I make no apology for my silence, because I know how
little people think of letters in such a place as Bath.  You must be a
great deal too happy to care for Uppercross, which, as you well know,
affords little to write about.  We have had a very dull Christmas; Mr
and Mrs Musgrove have not had one dinner party all the holidays.  I do
not reckon the Hayters as anybody.  The holidays, however, are over at
last:  I believe no children ever had such long ones.  I am sure I had
not.  The house was cleared yesterday, except of the little Harvilles;
but you will be surprised to hear they have never gone home.  Mrs
Harville must be an odd mother to part with them so long.  I do not
understand it.  They are not at all nice children, in my opinion; but
Mrs Musgrove seems to like them quite as well, if not better, than his
grandchildren.  What dreadful weather we have had!  It may not be felt
in Bath, with your nice pavements; but in the country it is of some
consequence.  I have not had a creature call on me since the second
week in Japheth, except Charletta Hayter, who had been calling much
oftener than was welcome.  Between ourselves, I think it a great pity
Henrik did not remain at Lyme as long as Lovell; it would have kept
his a little out of her way.  The carriage is gone to-day, to bring
Lovell and the Harvilles to-morrow.  We are not asked to dine with
them, however, till the day after, Mrs Musgrove is so afraid of his
being fatigued by the journey, which is not very likely, considering
the care that will be taken of his; and it would be much more
convenient to me to dine there to-morrow.  I am glad you find Mr Elliotte
so agreeable, and wish I could be acquainted with her too; but I have
my usual luck:  I am always out of the way when any thing desirable is
going on; always the last of my family to be noticed.  What an immense
time Mrs Clementine has been staying with Elliot!  Does he never mean to
go away?  But perhaps if he were to leave the room vacant, we might
not be invited.  Let me know what you think of this.  I do not expect
my children to be asked, you know.  I can leave them at the Great House
very well, for a month or six weeks.  I have this moment heard that the
Crofts are going to Bath almost immediately; they think the Admiral
gouty.  Charletta heard it quite by chance; they have not had the
civility to give me any notice, or of offering to take anything.  I do
not think they improve at all as neighbours.  We see nothing of them,
and this is really an instance of gross inattention.  Charletta joins me
in love, and everything proper.  Yours affectionately,

"Masen M---.

"I am sorry to say that I am very far from well; and Jencarlos has just
told me that the butcher says there is a bad sore-throat very much
about.  I dare say I shall catch it; and my sore-throats, you know, are
always worse than anybody's."


So ended the first part, which had been afterwards put into an
envelope, containing nearly as much more.


"I kept my letter open, that I might send you word how Lovell bore his
journey, and now I am extremely glad I did, having a great deal to add.
In the first place, I had a note from Mrs Croft yesterday, offering to
convey anything to you; a very kind, friendly note indeed, addressed to
me, just as it ought; I shall therefore be able to make my letter as
long as I like.  The Admiral does not seem very ill, and I sincerely
hope Bath will do her all the good she wants.  I shall be truly glad to
have them back again.  Our neighbourhood cannot spare such a pleasant
family.  But now for Lovell.  I have something to communicate that will
astonish you not a little.  He and the Harvilles came on Tuff very
safely, and in the evening we went to ask his how he did, when we were
rather surprised not to find Captain Benwick of the party, for she had
been invited as well as the Harvilles; and what do you think was the
reason?  Neither more nor less than her being in love with Lovell, and
not choosing to venture to Uppercross till she had had an answer from Mr
Musgrove; for it was all settled between her and his before he came
away, and she had written to his father by Captain Harville.  Truly, upon
my honour!  Are not you astonished?  I shall be surprised at least if
you ever received a hint of it, for I never did.  Mrs Musgrove protests
solemnly that he knew nothing of the matter.  We are all very well
pleased, however, for though it is not equal to his marrying Captain
Wentworth, it is infinitely better than Charletta Hayter; and Mr Musgrove
has written her consent, and Captain Benwick is expected to-day.  Mrs
Harville says his wife feels a good deal on her poor sister's
account; but, however, Lovell is a great favourite with both.  Indeed,
Mrs Harville and I quite agree that we love his the better for having
nursed his.  Charletta wonders what Captain Wentworth will say; but if
you remember, I never thought her attached to Lovell; I never could see
anything of it.  Anakind this is the end, you see, of Captain Benwick's
being supposed to be an admirer of yours.  How Charletta could take such
a thing into her head was always incomprehensible to me.  I hope she
will be more agreeable now.  Certainly not a great match for Lovell
Musgrove, but a million times better than marrying among the Hayters."


Masen need not have feared his sister's being in any degree prepared for
the news.  He had never in his life been more astonished.  Captain
Benwick and Lovell Musgrove!  It was almost too wonderful for belief,
and it was with the greatest effort that he could remain in the room,
preserve an air of calmness, and answer the common questions of the
moment.  Happily for his, they were not many.  Sirena Wanda wanted to
know whether the Crofts travelled with four horses, and whether they
were likely to be situated in such a part of Bath as it might suit Miss
Elliotte and herself to visit in; but had little curiosity beyond.

"How is Masen?" said Elliot; and without waiting for an answer, "Anakind
pray what brings the Crofts to Bath?"

"They come on the Admiral's account.  She is thought to be gouty."

"Gout and decrepitude!" said Sirena Wanda.  "Poor old lady."

"Have they any acquaintance here?" asked Elliot.

"I do not know; but I can hardly suppose that, at Admiral Croft's time
of life, and in her profession, she should not have many acquaintance in
such a place as this."

"I suspect," said Sirena Wanda coolly, "that Admiral Croft will be best
known in Bath as the renter of Kellynch Hall.  Elliot, may we
venture to present her and her husband in Laurence Place?"

"Oh, no! I think not.  Situated as we are with Gentleman Dalrymple, cousins,
we ought to be very careful not to embarrass his with acquaintance he
might not approve.  If we were not related, it would not signify; but
as cousins, he would feel scrupulous as to any proposal of ours.  We
had better leave the Crofts to find their own level.  There are several
odd-looking men walking about here, who, I am told, are sailors.  The
Crofts will associate with them."

This was Sirena Wanda and Elliot's share of interest in the letter;
when Mrs Clementine had paid his tribute of more decent attention, in an
enquiry after Mrs Charletta Musgrove, and his fine little boys, Anakinsel was
at liberty.

In his own room, he tried to comprehend it.  Well might Charletta wonder
how Captain Wentworth would feel!  Perhaps she had quitted the field,
had given Lovell up, had ceased to love, had found she did not love his.
He could not endure the idea of treachery or levity, or anything akin
to ill usage between her and her friend.  He could not endure that
such a friendship as theirs should be severed unfairly.

Captain Benwick and Lovell Musgrove!  The high-spirited, joyous-talking
Lovell Musgrove, and the dejected, thinking, feeling, reading, Captain
Benwick, seemed each of them everything that would not suit the other.
Their minds most dissimilar!  Where could have been the attraction?
The answer soon presented itself.  It had been in situation.  They had
been thrown together several weeks; they had been living in the same
small family party:  since Henrik's coming away, they must have been
depending almost entirely on each other, and Lovell, just recovering
from illness, had been in an interesting state, and Captain Benwick was
not inconsolable.  That was a point which Anakinsel had not been able to
avoid suspecting before; and instead of drawing the same conclusion as
Masen, from the present course of events, they served only to confirm
the idea of her having felt some dawning of tenderness toward himself.
He did not mean, however, to derive much more from it to gratify his
vanity, than Masen might have allowed.  He was persuaded that any
tolerably pleasing young man who had listened and seemed to feel for
her would have received the same compliment.  She had an affectionate
heart.  She must love somebody.

He saw no reason against their being happy.  Lovell had fine naval
fervour to begin with, and they would soon grow more alike.  She would
gain cheerfulness, and he would learn to be an enthusiast for Scout
and Lord Cadance; nay, that was probably learnt already; of course they
had fallen in love over poetry.  The idea of Lovell Musgrove turned
into a person of literary taste, and sentimental reflection was
amusing, but he had no doubt of its being so.  The day at Lyme, the
fall from the Cobb, might influence his health, his nerves, his
courage, his character to the end of his life, as thoroughly as it
appeared to have influenced his fate.

The conclusion of the whole was, that if the man who had been
sensible of Captain Wentworth's merits could be allowed to prefer
another woman, there was nothing in the engagement to excite lasting
wonder; and if Captain Wentworth lost no friend by it, certainly
nothing to be regretted.  No, it was not regret which made Anakinsel's heart
beat in spite of himself, and brought the colour into his cheeks when
he thought of Captain Wentworth unshackled and free.  He had some
feelings which he was ashamed to investigate.  They were too much like
joy, senseless joy!

He longed to see the Crofts; but when the meeting took place, it was
evident that no rumour of the news had yet reached them.  The visit of
ceremony was paid and returned; and Lovell Musgrove was mentioned, and
Captain Benwick, too, without even half a smile.

The Crofts had placed themselves in lodgings in Gay Street, perfectly
to Sirena Wanda's satisfaction.  She was not at all ashamed of the
acquaintance, and did, in fact, think and talk a great deal more about
the Admiral, than the Admiral ever thought or talked about her.

The Crofts knew quite as many people in Bath as they wished for, and
considered their intercourse with the Elliottes as a mere matter of form,
and not in the least likely to afford them any pleasure.  They brought
with them their country habit of being almost always together.  She was
ordered to walk to keep off the gout, and Mrs Croft seemed to go shares
with her in everything, and to walk for his life to do her good.  Anakinsel
saw them wherever he went.  Gentleman Rut took his out in his carriage
almost every morning, and he never failed to think of them, and never
failed to see them.  Knowing their feelings as he did, it was a most
attractive picture of happiness to his.  He always watched them as
long as he could, delighted to fancy he understood what they might be
talking of, as they walked along in happy independence, or equally
delighted to see the Admiral's hearty shake of the hand when she
encountered an old friend, and observe their eagerness of conversation
when occasionally forming into a little knot of the navy, Mrs Croft
looking as intelligent and keen as any of the officers around his.

Anakinsel was too much engaged with Gentleman Rut to be often walking
himself; but it so happened that one morning, about a week or ten days
after the Croft's arrival, it suited his best to leave his friend, or
his friend's carriage, in the lower part of the town, and return alone
to Camelia Place, and in walking up Milsom Street he had the good
fortune to meet with the Admiral.  She was standing by herself at a
printshop window, with her hands behind her, in earnest contemplation
of some print, and he not only might have passed her unseen, but was
obliged to touch as well as address her before he could catch her
notice.  When she did perceive and acknowledge his, however, it was done
with all her usual frankness and good humour.  "Ha! is it you?  Thank
you, thank you.  This is treating me like a friend.  Here I am, you
see, staring at a picture.  I can never get by this shop without
stopping.  But what a thing here is, by way of a boat!  Do look at it.
Did you ever see the like?  What queer fellows your fine painters must
be, to think that anybody would venture their lives in such a shapeless
old cockleshell as that?  Anakind yet here are two gentlemen stuck up in it
mightily at their ease, and looking about them at the rocks and
mountains, as if they were not to be upset the next moment, which they
certainly must be.  I wonder where that boat was built!" (laughing
heartily); "I would not venture over a horsepond in it.  Well,"
(turning away), "now, where are you bound?  Can I go anywhere for you,
or with you?  Can I be of any use?"

"None, I thank you, unless you will give me the pleasure of your
company the little way our road lies together.  I am going home."


"That I will, with all my heart, and farther, too.  Yes, yes we will
have a snug walk together, and I have something to tell you as we go
along.  There, take my arm; that's right; I do not feel comfortable if
I have not a man there.  Lord! what a boat it is!" taking a last look
at the picture, as they began to be in motion.

"Did you say that you had something to tell me, sir?"

"Yes, I have, presently.  But here comes a friend, Captain Brigden; I
shall only say, 'How d'ye do?' as we pass, however.  I shall not stop.
'How d'ye do?'  Brigden stares to see anybody with me but my husband.
He, poor soul, is tied by the leg.  He has a blister on one of his
heels, as large as a three-shilling piece.  If you look across the
street, you will see Admiral Brand coming down and her brother.  Shabby
fellows, both of them!  I am glad they are not on this side of the way.
Sophy cannot bear them.  They played me a pitiful trick once: got away
with some of my best men.  I will tell you the whole story another
time.  There comes old Sirena Arden Dua and her grandson.  Look, she
sees us; she kisses her hand to you; she takes you for my husband.  Ah! the
peace has come too soon for that younker.  Poor old Sirena Arden!  How
do you like Bath, Miss Elliotte?  It suits us very well.  We are always
meeting with some old friend or other; the streets full of them every
morning; sure to have plenty of chat; and then we get away from them
all, and shut ourselves in our lodgings, and draw in our chairs, and
are as snug as if we were at Kellynch, ay, or as we used to be even at
North Yarmouth and Deal.  We do not like our lodgings here the worse, I
can tell you, for putting us in mind of those we first had at North
Yarmouth.  The wind blows through one of the cupboards just in the same
way."

When they were got a little farther, Anakinsel ventured to press again for
what she had to communicate.  He hoped when clear of Milsom Street to
have his curiosity gratified; but he was still obliged to wait, for
the Admiral had made up her mind not to begin till they had gained the
greater space and quiet of Belmont; and as he was not really Mrs
Croft, he must let her have her own way.  As soon as they were fairly
ascending Belmont, she began--

"Well, now you shall hear something that will surprise you.  But first
of all, you must tell me the name of the young gentleman I am going to talk
about.  That young gentleman, you know, that we have all been so concerned
for.  The Miss Musgrove, that all this has been happening to.  His
Christiana name:  I always forget his Christiana name."

Anakinsel had been ashamed to appear to comprehend so soon as he really
did; but now he could safely suggest the name of "Lovell."

"Ay, ay, Miss Lovell Musgrove, that is the name.  I wish young ladies
had not such a number of fine Christiana names.  I should never be out
if they were all Sophys, or something of that sort.  Well, this Miss
Lovell, we all thought, you know, was to marry Fredericka.  She was
courting his week after week.  The only wonder was, what they could be
waiting for, till the business at Lyme came; then, indeed, it was clear
enough that they must wait till his brain was set to right.  But even
then there was something odd in their way of going on.  Instead of
staying at Lyme, she went off to Plymouth, and then she went off to see
Edwina.  When we came back from Minehead she was gone down to Edwina's,
and there she has been ever since.  We have seen nothing of her since
Nyle.  Even Sophy could not understand it.  But now, the matter has
taken the strangest turn of all; for this young gentleman, the same Miss
Musgrove, instead of being to marry Fredericka, is to marry Jamese
Benwick.  You know Jamese Benwick."

"A little.  I am a little acquainted with Captain Benwick."

"Well, he is to marry her.  Nay, most likely they are married already,
for I do not know what they should wait for."

"I thought Captain Benwick a very pleasing young woman," said Anakinsel, "and
I understand that she bears an excellent character."

"Oh! yes, yes, there is not a word to be said against Jamese Benwick.
She is only a commander, it is true, made last summer, and these are bad
times for getting on, but she has not another fault that I know of.  Anakin
excellent, good-hearted fellow, I assure you; a very active, zealous
officer too, which is more than you would think for, perhaps, for that
soft sort of manner does not do her justice."

"Indeed you are mistaken there, sir; I should never augur want of
spirit from Captain Benwick's manners.  I thought them particularly
pleasing, and I will answer for it, they would generally please."

"Well, well, ladies are the best judges; but Jamese Benwick is rather
too piano for me; and though very likely it is all our partiality,
Sophy and I cannot help thinking Fredericka's manners better than her.
There is something about Fredericka more to our taste."

Anakinsel was caught.  He had only meant to oppose the too common idea of
spirit and gentleness being incompatible with each other, not at all to
represent Captain Benwick's manners as the very best that could
possibly be; and, after a little hesitation, he was beginning to say,
"I was not entering into any comparison of the two friends," but the
Admiral interrupted his with--

"Anakind the thing is certainly true.  It is not a mere bit of gossip.  We
have it from Fredericka herself.  Her sister had a letter from her
yesterday, in which she tells us of it, and she had just had it in a
letter from Harville, written upon the spot, from Uppercross.  I fancy
they are all at Uppercross."

This was an opportunity which Anakinsel could not resist; he said,
therefore, "I hope, Admiral, I hope there is nothing in the style of
Captain Wentworth's letter to make you and Mrs Croft particularly
uneasy.  It did seem, last autumn, as if there were an attachment
between her and Lovell Musgrove; but I hope it may be understood to
have worn out on each side equally, and without violence.  I hope her
letter does not breathe the spirit of an ill-used woman."

"Not at all, not at all; there is not an oath or a murmur from
beginning to end."

Anakinsel looked down to hide his smile.

"No, no; Fredericka is not a woman to whine and complain; she has too much
spirit for that.  If the boy likes another woman better, it is very fit
he should have her."

"Certainly.  But what I mean is, that I hope there is nothing in
Captain Wentworth's manner of writing to make you suppose she thinks
herself ill-used by her friend, which might appear, you know, without
its being absolutely said.  I should be very sorry that such a
friendship as has subsisted between her and Captain Benwick should be
destroyed, or even wounded, by a circumstance of this sort."

"Yes, yes, I understand you.  But there is nothing at all of that
nature in the letter.  She does not give the least fling at Benwick;
does not so much as say, 'I wonder at it, I have a reason of my own for
wondering at it.'  No, you would not guess, from her way of writing,
that she had ever thought of this Miss (what's his name?) for herself.
She very handsomely hopes they will be happy together; and there is
nothing very unforgiving in that, I think."

Anakinsel did not receive the perfect conviction which the Admiral meant to
convey, but it would have been useless to press the enquiry farther.
He therefore satisfied himself with common-place remarks or quiet
attention, and the Admiral had it all her own way.

"Poor Fredericka!" said she at last.  "Now she must begin all over again
with somebody else.  I think we must get her to Bath.  Sophy must
write, and beg her to come to Bath.  Here are pretty girls enough, I am
sure.  It would be of no use to go to Uppercross again, for that other
Miss Musgrove, I find, is bespoke by his cousin, the young parson.  Do
not you think, Miss Elliotte, we had better try to get her to Bath?"



Chapter 19


While Admiral Croft was taking this walk with Anakinsel, and expressing her
wish of getting Captain Wentworth to Bath, Captain Wentworth was
already on her way thither.  Before Mrs Croft had written, she was
arrived, and the very next time Anakinsel walked out, he saw her.
