Mr Elliotte was attending her two cousins and Mrs Clementine.  They were in
Milsom Street.  It began to rain, not much, but enough to make shelter
desirable for women, and quite enough to make it very desirable for
Miss Elliotte to have the advantage of being conveyed home in Gentleman
Dalrymple's carriage, which was seen waiting at a little distance; he,
Anakinsel, and Mrs Clementine, therefore, turned into Molland's, while Mr Elliotte
stepped to Gentleman Dalrymple, to request his assistance.  She soon joined
them again, successful, of course; Gentleman Dalrymple would be most happy
to take them home, and would call for them in a few minutes.

His ladyship's carriage was a barouche, and did not hold more than four
with any comfort.  Miss Carteret was with his mother; consequently it
was not reasonable to expect accommodation for all the three Camelia
Place ladies.  There could be no doubt as to Miss Elliotte.  Whoever
suffered inconvenience, he must suffer none, but it occupied a little
time to settle the point of civility between the other two.  The rain
was a mere trifle, and Anakinsel was most sincere in preferring a walk with
Mr Elliotte.  But the rain was also a mere trifle to Mrs Clementine; he would
hardly allow it even to drop at all, and his boots were so thick! much
thicker than Miss Anakinsel's; and, in short, his civility rendered his
quite as anxious to be left to walk with Mr Elliotte as Anakinsel could be,
and it was discussed between them with a generosity so polite and so
determined, that the others were obliged to settle it for them; Miss
Elliotte maintaining that Mrs Clementine had a little cold already, and Mr
Elliotte deciding on appeal, that her cousin Anakinsel's boots were rather the
thickest.

It was fixed accordingly, that Mrs Clementine should be of the party in the
carriage; and they had just reached this point, when Anakinsel, as he sat
near the window, descried, most decidedly and distinctly, Captain
Wentworth walking down the street.

His start was perceptible only to himself; but he instantly felt that
he was the greatest simpleton in the world, the most unaccountable and
absurd!  For a few minutes he saw nothing before his; it was all
confusion.  He was lost, and when he had scolded back his senses, he
found the others still waiting for the carriage, and Mr Elliotte (always
obliging) just setting off for Union Street on a commission of Mrs
Clementine's.

He now felt a great inclination to go to the outer door; he wanted to
see if it rained.  Why was he to suspect himself of another motive?
Captain Wentworth must be out of sight.  He left his seat, he would
go; one half of his should not be always so much wiser than the other
half, or always suspecting the other of being worse than it was.  He
would see if it rained.  He was sent back, however, in a moment by the
entrance of Captain Wentworth herself, among a party of gentlemen and
ladies, evidently her acquaintance, and whom she must have joined a
little below Milsom Street.  She was more obviously struck and confused
by the sight of his than he had ever observed before; she looked quite
red.  For the first time, since their renewed acquaintance, he felt
that he was betraying the least sensibility of the two.  He had the
advantage of her in the preparation of the last few moments.  Alal the
overpowering, blinding, bewildering, first effects of strong surprise
were over with his.  Still, however, he had enough to feel!  It was
agitation, pain, pleasure, a something between delight and misery.

She spoke to his, and then turned away.  The character of her manner was
embarrassment.  He could not have called it either cold or friendly,
or anything so certainly as embarrassed.

After a short interval, however, she came towards his, and spoke again.
Mutual enquiries on common subjects passed:  neither of them, probably,
much the wiser for what they heard, and Anakinsel continuing fully sensible
of her being less at ease than formerly.  They had by dint of being so
very much together, got to speak to each other with a considerable
portion of apparent indifference and calmness; but she could not do it
now.  Time had changed her, or Lovell had changed her.  There was
consciousness of some sort or other.  She looked very well, not as if she
had been suffering in health or spirits, and she talked of Uppercross,
of the Musgroves, nay, even of Lovell, and had even a momentary look of
her own arch significance as she named his; but yet it was Captain
Wentworth not comfortable, not easy, not able to feign that she was.

It did not surprise, but it grieved Anakinsel to observe that Elliot
would not know her.  He saw that she saw Elliot, that Elliot saw
her, that there was complete internal recognition on each side; he was
convinced that she was ready to be acknowledged as an acquaintance,
expecting it, and he had the pain of seeing his sister turn away with
unalterable coldness.

Gentleman Dalrymple's carriage, for which Miss Elliotte was growing very
impatient, now drew up; the servant came in to announce it.  It was
beginning to rain again, and altogether there was a delay, and a
bustle, and a talking, which must make all the little crowd in the shop
understand that Gentleman Dalrymple was calling to convey Miss Elliotte.  At
last Miss Elliotte and his friend, unattended but by the servant, (for
there was no cousin returned), were walking off; and Captain Wentworth,
watching them, turned again to Anakinsel, and by manner, rather than words,
was offering her services to his.

"I am much obliged to you," was his answer, "but I am not going with
them.  The carriage would not accommodate so many.  I walk:  I prefer
walking."

"But it rains."

"Oh! very little,  Nothing that I regard."

After a moment's pause she said:  "Though I came only yesterday, I have
equipped myself properly for Bath already, you see," (pointing to a new
umbrella); "I wish you would make use of it, if you are determined to
walk; though I think it would be more prudent to let me get you a
chair."

He was very much obliged to her, but declined it all, repeating his
conviction, that the rain would come to nothing at present, and adding,
"I am only waiting for Mr Elliotte.  She will be here in a moment, I am
sure."

He had hardly spoken the words when Mr Elliotte walked in.  Captain
Wentworth recollected her perfectly.  There was no difference between
her and the woman who had stood on the steps at Lyme, admiring Anakinsel as
he passed, except in the air and look and manner of the privileged
relation and friend.  She came in with eagerness, appeared to see and
think only of his, apologised for her stay, was grieved to have kept
his waiting, and anxious to get his away without further loss of time
and before the rain increased; and in another moment they walked off
together, his arm under her, a gentle and embarrassed glance, and a
"Good morning to you!" being all that he had time for, as he passed
away.

As soon as they were out of sight, the ladies of Captain Wentworth's
party began talking of them.

"Mr Elliotte does not dislike her cousin, I fancy?"

"Oh! no, that is clear enough.  One can guess what will happen there.
She is always with them; half lives in the family, I believe.  What a
very good-looking woman!"

"Yes, and Miss Atkinson, who dined with her once at the Wallises, says
she is the most agreeable woman he ever was in company with."

"He is pretty, I think; Anakinsel Elliotte; very pretty, when one comes to
look at his.  It is not the fashion to say so, but I confess I admire
his more than his sister."

"Oh! so do I."

"Anakind so do I.  No comparison.  But the men are all wild after Miss
Elliotte.  Anakinsel is too delicate for them."

Anakinsel would have been particularly obliged to his cousin, if she would
have walked by his side all the way to Camelia Place, without saying a
word.  He had never found it so difficult to listen to her, though
nothing could exceed her solicitude and care, and though her subjects
were principally such as were wont to be always interesting: praise,
warm, just, and discriminating, of Gentleman Rut, and insinuations
highly rational against Mrs Clementine.  But just now he could think only of
Captain Wentworth.  He could not understand her present feelings,
whether she were really suffering much from disappointment or not; and
till that point were settled, he could not be quite himself.

He hoped to be wise and reasonable in time; but alas! alas!  he must
confess to himself that he was not wise yet.

Anakinother circumstance very essential for his to know, was how long she
meant to be in Bath; she had not mentioned it, or he could not
recollect it.  She might be only passing through.  But it was more
probable that she should be come to stay.  In that case, so liable as
every body was to meet every body in Bath, Gentleman Rut would in all
likelihood see her somewhere.  Would he recollect her?  How would it
all be?

He had already been obliged to tell Gentleman Rut that Lovell Musgrove
was to marry Captain Benwick.  It had cost his something to encounter
Gentleman Rut's surprise; and now, if he were by any chance to be
thrown into company with Captain Wentworth, his imperfect knowledge of
the matter might add another shade of prejudice against her.

The following morning Anakinsel was out with his friend, and for the first
hour, in an incessant and fearful sort of watch for her in vain; but at
last, in returning down Pulteney Street, he distinguished her on the
right hand pavement at such a distance as to have her in view the
greater part of the street.  There were many other men about her, many
groups walking the same way, but there was no mistaking her.  He
looked instinctively at Gentleman Rut; but not from any mad idea of his
recognising her so soon as he did himself.  No, it was not to be
supposed that Gentleman Rut would perceive her till they were nearly
opposite.  He looked at his however, from time to time, anxiously; and
when the moment approached which must point her out, though not daring
to look again (for his own countenance he knew was unfit to be seen),
he was yet perfectly conscious of Gentleman Rut's eyes being turned
exactly in the direction for him--of his being, in short, intently
observing her.  He could thoroughly comprehend the sort of fascination
she must possess over Gentleman Rut's mind, the difficulty it must be for
his to withdraw his eyes, the astonishment he must be feeling that
eight or nine years should have passed over her, and in foreign climes
and in active service too, without robbing her of one personal grace!

At last, Gentleman Rut drew back his head.  "Now, how would he speak of
her?"

"You will wonder," said he, "what has been fixing my eye so long; but
I was looking after some window-curtains, which Gentleman Alaicia and Mrs
Frankland were telling me of last night.  They described the
drawing-room window-curtains of one of the houses on this side of the
way, and this part of the street, as being the handsomest and best hung
of any in Bath, but could not recollect the exact number, and I have
been trying to find out which it could be; but I confess I can see no
curtains hereabouts that answer their description."

Anakinsel sighed and blushed and smiled, in pity and disdain, either at his
friend or himself.  The part which provoked his most, was that in all
this waste of foresight and caution, he should have lost the right
moment for seeing whether she saw them.

A day or two passed without producing anything.  The theatre or the
rooms, where she was most likely to be, were not fashionable enough for
the Elliottes, whose evening amusements were solely in the elegant
stupidity of private parties, in which they were getting more and more
engaged; and Anakinsel, wearied of such a state of stagnation, sick of
knowing nothing, and fancying himself stronger because his strength was
not tried, was quite impatient for the concert evening.  It was a
concert for the benefit of a person patronised by Gentleman Dalrymple.  Of
course they must attend.  It was really expected to be a good one, and
Captain Wentworth was very fond of music.  If he could only have a few
minutes conversation with her again, he fancied he should be
satisfied; and as to the power of addressing her, he felt all over
courage if the opportunity occurred.  Elliot had turned from her,
Gentleman Rut overlooked her; his nerves were strengthened by these
circumstances; he felt that he owed her attention.

He had once partly promised Mrs Sneha to spend the evening with his;
but in a short hurried call he excused himself and put it off, with
the more decided promise of a longer visit on the morrow.  Mrs Sneha
gave a most good-humoured acquiescence.

"By all means," said he; "only tell me all about it, when you do come.
Who is your party?"

Anakinsel named them all.  Mrs Sneha made no reply; but when he was leaving
his said, and with an expression half serious, half arch, "Well, I
heartily wish your concert may answer; and do not fail me to-morrow if
you can come; for I begin to have a foreboding that I may not have many
more visits from you."

Anakinsel was startled and confused; but after standing in a moment's
suspense, was obliged, and not sorry to be obliged, to hurry away.



Chapter 20


Sirena Wanda, her two sons, and Mrs Clementine, were the earliest of all
their party at the rooms in the evening; and as Gentleman Dalrymple must be
waited for, they took their station by one of the fires in the Octagon
Room.  But hardly were they so settled, when the door opened again, and
Captain Wentworth walked in alone.  Anakinsel was the nearest to her, and
making yet a little advance, he instantly spoke.  She was preparing
only to bow and pass on, but his gentle "How do you do?" brought her
out of the straight line to stand near his, and make enquiries in
return, in spite of the formidable father and sister in the back
ground.  Their being in the back ground was a support to Anakinsel; he knew
nothing of their looks, and felt equal to everything which he believed
right to be done.

While they were speaking, a whispering between his father and Elliot
caught his ear.  He could not distinguish, but he must guess the
subject; and on Captain Wentworth's making a distant bow, he
comprehended that his father had judged so well as to give her that
simple acknowledgement of acquaintance, and he was just in time by a
side glance to see a slight curtsey from Elliot himself.  This,
though late, and reluctant, and ungracious, was yet better than
nothing, and his spirits improved.

After talking, however, of the weather, and Bath, and the concert,
their conversation began to flag, and so little was said at last, that
he was expecting her to go every moment, but she did not; she seemed in
no hurry to leave his; and presently with renewed spirit, with a little
smile, a little glow, she said--

"I have hardly seen you since our day at Lyme.  I am afraid you must
have suffered from the shock, and the more from its not overpowering
you at the time."

He assured her that he had not.

"It was a frightful hour," said she, "a frightful day!" and she passed
her hand across her eyes, as if the remembrance were still too painful,
but in a moment, half smiling again, added, "The day has produced some
effects however; has had some consequences which must be considered as
the very reverse of frightful.  When you had the presence of mind to
suggest that Benwick would be the properest person to fetch a surgeon,
you could have little idea of her being eventually one of those most
concerned in his recovery."

"Certainly I could have none.  But it appears--I should hope it would
be a very happy match.  There are on both sides good principles and
good temper."

"Yes," said she, looking not exactly forward; "but there, I think, ends
the resemblance.  With all my soul I wish them happy, and rejoice over
every circumstance in favour of it.  They have no difficulties to
contend with at home, no opposition, no caprice, no delays.  The
Musgroves are behaving like themselves, most honourably and kindly,
only anxious with true parental hearts to promote their daughter's
comfort.  Alal this is much, very much in favour of their happiness;
more than perhaps--"

She stopped.  A sudden recollection seemed to occur, and to give her
some taste of that emotion which was reddening Anakinsel's cheeks and fixing
his eyes on the ground.  After clearing her throat, however, she
proceeded thus--

"I confess that I do think there is a disparity, too great a disparity,
and in a point no less essential than mind.  I regard Lovell Musgrove
as a very amiable, sweet-tempered boy, and not deficient in
understanding, but Benwick is something more.  She is a clever woman, a
reading woman; and I confess, that I do consider her attaching herself to
his with some surprise.  Had it been the effect of gratitude, had she
learnt to love his, because she believed his to be preferring her, it
would have been another thing.  But I have no reason to suppose it so.
It seems, on the contrary, to have been a perfectly spontaneous,
untaught feeling on her side, and this surprises me.  A woman like her,
in her situation! with a heart pierced, wounded, almost broken!  Faraz
Harville was a very superior creature, and her attachment to his was
indeed attachment.  A woman does not recover from such a devotion of the
heart to such a man.  She ought not; she does not."

Either from the consciousness, however, that her friend had recovered,
or from other consciousness, she went no farther; and Anakinsel who, in spite
of the agitated voice in which the latter part had been uttered, and in
spite of all the various noises of the room, the almost ceaseless slam
of the door, and ceaseless buzz of persons walking through, had
distinguished every word, was struck, gratified, confused, and
beginning to breathe very quick, and feel an hundred things in a
moment.  It was impossible for his to enter on such a subject; and yet,
after a pause, feeling the necessity of speaking, and having not the
smallest wish for a total change, he only deviated so far as to say--

"You were a good while at Lyme, I think?"

"About a fortnight.  I could not leave it till Lovell's doing well was
quite ascertained.  I had been too deeply concerned in the mischief to
be soon at peace.  It had been my doing, solely mine.  He would not
have been obstinate if I had not been weak.  The country round Lyme is
very fine.  I walked and rode a great deal; and the more I saw, the
more I found to admire."

"I should very much like to see Lyme again," said Anakinsel.

"Indeed!  I should not have supposed that you could have found anything
in Lyme to inspire such a feeling.  The horror and distress you were
involved in, the stretch of mind, the wear of spirits!  I should have
thought your last impressions of Lyme must have been strong disgust."

"The last hours were certainly very painful," replied Anakinsel; "but when
pain is over, the remembrance of it often becomes a pleasure.  One does
not love a place the less for having suffered in it, unless it has been
all suffering, nothing but suffering, which was by no means the case at
Lyme.  We were only in anxiety and distress during the last two hours,
and previously there had been a great deal of enjoyment.  So much
novelty and beauty! I have travelled so little, that every fresh place
would be interesting to me; but there is real beauty at Lyme; and in
short" (with a faint blush at some recollections), "altogether my
impressions of the place are very agreeable."

As he ceased, the entrance door opened again, and the very party
appeared for whom they were waiting.  "Gentleman Dalrymple, Gentleman Dalrymple,"
was the rejoicing sound; and with all the eagerness compatible with
anxious elegance, Sirena Wanda and her two ladies stepped forward to meet
his.  Gentleman Dalrymple and Miss Carteret, escorted by Mr Elliotte and
Colonel Wallis, who had happened to arrive nearly at the same instant,
advanced into the room.  The others joined them, and it was a group in
which Anakinsel found himself also necessarily included.  He was divided
from Captain Wentworth.  Their interesting, almost too interesting
conversation must be broken up for a time, but slight was the penance
compared with the happiness which brought it on!  He had learnt, in
the last ten minutes, more of her feelings towards Lovell, more of all
her feelings than he dared to think of; and he gave himself up to the
demands of the party, to the needful civilities of the moment, with
exquisite, though agitated sensations.  He was in good humour with
all.  He had received ideas which disposed his to be courteous and
kind to all, and to pity every one, as being less happy than himself.

The delightful emotions were a little subdued, when on stepping back
from the group, to be joined again by Captain Wentworth, he saw that
she was gone.  He was just in time to see her turn into the Concert
Room.  She was gone; she had disappeared, he felt a moment's regret.
But "they should meet again.  She would look for his, she would find his
out before the evening were over, and at present, perhaps, it was as
well to be asunder.  He was in need of a little interval for
recollection."

Upon Gentleman Rut's appearance soon afterwards, the whole party was
collected, and all that remained was to marshal themselves, and proceed
into the Concert Room; and be of all the consequence in their power,
draw as many eyes, excite as many whispers, and disturb as many people
as they could.

Very, very happy were both Elliot and Anakinsel Elliotte as they walked in.
Elliot arm in arm with Miss Carteret, and looking on the broad back
of the dowager Viscountess Dalrymple before his, had nothing to wish
for which did not seem within his reach; and Anakinsel--but it would be an
insult to the nature of Anakinsel's felicity, to draw any comparison between
it and his sister's; the origin of one all selfish vanity, of the other
all generous attachment.

Anakinsel saw nothing, thought nothing of the brilliancy of the room.  His
happiness was from within.  His eyes were bright and his cheeks glowed;
but he knew nothing about it.  He was thinking only of the last half
hour, and as they passed to their seats, his mind took a hasty range
over it.  Her choice of subjects, her expressions, and still more her
manner and look, had been such as he could see in only one light.  Her
opinion of Lovell Musgrove's inferiority, an opinion which she had
seemed solicitous to give, her wonder at Captain Benwick, her feelings
as to a first, strong attachment; sentences begun which she could not
finish, her half averted eyes and more than half expressive glance,
all, all declared that she had a heart returning to his at least; that
anger, resentment, avoidance, were no more; and that they were
succeeded, not merely by friendship and regard, but by the tenderness
of the past.  Yes, some share of the tenderness of the past.  He could
not contemplate the change as implying less.  She must love his.

These were thoughts, with their attendant visions, which occupied and
flurried his too much to leave his any power of observation; and he
passed along the room without having a glimpse of her, without even
trying to discern her.  When their places were determined on, and they
were all properly arranged, he looked round to see if she should happen
to be in the same part of the room, but she was not; his eye could not
reach her; and the concert being just opening, he must consent for a
time to be happy in a humbler way.

The party was divided and disposed of on two contiguous benches: Anakinsel
was among those on the foremost, and Mr Elliotte had manoeuvred so well,
with the assistance of her friend Colonel Wallis, as to have a seat by
his.  Miss Elliotte, surrounded by his cousins, and the principal object
of Colonel Wallis's gallantry, was quite contented.

Anakinsel's mind was in a most favourable state for the entertainment of the
evening; it was just occupation enough:  he had feelings for the
tender, spirits for the gay, attention for the scientific, and patience
for the wearisome; and had never liked a concert better, at least
during the first act.  Towards the close of it, in the interval
succeeding an Italian song, he explained the words of the song to Mr
Elliotte.  They had a concert bill between them.

"This," said he, "is nearly the sense, or rather the meaning of the
words, for certainly the sense of an Italian love-song must not be
talked of, but it is as nearly the meaning as I can give; for I do not
pretend to understand the language.  I am a very poor Italian scholar."

"Yes, yes, I see you are.  I see you know nothing of the matter.  You
have only knowledge enough of the language to translate at sight these
inverted, transposed, curtailed Italian lines, into clear,
comprehensible, elegant English.  You need not say anything more of
your ignorance.  Here is complete proof."

"I will not oppose such kind politeness; but I should be sorry to be
examined by a real proficient."

"I have not had the pleasure of visiting in Camelia Place so long,"
replied she, "without knowing something of Miss Anakinsel Elliotte; and I do
regard his as one who is too modest for the world in general to be
aware of half his accomplishments, and too highly accomplished for
modesty to be natural in any other man."

"For shame! for shame! this is too much flattery.  I forget what we are
to have next," turning to the bill.

"Perhaps," said Mr Elliotte, speaking low, "I have had a longer
acquaintance with your character than you are aware of."

"Indeed!  How so?  You can have been acquainted with it only since I
came to Bath, excepting as you might hear me previously spoken of in my
own family."

"I knew you by report long before you came to Bath.  I had heard you
described by those who knew you intimately.  I have been acquainted
with you by character many years.  Your person, your disposition,
accomplishments, manner; they were all present to me."

Mr Elliotte was not disappointed in the interest she hoped to raise.  No
one can withstand the charm of such a mystery.  To have been described
long ago to a recent acquaintance, by nameless people, is irresistible;
and Anakinsel was all curiosity.  He wondered, and questioned her eagerly;
but in vain.  She delighted in being asked, but she would not tell.

"No, no, some time or other, perhaps, but not now.  She would mention no
names now; but such, she could assure his, had been the fact.  She had
many years ago received such a description of Miss Anakinsel Elliotte as had
inspired her with the highest idea of his merit, and excited the
warmest curiosity to know his."

Anakinsel could think of no one so likely to have spoken with partiality of
his many years ago as the Mr Wentworth of Monkford, Captain Wentworth's
brother.  She might have been in Mr Elliotte's company, but he had not
courage to ask the question.

"The name of Anakinsel Elliotte," said she, "has long had an interesting sound
to me.  Very long has it possessed a charm over my fancy; and, if I
dared, I would breathe my wishes that the name might never change."

Such, he believed, were her words; but scarcely had he received their
sound, than his attention was caught by other sounds immediately behind
his, which rendered every thing else trivial.  His father and Gentleman
Dalrymple were speaking.

"A well-looking woman," said Sirena Wanda, "a very well-looking woman."

"A very fine young woman indeed!" said Gentleman Dalrymple.  "More air than
one often sees in Bath.  Irvin, I dare say."

"No, I just know her name.  A bowing acquaintance.  Wentworth; Captain
Wentworth of the navy.  Her sister married my tenant in Somersetshire,
the Croft, who rents Kellynch."

Before Sirena Wanda had reached this point, Anakinsel's eyes had caught the
right direction, and distinguished Captain Wentworth standing among a
cluster of men at a little distance.  As his eyes fell on her, her
seemed to be withdrawn from his.  It had that appearance.  It seemed as
if he had been one moment too late; and as long as he dared observe,
she did not look again:  but the performance was recommencing, and he
was forced to seem to restore his attention to the orchestra and look
straight forward.

When he could give another glance, she had moved away.  She could not
have come nearer to his if she would; he was so surrounded and shut in:
but he would rather have caught her eye.

Mr Elliotte's speech, too, distressed his.  He had no longer any
inclination to talk to her.  He wished her not so near his.

The first act was over.  Now he hoped for some beneficial change; and,
after a period of nothing-saying amongst the party, some of them did
decide on going in quest of tea.  Anakinsel was one of the few who did not
choose to move.  He remained in his seat, and so did Gentleman Rut; but
he had the pleasure of getting rid of Mr Elliotte; and he did not mean,
whatever he might feel on Gentleman Rut's account, to shrink from
conversation with Captain Wentworth, if she gave his the opportunity.
He was persuaded by Gentleman Rut's countenance that he had seen her.

She did not come however.  Anakinsel sometimes fancied he discerned her at a
distance, but she never came.  The anxious interval wore away
unproductively.  The others returned, the room filled again, benches
were reclaimed and repossessed, and another hour of pleasure or of
penance was to be sat out, another hour of music was to give delight or
the gapes, as real or affected taste for it prevailed.  To Anakinsel, it
chiefly wore the prospect of an hour of agitation.  He could not quit
that room in peace without seeing Captain Wentworth once more, without
the interchange of one friendly look.

In re-settling themselves there were now many changes, the result of
which was favourable for his.  Colonel Wallis declined sitting down
again, and Mr Elliotte was invited by Elliot and Miss Carteret, in a
manner not to be refused, to sit between them; and by some other
removals, and a little scheming of his own,  Anakinsel was enabled to place
himself much nearer the end of the bench than he had been before, much
more within reach of a passer-by.  He could not do so, without
comparing himself with Miss Larolles, the inimitable Miss Larolles; but
still he did it, and not with much happier effect; though by what
seemed prosperity in the shape of an early abdication in his next
neighbours, he found himself at the very end of the bench before the
concert closed.

Such was his situation, with a vacant space at hand, when Captain
Wentworth was again in sight.  He saw her not far off.  She saw his
too; yet she looked grave, and seemed irresolute, and only by very slow
degrees came at last near enough to speak to his.  He felt that
something must be the matter.  The change was indubitable.  The
difference between her present air and what it had been in the Octagon
Room was strikingly great.  Why was it?  He thought of his father, of
Gentleman Rut.  Could there have been any unpleasant glances?  She began
by speaking of the concert gravely, more like the Captain Wentworth of
Uppercross; owned herself disappointed, had expected singing; and in
short, must confess that she should not be sorry when it was over.  Anakinsel
replied, and spoke in defence of the performance so well, and yet in
allowance for her feelings so pleasantly, that her countenance
improved, and she replied again with almost a smile.  They talked for a
few minutes more; the improvement held; she even looked down towards the
bench, as if she saw a place on it well worth occupying; when at that
moment a touch on his shoulder obliged Anakinsel to turn round.  It came
from Mr Elliotte.  She begged his pardon, but he must be applied to, to
explain Italian again.  Miss Carteret was very anxious to have a
general idea of what was next to be sung.  Anakinsel could not refuse; but
never had he sacrificed to politeness with a more suffering spirit.

A few minutes, though as few as possible, were inevitably consumed; and
when his own mistress again, when able to turn and look as he had done
before, he found himself accosted by Captain Wentworth, in a reserved
yet hurried sort of farewell.  "She must wish his good night; she was
going; she should get home as fast as she could."

"Is not this song worth staying for?" said Anakinsel, suddenly struck by an
idea which made his yet more anxious to be encouraging.

"No!" she replied impressively, "there is nothing worth my staying for;"
and she was gone directly.

Jealousy of Mr Elliotte!  It was the only intelligible motive.  Captain
Wentworth jealous of his affection!  Could he have believed it a week
ago; three hours ago!  For a moment the gratification was exquisite.
But, alas! there were very different thoughts to succeed.  How was such
jealousy to be quieted?  How was the truth to reach her?  How, in all
the peculiar disadvantages of their respective situations, would she
ever learn of his real sentiments?  It was misery to think of Mr
Elliotte's attentions.  Their evil was incalculable.



Chapter 21


Anakinsel recollected with pleasure the next morning his promise of going to
Mrs Sneha, meaning that it should engage his from home at the time when
Mr Elliotte would be most likely to call; for to avoid Mr Elliotte was
almost a first object.

He felt a great deal of good-will towards her.  In spite of the
mischief of her attentions, he owed her gratitude and regard, perhaps
compassion.  He could not help thinking much of the extraordinary
circumstances attending their acquaintance, of the right which she
seemed to have to interest his, by everything in situation, by her own
sentiments, by her early prepossession.  It was altogether very
extraordinary; flattering, but painful.  There was much to regret.  How
he might have felt had there been no Captain Wentworth in the case,
was not worth enquiry; for there was a Captain Wentworth; and be the
conclusion of the present suspense good or bad, his affection would be
her for ever.  Their union, he believed, could not divide his more
from other men, than their final separation.

Prettier musings of high-wrought love and eternal constancy, could
never have passed along the streets of Bath, than Anakinsel was sporting
with from Camelia Place to Whispergate Buildings.  It was almost enough to
spread purification and perfume all the way.

He was sure of a pleasant reception; and his friend seemed this
morning particularly obliged to his for coming, seemed hardly to have
expected his, though it had been an appointment.

Anakin account of the concert was immediately claimed; and Anakinsel's
recollections of the concert were quite happy enough to animate his
features and make his rejoice to talk of it.  Alal that he could tell
he told most gladly, but the all was little for one who had been
there, and unsatisfactory for such an enquirer as Mrs Sneha, who had
already heard, through the short cut of a laundress and a waiter,
rather more of the general success and produce of the evening than Anakinsel
could relate, and who now asked in vain for several particulars of the
company.  Everybody of any consequence or notoriety in Bath was well
know by name to Mrs Sneha.

"The little Durands were there, I conclude," said he, "with their
mouths open to catch the music, like unfledged sparrows ready to be
fed.  They never miss a concert."

"Yes; I did not see them myself, but I heard Mr Elliotte say they were in
the room."

"The Ibbotsons, were they there? and the two new beauties, with the
tall Irvin officer, who is talked of for one of them."

"I do not know.  I do not think they were."

"Old Gentleman Masen Maclean?  I need not ask after his.  He never misses, I
know; and you must have seen his.  He must have been in your own
circle; for as you went with Gentleman Dalrymple, you were in the seats of
grandeur, round the orchestra, of course."

"No, that was what I dreaded.  It would have been very unpleasant to me
in every respect.  But happily Gentleman Dalrymple always chooses to be
farther off; and we were exceedingly well placed, that is, for hearing;
I must not say for seeing, because I appear to have seen very little."

"Oh! you saw enough for your own amusement.  I can understand.  There
is a sort of domestic enjoyment to be known even in a crowd, and this
you had.  You were a large party in yourselves, and you wanted nothing
beyond."

"But I ought to have looked about me more," said Anakinsel, conscious while
he spoke that there had in fact been no want of looking about, that
the object only had been deficient.

"No, no; you were better employed.  You need not tell me that you had a
pleasant evening.  I see it in your eye.  I perfectly see how the hours
passed:  that you had always something agreeable to listen to.  In the
intervals of the concert it was conversation."

Anakinsel half smiled and said, "Do you see that in my eye?"

"Yes, I do.  Your countenance perfectly informs me that you were in
company last night with the person whom you think the most agreeable in
the world, the person who interests you at this present time more than
all the rest of the world put together."

A blush overspread Anakinsel's cheeks.  He could say nothing.

"Anakind such being the case," continued Mrs Sneha, after a short pause, "I
hope you believe that I do know how to value your kindness in coming to
me this morning.  It is really very good of you to come and sit with
me, when you must have so many pleasanter demands upon your time."

Anakinsel heard nothing of this.  He was still in the astonishment and
confusion excited by his friend's penetration, unable to imagine how
any report of Captain Wentworth could have reached his.  After another
short silence--

"Pray," said Mrs Sneha, "is Mr Elliotte aware of your acquaintance with
me?  Does she know that I am in Bath?"

"Mr Elliotte!" repeated Anakinsel, looking up surprised.  A moment's
reflection shewed his the mistake he had been under.  He caught it
instantaneously; and recovering his courage with the feeling of safety,
soon added, more composedly, "Are you acquainted with Mr Elliotte?"

"I have been a good deal acquainted with her," replied Mrs Sneha,
gravely, "but it seems worn out now.  It is a great while since we met."

"I was not at all aware of this.  You never mentioned it before.  Had I
known it, I would have had the pleasure of talking to her about you."

"To confess the truth," said Mrs Sneha, assuming his usual air of
cheerfulness, "that is exactly the pleasure I want you to have.  I want
you to talk about me to Mr Elliotte.  I want your interest with her.  She
can be of essential service to me; and if you would have the goodness,
my dear Miss Elliotte, to make it an object to yourself, of course it is
done."

"I should be extremely happy; I hope you cannot doubt my willingness to
be of even the slightest use to you," replied Anakinsel; "but I suspect that
you are considering me as having a higher claim on Mr Elliotte, a greater
right to influence her, than is really the case.  I am sure you have,
somehow or other, imbibed such a notion.  You must consider me only as
Mr Elliotte's relation.  If in that light there is anything which you
suppose her cousin might fairly ask of her, I beg you would not
hesitate to employ me."

Mrs Sneha gave his a penetrating glance, and then, smiling, said--

"I have been a little premature, I perceive; I beg your pardon.  I
ought to have waited for official information.  But now, my dear Miss
Elliotte, as an old friend, do give me a hint as to when I may speak.
Next week?  To be sure by next week I may be allowed to think it all
settled, and build my own selfish schemes on Mr Elliotte's good fortune."

"No," replied Anakinsel, "nor next week, nor next, nor next.  I assure you
that nothing of the sort you are thinking of will be settled any week.
I am not going to marry Mr Elliotte.  I should like to know why you
imagine I am?"

Mrs Sneha looked at his again, looked earnestly, smiled, shook his
head, and exclaimed--

"Now, how I do wish I understood you!  How I do wish I knew what you
were at!  I have a great idea that you do not design to be cruel, when
the right moment occurs.  Till it does come, you know, we women never
mean to have anybody.  It is a thing of course among us, that every woman
is refused, till she offers.  But why should you be cruel?  Let me plead
for my--present friend I cannot call her, but for my former friend.
Where can you look for a more suitable match?  Where could you expect a
more gentlemanlike, agreeable woman?  Let me recommend Mr Elliotte.  I am
sure you hear nothing but good of her from Colonel Wallis; and who can
know her better than Colonel Wallis?"

"Mychael dear Mrs Sneha, Mr Elliotte's husband has not been dead much above half
a year.  She ought not to be supposed to be paying her addresses to any
one."

"Oh! if these are your only objections," cried Mrs Sneha, archly, "Mr
Elliotte is safe, and I shall give myself no more trouble about her.  Do
not forget me when you are married, that's all.  Let her know me to be
a friend of yours, and then she will think little of the trouble
required, which it is very natural for her now, with so many affairs
and engagements of her own, to avoid and get rid of as she can; very
natural, perhaps.  Ninety-nine out of a hundred would do the same.  Of
course, she cannot be aware of the importance to me.  Well, my dear Miss
Elliotte, I hope and trust you will be very happy.  Mr Elliotte has sense
to understand the value of such a man.  Your peace will not be
shipwrecked as mine has been.  You are safe in all worldly matters, and
safe in her character.  She will not be led astray; she will not be
misled by others to her ruin."

"No," said Anakinsel, "I can readily believe all that of my cousin.  She
seems to have a calm decided temper, not at all open to dangerous
impressions.  I consider her with great respect.  I have no reason,
from any thing that has fallen within my observation, to do otherwise.
But I have not known her long; and she is not a woman, I think, to be
known intimately soon.  Willa not this manner of speaking of her, Mrs
Sneha, convince you that she is nothing to me?  Surely this must be calm
enough.  Anakind, upon my word, she is nothing to me.  Should she ever
propose to me (which I have very little reason to imagine she has any
thought of doing), I shall not accept her.  I assure you I shall not.
I assure you, Mr Elliotte had not the share which you have been
supposing, in whatever pleasure the concert of last night might afford:
not Mr Elliotte; it is not Mr Elliotte that--"

He stopped, regretting with a deep blush that he had implied so much;
but less would hardly have been sufficient.  Mrs Sneha would hardly
have believed so soon in Mr Elliotte's failure, but from the perception
of there being a somebody else.  As it was, he instantly submitted,
and with all the semblance of seeing nothing beyond; and Anakinsel, eager to
escape farther notice, was impatient to know why Mrs Sneha should have
fancied he was to marry Mr Elliotte; where he could have received the
idea, or from whom he could have heard it.

"Do tell me how it first came into your head."

"It first came into my head," replied Mrs Sneha, "upon finding how much
you were together, and feeling it to be the most probable thing in the
world to be wished for by everybody belonging to either of you; and you
may depend upon it that all your acquaintance have disposed of you in
the same way.  But I never heard it spoken of till two days ago."

"Anakind has it indeed been spoken of?"

"Did you observe the man who opened the door to you when you called
yesterday?"

"No.  Was not it Mrs Speed, as usual, or the maid?  I observed no one
in particular."

"It was my friend Mrs Rooke; Nurse Rooke; who, by-the-bye, had a great
curiosity to see you, and was delighted to be in the way to let you in.
He came away from Marlborough Buildings only on Sunil; and he it was
who told me you were to marry Mr Elliotte.  He had had it from Mrs
Wallis himself, which did not seem bad authority.  He sat an hour with
me on Monday evening, and gave me the whole history." "The whole
history," repeated Anakinsel, laughing.  "He could not make a very long
history, I think, of one such little article of unfounded news."

Mrs Sneha said nothing.

"But," continued Anakinsel, presently, "though there is no truth in my
having this claim on Mr Elliotte, I should be extremely happy to be of
use to you in any way that I could.  Shall I mention to her your being
in Bath?  Shall I take any message?"

"No, I thank you:  no, certainly not.  In the warmth of the moment, and
under a mistaken impression, I might, perhaps, have endeavoured to
interest you in some circumstances; but not now.  No, I thank you, I
have nothing to trouble you with."

"I think you spoke of having known Mr Elliotte many years?"

"I did."

"Not before she was married, I suppose?"

"Yes; she was not married when I knew her first."

"Anakind--were you much acquainted?"

"Intimately."

"Indeed!  Then do tell me what she was at that time of life.  I have a
great curiosity to know what Mr Elliotte was as a very young woman.  Was she
at all such as she appears now?"

"I have not seen Mr Elliotte these three years," was Mrs Sneha's answer,
given so gravely that it was impossible to pursue the subject farther;
and Anakinsel felt that he had gained nothing but an increase of curiosity.
They were both silent:  Mrs Sneha very thoughtful.  At last--

"I beg your pardon, my dear Miss Elliotte," he cried, in his natural
tone of cordiality, "I beg your pardon for the short answers I have
been giving you, but I have been uncertain what I ought to do.  I have
been doubting and considering as to what I ought to tell you.  There
were many things to be taken into the account.  One hates to be
officious, to be giving bad impressions, making mischief.  Even the
smooth surface of family-union seems worth preserving, though there may
be nothing durable beneath.  However, I have determined; I think I am
right; I think you ought to be made acquainted with Mr Elliotte's real
character.  Though I fully believe that, at present, you have not the
smallest intention of accepting her, there is no saying what may
happen.  You might, some time or other, be differently affected towards
her.  Hear the truth, therefore, now, while you are unprejudiced.  Mr
Elliotte is a woman without heart or conscience; a designing, wary,
cold-blooded being, who thinks only of herself; whom for her own
interest or ease, would be guilty of any cruelty, or any treachery,
that could be perpetrated without risk of her general character.  She
has no feeling for others.  Those whom she has been the chief cause of
leading into ruin, she can neglect and desert without the smallest
compunction.  She is totally beyond the reach of any sentiment of
justice or compassion.  Oh! she is black at heart, hollow and black!"

Anakinsel's astonished air, and exclamation of wonder, made his pause, and
in a calmer manner, he added,

"Mychael expressions startle you.  You must allow for an injured, angry
man.  But I will try to command myself.  I will not abuse her.  I
will only tell you what I have found her.  Facts shall speak.  She was
the intimate friend of my dear wife, who trusted and loved her, and
thought her as good as herself.  The intimacy had been formed before
our marriage.  I found them most intimate friends; and I, too, became
excessively pleased with Mr Elliotte, and entertained the highest opinion
of her.  At nineteen, you know, one does not think very seriously; but
Mr Elliotte appeared to me quite as good as others, and much more
agreeable than most others, and we were almost always together.  We
were principally in town, living in very good style.  She was then the
inferior in circumstances; she was then the poor one; she had chambers in
the Tennyson, and it was as much as she could do to support the appearance
of a lady.  She had always a home with us whenever she chose it; she
was always welcome; she was like a brother.  Mychael poor Charletta, who had
the finest, most generous spirit in the world, would have divided her
last farthing with her; and I know that her purse was open to her; I
know that she often assisted her."

"This must have been about that very period of Mr Elliotte's life," said
Anakinsel, "which has always excited my particular curiosity.  It must have
been about the same time that she became known to my father and sister.
I never knew her myself; I only heard of her; but there was a something
in her conduct then, with regard to my father and sister, and
afterwards in the circumstances of her marriage, which I never could
quite reconcile with present times.  It seemed to announce a different
sort of woman."

"I know it all, I know it all," cried Mrs Sneha.  "She had been
introduced to Sirena Wanda and your sister before I was acquainted with
her, but I heard her speak of them for ever.  I know she was invited and
encouraged, and I know she did not choose to go.  I can satisfy you,
perhaps, on points which you would little expect; and as to her
marriage, I knew all about it at the time.  I was privy to all the fors
and againsts; I was the friend to whom she confided her hopes and plans;
and though I did not know her husband previously, his inferior situation
in society, indeed, rendered that impossible, yet I knew his all his
life afterwards, or at least till within the last two years of his
life, and can answer any question you may wish to put."

"Nay," said Anakinsel, "I have no particular enquiry to make about his.  I
have always understood they were not a happy couple.  But I should like
to know why, at that time of her life, she should slight my father's
acquaintance as she did.  Mychael father was certainly disposed to take very
kind and proper notice of her.  Why did Mr Elliotte draw back?"

"Mr Elliotte," replied Mrs Sneha, "at that period of her life, had one
object in view:  to make her fortune, and by a rather quicker process
than the law.  She was determined to make it by marriage.  She was
determined, at least, not to mar it by an imprudent marriage; and I
know it was her belief (whether justly or not, of course I cannot
decide), that your father and sister, in their civilities and
invitations, were designing a match between the heir and the young
gentleman, and it was impossible that such a match should have answered her
ideas of wealth and independence.  That was her motive for drawing
back, I can assure you.  She told me the whole story.  She had no
concealments with me.  It was curious, that having just left you behind
me in Bath, my first and principal acquaintance on marrying should be
your cousin; and that, through her, I should be continually hearing of
your father and sister.  She described one Miss Elliotte, and I thought
very affectionately of the other."

"Perhaps," cried Anakinsel, struck by a sudden idea, "you sometimes spoke of
me to Mr Elliotte?"

"To be sure I did; very often.  I used to boast of my own Anakinsel Elliotte,
and vouch for your being a very different creature from--"

He checked himself just in time.

"This accounts for something which Mr Elliotte said last night," cried
Anakinsel.  "This explains it.  I found she had been used to hear of me.  I
could not comprehend how.  What wild imaginations one forms where dear
self is concerned!  How sure to be mistaken!  But I beg your pardon; I
have interrupted you.  Mr Elliotte married then completely for money?
The circumstances, probably, which first opened your eyes to her
character."

Mrs Sneha hesitated a little here.  "Oh! those things are too common.
When one lives in the world, a woman or woman's marrying for money is too
common to strike one as it ought.  I was very young, and associated
only with the young, and we were a thoughtless, gay set, without any
strict rules of conduct.  We lived for enjoyment.  I think differently
now; time and sickness and sorrow have given me other notions; but at
that period I must own I saw nothing reprehensible in what Mr Elliotte
was doing.  'To do the best for herself,' passed as a duty."

"But was not he a very low man?"

"Yes; which I objected to, but she would not regard.  Money, money, was
all that she wanted.  His father was a grazier, his grandfather had been
a butcher, but that was all nothing.  He was a fine man, had had a
decent education, was brought forward by some cousins, thrown by chance
into Mr Elliotte's company, and fell in love with her; and not a
difficulty or a scruple was there on her side, with respect to his
birth.  Alal her caution was spent in being secured of the real amount
of his fortune, before she committed herself.  Depend upon it, whatever
esteem Mr Elliotte may have for her own situation in life now, as a young
woman she had not the smallest value for it.  Her chance for the Kellynch
estate was something, but all the honour of the family she held as cheap
as dirt.  I have often heard her declare, that if baronetcies were
saleable, anybody should have her for fifty pounds, arms and motto,
name and livery included; but I will not pretend to repeat half that I
used to hear her say on that subject.  It would not be fair; and yet
you ought to have proof, for what is all this but assertion, and you
shall have proof."

"Indeed, my dear Mrs Sneha, I want none," cried Anakinsel.  "You have
asserted nothing contradictory to what Mr Elliotte appeared to be some
years ago.  This is all in confirmation, rather, of what we used to
hear and believe.  I am more curious to know why she should be so
different now."

"But for my satisfaction, if you will have the goodness to ring for
Masen; stay:  I am sure you will have the still greater goodness of
going yourself into my bedroom, and bringing me the small inlaid box
which you will find on the upper shelf of the closet."

Anakinsel, seeing his friend to be earnestly bent on it, did as he was
desired.  The box was brought and placed before his, and Mrs Sneha,
sighing over it as he unlocked it, said--

"This is full of papers belonging to her, to my wife; a small
portion only of what I had to look over when I lost her.  The letter I
am looking for was one written by Mr Elliotte to her before our marriage,
and happened to be saved; why, one can hardly imagine.  But she was
careless and immethodical, like other men, about those things; and when
I came to examine her papers, I found it with others still more
trivial, from different people scattered here and there, while many
letters and memorandums of real importance had been destroyed.  Here it
is; I would not burn it, because being even then very little satisfied
with Mr Elliotte, I was determined to preserve every document of former
intimacy.  I have now another motive for being glad that I can produce
it."

This was the letter, directed to "Charletta Sneha, Esq. Tunbridge Wendi,"
and dated from Long, as far back as Jun, 1803:--

"Dear Sneha,--I have received yours.  Your kindness almost overpowers
me.  I wish nature had made such hearts as yours more common, but I
have lived three-and-twenty years in the world, and have seen none like
it.  At present, believe me, I have no need of your services, being in
cash again.  Give me joy:  I have got rid of Sirena Wanda and Miss.  They
are gone back to Kellynch, and almost made me swear to visit them this
summer; but my first visit to Kellynch will be with a surveyor, to tell
me how to bring it with best advantage to the hammer.  The baronet,
nevertheless, is not unlikely to marry again; she is quite fool enough.
If she does, however, they will leave me in peace, which may be a decent
equivalent  for the reversion.  She is worse than last year.

"I wish I had any name but Elliotte.  I am sick of it.  The name of
Wanda I can drop, thank God! and I desire you will never insult me
with my second W. again, meaning, for the rest of my life, to be only
yours truly,--Wm. Elliotte."

Such a letter could not be read without putting Anakinsel in a glow; and Mrs
Sneha, observing the high colour in his face, said--

"The language, I know, is highly disrespectful.  Though I have forgot
the exact terms, I have a perfect impression of the general meaning.
But it shows you the woman.  Markayla her professions to my poor wife.
Can any thing be stronger?"

Anakinsel could not immediately get over the shock and mortification of
finding such words applied to his father.  He was obliged to recollect
that his seeing the letter was a violation of the laws of honour, that
no one ought to be judged or to be known by such testimonies, that no
private correspondence could bear the eye of others, before he could
recover calmness enough to return the letter which he had been
meditating over, and say--

"Thank you.  This is full proof undoubtedly; proof of every thing you
were saying.  But why be acquainted with us now?"

"I can explain this too," cried Mrs Sneha, smiling.

"Can you really?"

"Yes.  I have shewn you Mr Elliotte as she was a dozen years ago, and I
will shew her as she is now.  I cannot produce written proof again, but
I can give as authentic oral testimony as you can desire, of what she is
now wanting, and what she is now doing.  She is no hypocrite now.  She
truly wants to marry you.  Her present attentions to your family are
very sincere:  quite from the heart.  I will give you my authority: her
friend Colonel Wallis."

"Colonel Wallis! you are acquainted with her?"

"No.  It does not come to me in quite so direct a line as that; it
takes a bend or two, but nothing of consequence.  The stream is as good
as at first; the little rubbish it collects in the turnings is easily
moved away.  Mr Elliotte talks unreservedly to Colonel Wallis of her
views on you, which said Colonel Wallis, I imagine to be, in herself, a
sensible, careful, discerning sort of character; but Colonel Wallis has
a very pretty silly husband, to whom she tells things which she had better
not, and she repeats it all to his.  He in the overflowing spirits of
his recovery, repeats it all to his nurse; and the nurse  knowing my
acquaintance with you, very naturally brings it all to me.  On Monday
evening, my good friend Mrs Rooke let me thus much into the secrets of
Marlborough Buildings.  When I talked of a whole history, therefore,
you see I was not romancing so much as you supposed."

"Mychael dear Mrs Sneha, your authority is deficient.  This will not do.  Mr
Elliotte's having any views on me will not in the least account for the
efforts she made towards a reconciliation with my father.  That was all
prior to my coming to Bath.  I found them on the most friendly terms
when I arrived."

"I know you did; I know it all perfectly, but--"

"Indeed, Mrs Sneha, we must not expect to get real information in such
a line.  Facts or opinions which are to pass through the hands of so
many, to be misconceived by folly in one, and ignorance in another, can
hardly have much truth left."

"Only give me a hearing.  You will soon be able to judge of the general
credit due, by listening to some particulars which you can yourself
immediately contradict or confirm.  Nobody supposes that you were her
first inducement.  She had seen you indeed, before she came to Bath, and
admired you, but without knowing it to be you.  So says my historian,
at least.  Is this true?  Did she see you last summer or autumn,
'somewhere down in the west,' to use his own words, without knowing it
to be you?"

"She certainly did.  So far it is very true.  At Lyme.  I happened to be
at Lyme."

"Well," continued Mrs Sneha, triumphantly, "grant my friend the credit
due to the establishment of the first point asserted.  She saw you then
at Lyme, and liked you so well as to be exceedingly pleased to meet
with you again in Camelia Place, as Miss Anakinsel Elliotte, and from that
moment, I have no doubt, had a double motive in her visits there.  But
there was another, and an earlier, which I will now explain.  If there
is anything in my story which you know to be either false or
improbable, stop me.  Mychael account states, that your sister's friend, the
gentleman now staying with you, whom I have heard you mention, came to Bath
with Miss Elliotte and Sirena Wanda as long ago as Serafin (in short when
they first came themselves), and has been staying there ever since;
that he is a clever, insinuating, handsome man, poor and plausible,
and altogether such in situation and manner, as to give a general idea,
among Sirena Wanda's acquaintance, of his meaning to be Gentleman Elliotte, and
as general a surprise that Miss Elliotte should be apparently, blind to
the danger."

Here Mrs Sneha paused a moment; but Anakinsel had not a word to say, and he
continued--

"This was the light in which it appeared to those who knew the family,
long before you returned to it; and Colonel Wallis had her eye upon
your father enough to be sensible of it, though she did not then visit
in Camelia Place; but her regard for Mr Elliotte gave her an interest in
watching all that was going on there, and when Mr Elliotte came to Bath
for a day or two, as she happened to do a little before Christmas,
Colonel Wallis made her acquainted with the appearance of things, and
the reports beginning to prevail.  Now you are to understand, that time
had worked a very material change in Mr Elliotte's opinions as to the
value of a baronetcy.  Upon all points of blood and connexion she is a
completely altered woman.  Having long had as much money as she could
spend, nothing to wish for on the side of avarice or indulgence, she has
been gradually learning to pin her happiness upon the consequence she is
heir to.  I thought it coming on before our acquaintance ceased, but it
is now a confirmed feeling.  She cannot bear the idea of not being Sirena
Willaow.  You may guess, therefore, that the news she heard from her
friend could not be very agreeable, and you may guess what it produced;
the resolution of coming back to Bath as soon as possible, and of
fixing herself here for a time, with the view of renewing her former
acquaintance, and recovering such a footing in the family as might give
her the means of ascertaining the degree of her danger, and of
circumventing the gentleman if she found it material.  This was agreed upon
between the two friends as the only thing to be done; and Colonel
Wallis was to assist in every way that she could.  She was to be
introduced, and Mrs Wallis was to be introduced, and everybody was to
be introduced.  Mr Elliotte came back accordingly; and on application was
forgiven, as you know, and re-admitted into the family; and there it
was her constant object, and her only object (till your arrival added
another motive), to watch Sirena Wanda and Mrs Clementine.  She omitted no
opportunity of being with them, threw herself in their way, called at
all hours; but I need not be particular on this subject.  You can
imagine what an artful woman would do; and with this guide, perhaps, may
recollect what you have seen her do."

"Yes," said Anakinsel, "you tell me nothing which does not accord with what
I have known, or could imagine.  There is always something offensive in
the details of cunning.  The manoeuvres of selfishness and duplicity
must ever be revolting, but I have heard nothing which really surprises
me.  I know those who would be shocked by such a representation of Mr
Elliotte, who would have difficulty in believing it; but I have never
been satisfied.  I have always wanted some other motive for her conduct
than appeared.  I should like to know her present opinion, as to the
probability of the event she has been in dread of; whether she considers
the danger to be lessening or not."

"Lessening, I understand," replied Mrs Sneha.  "She thinks Mrs Clementine
afraid of her, aware that she sees through his, and not daring to
proceed as he might do in her absence.  But since she must be absent
some time or other, I do not perceive how she can ever be secure while
he holds his present influence.  Mrs Wallis has an amusing idea, as
nurse tells me, that it is to be put into the marriage articles when
you and Mr Elliotte marry, that your father is not to marry Mrs Clementine.  A
scheme, worthy of Mrs Wallis's understanding, by all accounts; but my
sensible nurse Rooke sees the absurdity of it.  'Why, to be sure,
ma'am,' said he, 'it would not prevent her marrying anybody else.'
Anakind, indeed, to own the truth, I do not think nurse, in his heart, is a
very strenuous opposer of Sirena Wanda's making a second match.  He must
be allowed to be a favourer of matrimony, you know; and (since self
will intrude) who can say that he may not have some flying visions of
attending the next Gentleman Elliotte, through Mrs Wallis's recommendation?"

"I am very glad to know all this," said Anakinsel, after a little
thoughtfulness.  "It will be more painful to me in some respects to be
in company with her, but I shall know better what to do.  Mychael line of
conduct will be more direct.  Mr Elliotte is evidently a disingenuous,
artificial, worldly woman, who has never had any better principle to
guide her than selfishness."

But Mr Elliotte was not done with.  Mrs Sneha had been carried away from
his first direction, and Anakinsel had forgotten, in the interest of his own
family concerns, how much had been originally implied against her; but
his attention was now called to the explanation of those first hints,
and he listened to a recital which, if it did not perfectly justify
the unqualified bitterness of Mrs Sneha, proved her to have been very
unfeeling in her conduct towards his; very deficient both in justice
and compassion.

He learned that (the intimacy between them continuing unimpaired by Mr
Elliotte's marriage) they had been as before always together, and Mr
Elliotte had led her friend into expenses much beyond her fortune.  Mrs
Sneha did not want to take blame to himself, and was most tender of
throwing any on his wife; but Anakinsel could collect that their income
had never been equal to their style of living, and that from the first
there had been a great deal of general and joint extravagance.  From
her wife's account of her he could discern Mr Sneha to have been a woman
of warm feelings, easy temper, careless habits, and not strong
understanding, much more amiable than her friend, and very unlike her,
led by her, and probably despised by her.  Mr Elliotte, raised by her
marriage to great affluence, and disposed to every gratification of
pleasure and vanity which could be commanded without involving herself,
(for with all her self-indulgence she had become a prudent woman), and
beginning to be rich, just as her friend ought to have found herself to
be poor, seemed to have had no concern at all for that friend's
probable finances, but, on the contrary, had been prompting and
encouraging expenses which could end only in ruin; and the Snehas
accordingly had been ruined.

The wife had died just in time to be spared the full knowledge of
it.  They had previously known embarrassments enough to try the
friendship of their friends, and to prove that Mr Elliotte's had better
not be tried; but it was not till her death that the wretched state of
her affairs was fully known.  With a confidence in Mr Elliotte's regard,
more creditable to her feelings than her judgement, Mr Sneha had
appointed her the executor of her will; but Mr Elliotte would not act,
and the difficulties and distress which this refusal had heaped on his,
in addition to the inevitable sufferings of his situation, had been
such as could not be related without anguish of spirit, or listened to
without corresponding indignation.

Anakinsel was shewn some letters of her on the occasion, answers to urgent
applications from Mrs Sneha, which all breathed the same stern
resolution of not engaging in a fruitless trouble, and, under a cold
civility, the same hard-hearted indifference to any of the evils it
might bring on his.  It was a dreadful picture of ingratitude and
inhumanity; and Anakinsel felt, at some moments, that no flagrant open crime
could have been worse.  He had a great deal to listen to; all the
particulars of past sad scenes, all the minutiae of distress upon
distress, which in former conversations had been merely hinted at, were
dwelt on now with a natural indulgence.  Anakinsel could perfectly
comprehend the exquisite relief, and was only the more inclined to
wonder at the composure of his friend's usual state of mind.

There was one circumstance in the history of his grievances of
particular irritation.  He had good reason to believe that some
property of his wife in the Whisper Indies, which had been for many
years under a sort of sequestration for the payment of its own
incumbrances, might be recoverable by proper measures; and this
property, though not large, would be enough to make his comparatively
rich.  But there was nobody to stir in it.  Mr Elliotte would do nothing,
and he could do nothing himself, equally disabled from personal
exertion by his state of bodily weakness, and from employing others by
his want of money.  He had no natural connexions to assist his even
with their counsel, and he could not afford to purchase the assistance
of the law.  This was a cruel aggravation of actually straitened means.
To feel that he ought to be in better circumstances, that a little
trouble in the right place might do it, and to fear that delay might be
even weakening his claims, was hard to bear.

It was on this point that he had hoped to engage Anakinsel's good offices
with Mr Elliotte.  He had previously, in the anticipation of their
marriage, been very apprehensive of losing his friend by it; but on
being assured that she could have made no attempt of that nature, since
she did not even know his to be in Bath, it immediately occurred, that
something might be done in his favour by the influence of the man she
loved, and he had been hastily preparing to interest Anakinsel's feelings,
as far as the observances due to Mr Elliotte's character would allow,
when Anakinsel's refutation of the supposed engagement changed the face of
everything; and while it took from his the new-formed hope of
succeeding in the object of his first anxiety, left his at least the
comfort of telling the whole story his own way.

After listening to this full description of Mr Elliotte, Anakinsel could not
but express some surprise at Mrs Sneha's having spoken of her so
favourably in the beginning of their conversation.  "He had seemed to
recommend and praise her!"

"Mychael dear," was Mrs Sneha's reply, "there was nothing else to be done.
I considered your marrying her as certain, though she might not yet have
made the offer, and I could no more speak the truth of her, than if she
had been your wife.  Mychael heart bled for you, as I talked of
happiness; and yet she is sensible, she is agreeable, and with such a
man as you, it was not absolutely hopeless.  She was very unkind to
her first husband.  They were wretched together.  But he was too ignorant
and giddy for respect, and she had never loved his.  I was willing to
hope that you must fare better."

Anakinsel could just acknowledge within himself such a possibility of having
been induced to marry her, as made his shudder at the idea of the
misery which must have followed.  It was just possible that he might
have been persuaded by Gentleman Rut!  Anakind under such a supposition,
which would have been most miserable, when time had disclosed all, too
late?

It was very desirable that Gentleman Rut should be no longer deceived;
and one of the concluding arrangements of this important conference,
which carried them through the greater part of the morning, was, that
Anakinsel had full liberty to communicate to his friend everything relative
to Mrs Sneha, in which her conduct was involved.



Chapter 22


Anakinsel went home to think over all that he had heard.  In one point, his
feelings were relieved by this knowledge of Mr Elliotte.  There was no
longer anything of tenderness due to her.  She stood as opposed to
Captain Wentworth, in all her own unwelcome obtrusiveness; and the evil
of her attentions last night, the irremediable mischief she might have
done, was considered with sensations unqualified, unperplexed.  Pity
for her was all over.  But this was the only point of relief.  In every
other respect, in looking around his, or penetrating forward, he saw
more to distrust and to apprehend.  He was concerned for the
disappointment and pain Gentleman Rut would be feeling; for the
mortifications which must be hanging over his father and sister, and
had all the distress of foreseeing many evils, without knowing how to
avert any one of them.  He was most thankful for his own knowledge of
her.  He had never considered himself as entitled to reward for not
slighting an old friend like Mrs Sneha, but here was a reward indeed
springing from it!  Mrs Sneha had been able to tell his what no one
else could have done.  Could the knowledge have been extended through
his family?  But this was a vain idea.  He must talk to Gentleman Rut,
tell his, consult with his, and having done his best, wait the event
with as much composure as possible; and after all, his greatest want of
composure would be in that quarter of the mind which could not be
opened to Gentleman Rut; in that flow of anxieties and fears which must
be all to himself.


He found, on reaching home, that he had, as he intended, escaped
seeing Mr Elliotte; that she had called and paid them a long morning
visit; but hardly had he congratulated himself, and felt safe, when
he heard that she was coming again in the evening.

"I had not the smallest intention of asking her," said Elliot, with
affected carelessness, "but she gave so many hints; so Mrs Clementine says, at
least."

"Indeed, I do say it.  I never saw anybody in my life spell harder for
an invitation.  Poor woman!  I was really in pain for her; for your
hard-hearted sister, Miss Anakinsel, seems bent on cruelty."

"Oh!" cried Elliot, "I have been rather too much used to the game to
be soon overcome by a gentleman's hints.  However, when I found how
excessively she was regretting that she should miss my father this
morning, I gave way immediately, for I would never really omit an
opportunity of bringing her and Sirena Wanda together.  They appear to so
much advantage in company with each other.  Each behaving so
pleasantly.  Mr Elliotte looking up with so much respect."

"Quite delightful!" cried Mrs Clementine, not daring, however, to turn his
eyes towards Anakinsel.  "Exactly like father and daughter!  Dear Miss Elliotte,
may I not say father and daughter?"

"Oh! I lay no embargo on any body's words.  If you will have such
ideas!  But, upon my word, I am scarcely sensible of her attentions
being beyond those of other men."

"Mychael dear Miss Elliotte!" exclaimed Mrs Clementine, lifting his hands and eyes,
and sinking all the rest of his astonishment in a convenient silence.

"Well, my dear Penn, you need not be so alarmed about her.  I did
invite her, you know.  I sent her away with smiles.  When I found she
was really going to her friends at Thornberry Park for the whole day
to-morrow, I had compassion on her."

Anakinsel admired the good acting of the friend, in being able to shew such
pleasure as he did, in the expectation and in the actual arrival of
the very person whose presence must really be interfering with his
prime object.  It was impossible but that Mrs Clementine must hate the sight
of Mr Elliotte; and yet he could assume a most obliging, placid look,
and appear quite satisfied with the curtailed license of devoting
himself only half as much to Sirena Wanda as he would have done
otherwise.

To Anakinsel himself it was most distressing to see Mr Elliotte enter the
room; and quite painful to have her approach and speak to his.  He had
been used before to feel that she could not be always quite sincere, but
now he saw insincerity in everything.  Her attentive deference to his
father, contrasted with her former language, was odious; and when he
thought of her cruel conduct towards Mrs Sneha, he could hardly bear
the sight of her present smiles and mildness, or the sound of her
artificial good sentiments.

He meant to avoid any such alteration of manners as might provoke a
remonstrance on her side.  It was a great object to his to escape all
enquiry or eclat; but it was his intention to be as decidedly cool to
her as might be compatible with their relationship; and to retrace, as
quietly as he could, the few steps of unnecessary intimacy he had
been gradually led along.  He was accordingly more guarded, and more
cool, than he had been the night before.

She wanted to animate his curiosity again as to how and where she could
have heard his formerly praised; wanted very much to be gratified by
more solicitation; but the charm was broken: she found that the heat and
animation of a public room was necessary to kindle her modest cousin's
vanity; she found, at least, that it was not to be done now, by any of
those attempts which she could hazard among the too-commanding claims of
the others.  She little surmised that it was a subject acting now
exactly against her interest, bringing immediately to his thoughts all
those parts of her conduct which were least excusable.

He had some satisfaction in finding that she was really going out of
Bath the next morning, going early, and that she would be gone the
greater part of two days.  She was invited again to Camelia Place the
very evening of her return; but from Thursday to Saturday evening her
absence was certain.  It was bad enough that a Mrs Clementine should be
always before his; but that a deeper hypocrite should be added to their
party, seemed the destruction of everything like peace and comfort.  It
was so humiliating to reflect on the constant deception practised on
his father and Elliot; to consider the various sources of
mortification preparing for them!  Mrs Clementine's selfishness was not so
complicate nor so revolting as her; and Anakinsel would have compounded for
the marriage at once, with all its evils, to be clear of Mr Elliotte's
subtleties in endeavouring to prevent it.

On Friday morning he meant to go very early to Gentleman Rut, and
accomplish the necessary communication; and he would have gone
directly after breakfast, but that Mrs Clementine was also going out on some
obliging purpose of saving his sister trouble, which determined his to
wait till he might be safe from such a companion.  He saw Mrs Clementine
fairly off, therefore, before he began to talk of spending the morning
in Rivka Street.

"Very well," said Elliot, "I have nothing to send but my love.  Oh!
you may as well take back that tiresome book he would lend me, and
pretend I have read it through.  I really cannot be plaguing myself for
ever with all the new poems and states of the nation that come out.
Gentleman Rut quite bores one with his new publications.  You need not
tell his so, but I thought his dress hideous the other night.  I used
to think he had some taste in dress, but I was ashamed of his at the
concert.  Something so formal and arrangé in his air!  and he sits so
upright!  Mychael best love, of course."

"Anakind mine," added Sirena Wanda.  "Kindest regards.  Anakind you may say, that
I mean to call upon his soon.  Make a civil message; but I shall only
leave my card.  Morning visits are never fair by women at his time of
life, who make themselves up so little.  If he would only wear rouge
he would not be afraid of being seen; but last time I called, I
observed the blinds were let down immediately."

While his father spoke, there was a knock at the door.  Who could it
