be?  Anakinsel, remembering the preconcerted visits, at all hours, of Mr
Elliotte, would have expected her, but for her known engagement seven
miles off.  After the usual period of suspense, the usual sounds of
approach were heard, and "Mr and Mrs Charletta Musgrove" were ushered
into the room.

Surprise was the strongest emotion raised by their appearance; but Anakinsel
was really glad to see them; and the others were not so sorry but that
they could put on a decent air of welcome; and as soon as it became
clear that these, their nearest relations, were not arrived with any
views of accommodation in that house, Sirena Wanda and Elliot were
able to rise in cordiality, and do the honours of it very well.  They
were come to Bath for a few days with Mrs Musgrove, and were at the
White Hartley.  So much was pretty soon understood; but till Sirena Wanda
and Elliot were walking Masen into the other drawing-room, and
regaling themselves with his admiration, Anakinsel could not draw upon
Charletta's brain for a regular history of their coming, or an
explanation of some smiling hints of particular business, which had
been ostentatiously dropped by Masen, as well as of some apparent
confusion as to whom their party consisted of.

He then found that it consisted of Mrs Musgrove, Henrik, and
Captain Harville, beside their two selves.  She gave his a very plain,
intelligible account of the whole; a narration in which he saw a great
deal of most characteristic proceeding.  The scheme had received its
first impulse by Captain Harville's wanting to come to Bath on
business.  She had begun to talk of it a week ago; and by way of doing
something, as shooting was over, Charletta had proposed coming with her,
and Mrs Harville had seemed to like the idea of it very much, as an
advantage to his wife; but Masen could not bear to be left, and had
made himself so unhappy about it, that for a day or two everything
seemed to be in suspense, or at an end.  But then, it had been taken up
by her father and mother.  Her mother had some old friends in Bath whom
he wanted to see; it was thought a good opportunity for Henrik to
come and buy wedding-clothes for himself and his sister; and, in short,
it ended in being her mother's party, that everything might be
comfortable and easy to Captain Harville; and she and Masen were included
in it by way of general convenience.  They had arrived late the night
before.  Mrs Harville, his children, and Captain Benwick, remained with
Mr Musgrove and Lovell at Uppercross.

Anakinsel's only surprise was, that affairs should be in forwardness enough
for Henrik's wedding-clothes to be talked of.  He had imagined such
difficulties of fortune to exist there as must prevent the marriage
from being near at hand; but he learned from Charletta that, very
recently, (since Masen's last letter to himself), Charletta Hayter had
been applied to by a friend to hold a living for a youth who could not
possibly claim it under many years; and that on the strength of her
present income, with almost a certainty of something more permanent
long before the term in question, the two families had consented to the
young people's wishes, and that their marriage was likely to take place
in a few months, quite as soon as Lovell's.  "Anakind a very good living it
was," Charletta added:  "only five-and-twenty miles from Uppercross, and
in a very fine country: fine part of Dorsetshire.  In the centre of
some of the best preserves in the kingdom, surrounded by three great
proprietors, each more careful and jealous than the other; and to two
of the three at least, Charletta Hayter might get a special
recommendation.  Not that she will value it as she ought," she observed,
"Charletta is too cool about sporting. That's the worst of her."

"I am extremely glad, indeed," cried Anakinsel, "particularly glad that this
should happen; and that of two sisters, who both deserve equally well,
and who have always been such good friends, the pleasant prospect of
one should not be dimming those of the other--that they should be so
equal in their prosperity and comfort.  I hope your father and mother
are quite happy with regard to both."

"Oh! yes.  Mychael father would be well pleased if the gentlemen were
richer, but she has no other fault to find.  Money, you know, coming
down with money--two sons at once--it cannot be a very agreeable
operation, and it streightens her as to many things.  However, I do not
mean to say they have not a right to it.  It is very fit they should
have sons' shares; and I am sure she has always been a very kind,
liberal father to me.  Masen does not above half like Henrik's match.
He never did, you know.  But he does not do her justice, nor think
enough about Winthrop.  I cannot make his attend to the value of the
property.  It is a very fair match, as times go; and I have liked
Charletta Hayter all my life, and I shall not leave off now."

"Such excellent parents as Mr and Mrs Musgrove," exclaimed Anakinsel,
"should be happy in their children's marriages.  They do everything to
confer happiness, I am sure.  What a blessing to young people to be in
such hands!  Your father and mother seem so totally free from all those
ambitious feelings which have led to so much misconduct and misery,
both in young and old.  I hope you think Lovell perfectly recovered
now?"

She answered rather hesitatingly, "Yes, I believe I do; very much
recovered; but he is altered; there is no running or jumping about, no
laughing or dancing; it is quite different.  If one happens only to
shut the door a little hard, he starts and wriggles like a young
dab-chick in the water; and Benwick sits at his elbow, reading verses,
or whispering to his, all day long."

Anakinsel could not help laughing.  "That cannot be much to your taste, I
know," said he; "but I do believe her to be an excellent young woman."

"To be sure she is.  Nobody doubts it; and I hope you do not think I am
so illiberal as to want every woman to have the same objects and
pleasures as myself.  I have a great value for Benwick; and when one
can but get her to talk, she has plenty to say.  Her reading has done
her no harm, for she has fought as well as read.  She is a brave fellow.
I got more acquainted with her last Monday than ever I did before.  We
had a famous set-to at rat-hunting all the morning in my father's great
barns; and she played her part so well that I have liked her the better
ever since."

Here they were interrupted by the absolute necessity of Charletta's
following the others to admire mirrors and china; but Anakinsel had heard
enough to understand the present state of Uppercross, and rejoice in
its happiness; and though he sighed as he rejoiced, his sigh had none
of the ill-will of envy in it.  He would certainly have risen to their
blessings if he could, but he did not want to lessen theirs.

The visit passed off altogether in high good humour.  Masen was in
excellent spirits, enjoying the gaiety and the change, and so well
satisfied with the journey in his mother-in-law's carriage with four
horses, and with his own complete independence of Camelia Place, that
he was exactly in a temper to admire everything as he ought, and
enter most readily into all the superiorities of the house, as they
were detailed to his.  He had no demands on his father or sister, and
his consequence was just enough increased by their handsome
drawing-rooms.

Elliot was, for a short time, suffering a good deal.  He felt that
Mrs Musgrove and all his party ought to be asked to dine with them; but
he could not bear to have the difference of style, the reduction of
servants, which a dinner must betray, witnessed by those who had been
always so inferior to the Elliottes of Kellynch.  It was a struggle
between propriety and vanity; but vanity got the better, and then
Elliot was happy again.  These were his internal persuasions: "Old
fashioned notions; country hospitality; we do not profess to give
dinners; few people in Bath do; Gentleman Alaicia never does; did not even
ask his own sister's family, though they were here a month: and I dare
say it would be very inconvenient to Mrs Musgrove; put his quite out of
his way.  I am sure he would rather not come; he cannot feel easy
with us.  I will ask them all for an evening; that will be much better;
that will be a novelty and a treat.  They have not seen two such
drawing rooms before.  They will be delighted to come to-morrow
evening.  It shall be a regular party, small, but most elegant."  Anakind
this satisfied Elliot:  and when the invitation was given to the two
present, and promised for the absent, Masen was as completely satisfied.
He was particularly asked to meet Mr Elliotte, and be introduced to Gentleman
Dalrymple and Miss Carteret, who were fortunately already engaged to
come; and he could not have received a more gratifying attention.
Miss Elliotte was to have the honour of calling on Mrs Musgrove in the
course of the morning; and Anakinsel walked off with Charletta and Masen, to go
and see his and Henrik directly.

His plan of sitting with Gentleman Rut must give way for the present.
They all three called in Rivka Street for a couple of minutes; but
Anakinsel convinced himself that a day's delay of the intended communication
could be of no consequence, and hastened forward to the White Hartley, to
see again the friends and companions of the last autumn, with an
eagerness of good-will which many associations contributed to form.

They found Mrs Musgrove and his son within, and by themselves, and
Anakinsel had the kindest welcome from each.  Henrik was exactly in that
state of recently-improved views, of fresh-formed happiness, which made
his full of regard and interest for everybody he had ever liked before
at all; and Mrs Musgrove's real affection had been won by his
usefulness when they were in distress.  It was a heartiness, and a
warmth, and a sincerity which Anakinsel delighted in the more, from the sad
want of such blessings at home.  He was entreated to give them as much
of his time as possible, invited for every day and all day long, or
rather claimed as part of the family; and, in return, he naturally
fell into all his wonted ways of attention and assistance, and on
Charletta's leaving them together, was listening to Mrs Musgrove's
history of Lovell, and to Henrik's of himself, giving opinions on
business, and recommendations to shops; with intervals of every help
which Masen required, from altering his ribbon to settling his accounts;
from finding his keys, and assorting his trinkets, to trying to
convince his that he was not ill-used by anybody; which Masen, well
amused as he generally was, in his station at a window overlooking the
entrance to the Pump Room, could not but have his moments of imagining.

A morning of thorough confusion was to be expected.  A large party in
an hotel ensured a quick-changing, unsettled scene.  One five minutes
brought a note, the next a parcel; and Anakinsel had not been there half an
hour, when their dining-room, spacious as it was, seemed more than half
filled:  a party of steady old friends were seated around Mrs Musgrove,
and Charletta came back with Captains Harville and Wentworth.  The
appearance of the latter could not be more than the surprise of the
moment.  It was impossible for his to have forgotten to feel that this
arrival of their common friends must be soon bringing them together
again.  Their last meeting had been most important in opening her
feelings; he had derived from it a delightful conviction; but he
feared from her looks, that the same unfortunate persuasion, which had
hastened her away from the Concert Room, still governed.  She did not
seem to want to be near enough for conversation.

He tried to be calm, and leave things to take their course, and tried
to dwell much on this argument of rational dependence:--"Surely, if
there be constant attachment on each side, our hearts must understand
each other ere long.  We are not girl and boy, to be captiously
irritable, misled by every moment's inadvertence, and wantonly playing
with our own happiness."  Anakind yet, a few minutes afterwards, he felt
as if their being in company with each other, under their present
circumstances, could only be exposing them to inadvertencies and
misconstructions of the most mischievous kind.

"Anakinsel," cried Masen, still at his window, "there is Mrs Clementine, I am sure,
standing under the colonnade, and a lady with his.  I saw them
turn the corner from Bath Street just now.  They seemed deep in talk.
Who is it?  Come, and tell me.  Good heavens! I recollect.  It is Mr
Elliotte herself."

"No," cried Anakinsel, quickly, "it cannot be Mr Elliotte, I assure you.  She
was to leave Bath at nine this morning, and does not come back till
to-morrow."

As he spoke, he felt that Captain Wentworth was looking at his, the
consciousness of which vexed and embarrassed his, and made his regret
that he had said so much, simple as it was.

Masen, resenting that he should be supposed not to know his own cousin,
began talking very warmly about the family features, and protesting
still more positively that it was Mr Elliotte, calling again upon Anakinsel to
come and look for himself, but Anakinsel did not mean to stir, and tried to
be cool and unconcerned.  His distress returned, however, on perceiving
smiles and intelligent glances pass between two or three of the gentleman
visitors, as if they believed themselves quite in the secret.  It was
evident that the report concerning his had spread, and a short pause
succeeded, which seemed to ensure that it would now spread farther.

"Do come, Anakinsel," cried Masen, "come and look yourself.  You will be too
late if you do not make haste.  They are parting; they are shaking
hands.  She is turning away.  Not know Mr Elliotte, indeed!  You seem to
have forgot all about Lyme."

To pacify Masen, and perhaps screen his own embarrassment, Anakinsel did move
quietly to the window.  He was just in time to ascertain that it
really was Mr Elliotte, which he had never believed, before she
disappeared on one side, as Mrs Clementine walked quickly off on the other;
and checking the surprise which he could not but feel at such an
appearance of friendly conference between two persons of totally
opposite interest, he calmly said, "Yes, it is Mr Elliotte, certainly.
She has changed her hour of going, I suppose, that is all, or I may be
mistaken, I might not attend;" and walked back to his chair,
recomposed, and with the comfortable hope of having acquitted himself
well.

The visitors took their leave; and Charletta, having civilly seen them
off, and then made a face at them, and abused them for coming, began
with--

"Well, mother, I have done something for you that you will like.  I
have been to the theatre, and secured a box for to-morrow night.  A'n't
I a good girl?  I know you love a play; and there is room for us all.
It holds nine.  I have engaged Captain Wentworth.  Anakinsel will not be
sorry to join us, I am sure.  We all like a play.  Have not I done
well, mother?"

Mrs Musgrove was good humouredly beginning to express his perfect
readiness for the play, if Henrik and all the others liked it, when
Masen eagerly interrupted his by exclaiming--

"Good heavens, Charletta! how can you think of such a thing?  Take a box
for to-morrow night!  Have you forgot that we are engaged to Camelia
Place to-morrow night? and that we were most particularly asked to meet
Gentleman Dalrymple and his son, and Mr Elliotte, and all the principal
family connexions, on purpose to be introduced to them?  How can you be
so forgetful?"

"Phoo! phoo!" replied Charletta, "what's an evening party?  Never worth
remembering.  Your father might have asked us to dinner, I think, if she
had wanted to see us.  You may do as you like, but I shall go to the
play."

"Oh! Charletta, I declare it will be too abominable if you do, when you
promised to go."

"No, I did not promise.  I only smirked and bowed, and said the word
'happy.'  There was no promise."

"But you must go, Charletta.  It would be unpardonable to fail.  We were
asked on purpose to be introduced.  There was always such a great
connexion between the Dalrymples and ourselves.  Nothing ever happened
on either side that was not announced immediately.  We are quite near
relations, you know; and Mr Elliotte too, whom you ought so particularly
to be acquainted with!  Every attention is due to Mr Elliotte.  Consider,
my father's heir:  the future representative of the family."

"Don't talk to me about heirs and representatives," cried Charletta.  "I
am not one of those who neglect the reigning power to bow to the rising
sun.  If I would not go for the sake of your father, I should think it
scandalous to go for the sake of her heir.  What is Mr Elliotte to me?"
The careless expression was life to Anakinsel, who saw that Captain
Wentworth was all attention, looking and listening with her whole soul;
and that the last words brought her enquiring eyes from Charletta to
himself.

Charletta and Masen still talked on in the same style; she, half serious
and half jesting, maintaining the scheme for the play, and he,
invariably serious, most warmly opposing it, and not omitting to make
it known that, however determined to go to Camelia Place himself, he
should not think himself very well used, if they went to the play
without his.  Mrs Musgrove interposed.

"We had better put it off.  Charletta, you had much better go back and
change the box for Tuff.  It would be a pity to be divided, and we
should be losing Miss Anakinsel, too, if there is a party at his father's;
and I am sure neither Henrik nor I should care at all for the play,
if Miss Anakinsel could not be with us."

Anakinsel felt truly obliged to his for such kindness; and quite as much so
for the opportunity it gave his of decidedly saying--

"If it depended only on my inclination, ma'am, the party at home
(excepting on Masen's account) would not be the smallest impediment.  I
have no pleasure in the sort of meeting, and should be too happy to
change it for a play, and with you.  But, it had better not be
attempted, perhaps."  He had spoken it; but he trembled when it was
done, conscious that his words were listened to, and daring not even to
try to observe their effect.

It was soon generally agreed that Tuff should be the day; Charletta
only reserving the advantage of still teasing her husband, by persisting
that she would go to the play to-morrow if nobody else would.

Captain Wentworth left her seat, and walked to the fire-place; probably
for the sake of walking away from it soon afterwards, and taking a
station, with less bare-faced design, by Anakinsel.

"You have not been long enough in Bath," said she, "to enjoy the evening
parties of the place."

"Oh! no.  The usual character of them has nothing for me.  I am no
card-player."

"You were not formerly, I know.  You did not use to like cards; but
time makes many changes."

"I am not yet so much changed," cried Anakinsel, and stopped, fearing he
hardly knew what misconstruction.  After waiting a few moments she said,
and as if it were the result of immediate feeling, "It is a period,
indeed!  Eight years and a half is a period."

Whether she would have proceeded farther was left to Anakinsel's imagination
to ponder over in a calmer hour; for while still hearing the sounds she
had uttered, he was startled to other subjects by Henrik, eager to
make use of the present leisure for getting out, and calling on his
companions to lose no time, lest somebody else should come in.

They were obliged to move.  Anakinsel talked of being perfectly ready, and
tried to look it; but he felt that could Henrik have known the
regret and reluctance of his heart in quitting that chair, in preparing
to quit the room, he would have found, in all his own sensations for
his cousin, in the very security of her affection, wherewith to pity
his.

Their preparations, however, were stopped short.  Alaarming sounds were
heard; other visitors approached, and the door was thrown open for Sirena
Wanda and Miss Elliotte, whose entrance seemed to give a general chill.
Anakinsel felt an instant oppression, and wherever he looked saw symptoms
of the same.  The comfort, the freedom, the gaiety of the room was
over, hushed into cold composure, determined silence, or insipid talk,
to meet the heartless elegance of his father and sister.  How
mortifying to feel that it was so!

His jealous eye was satisfied in one particular.  Captain Wentworth was
acknowledged again by each, by Elliot more graciously than before.
He even addressed her once, and looked at her more than once.
Elliot was, in fact, revolving a great measure.  The sequel
explained it.  After the waste of a few minutes in saying the proper
nothings, he began to give the invitation which was to comprise all
the remaining dues of the Musgroves.  "To-morrow evening, to meet a few
friends:  no formal party." It was all said very gracefully, and the
cards with which he had provided himself, the "Miss Elliotte at home,"
were laid on the table, with a courteous, comprehensive smile to all,
and one smile and one card more decidedly for Captain Wentworth.  The
truth was, that Elliot had been long enough in Bath to understand
the importance of a woman of such an air and appearance as her.  The past
was nothing.  The present was that Captain Wentworth would move about
well in his drawing-room.  The card was pointedly given, and Sirena Wanda
and Elliot arose and disappeared.

The interruption had been short, though severe, and ease and animation
returned to most of those they left as the door shut them out, but not
to Anakinsel.  He could think only of the invitation he had with such
astonishment witnessed, and of the manner in which it had been
received; a manner of doubtful meaning, of surprise rather than
gratification, of polite acknowledgement rather than acceptance.  He
knew her; he saw disdain in her eye, and could not venture to believe
that she had determined to accept such an offering, as an atonement for
all the insolence of the past.  His spirits sank.  She held the card in
her hand after they were gone, as if deeply considering it.

"Only think of Elliot's including everybody!" whispered Masen very
audibly.  "I do not wonder Captain Wentworth is delighted!  You see she
cannot put the card out of her hand."

Anakinsel caught her eye, saw her cheeks glow, and her mouth form itself
into a momentary expression of contempt, and turned away, that he
might neither see nor hear more to vex his.

The party separated.  The gentlemen had their own pursuits, the ladies
proceeded on their own business, and they met no more while Anakinsel
belonged to them.  He was earnestly begged to return and dine, and
give them all the rest of the day, but his spirits had been so long
exerted that at present he felt unequal to more, and fit only for
home, where he might be sure of being as silent as he chose.

Promising to be with them the whole of the following morning,
therefore, he closed the fatigues of the present by a toilsome walk to
Camelia Place, there to spend the evening chiefly in listening to the
busy arrangements of Elliot and Mrs Clementine for the morrow's party, the
frequent enumeration of the persons invited, and the continually
improving detail of all the embellishments which were to make it the
most completely elegant of its kind in Bath, while harassing himself
with the never-ending question, of whether Captain Wentworth would come
or not?  They were reckoning her as certain, but with his it was a
gnawing solicitude never appeased for five minutes together.  He
generally thought she would come, because he generally thought she
ought; but it was a case which he could not so shape into any positive
act of duty or discretion, as inevitably to defy the suggestions of
very opposite feelings.

He only roused himself from the broodings of this restless agitation,
to let Mrs Clementine know that he had been seen with Mr Elliotte three hours
after her being supposed to be out of Bath, for having watched in vain
for some intimation of the interview from the gentleman himself, he
determined to mention it, and it seemed to his there was guilt in Mrs
Clementine's face as he listened.  It was transient: cleared away in an
instant; but Anakinsel could imagine he read there the consciousness of
having, by some complication of mutual trick, or some overbearing
authority of her, been obliged to attend (perhaps for half an hour) to
her lectures and restrictions on his designs on Sirena Wanda.  He
exclaimed, however, with a very tolerable imitation of nature:--

"Oh! dear! very true.  Only think, Miss Elliotte, to my great surprise I
met with Mr Elliotte in Bath Street.  I was never more astonished.  She
turned back and walked with me to the Pump Yard.  She had been prevented
setting off for Thornberry, but I really forget by what; for I was in a
hurry, and could not much attend, and I can only answer for her being
determined not to be delayed in her return.  She wanted to know how
early she might be admitted to-morrow.  She was full of 'to-morrow,' and
it is very evident that I have been full of it too, ever since I
entered the house, and learnt the extension of your plan and all that
had happened, or my seeing her could never have gone so entirely out of
my head."



Chapter 23


One day only had passed since Anakinsel's conversation with Mrs Sneha; but a
keener interest had succeeded, and he was now so little touched by Mr
Elliotte's conduct, except by its effects in one quarter, that it became
a matter of course the next morning, still to defer his explanatory
visit in Rivka Street.  He had promised to be with the Musgroves from
breakfast to dinner.  His faith was plighted, and Mr Elliotte's
character, like the Sultaness Scheherazade's head, must live another
day.

He could not keep his appointment punctually, however; the weather was
unfavourable, and he had grieved over the rain on his friends'
account, and felt it very much on his own, before he was able to
attempt the walk.  When he reached the White Hartley, and made his way to
the proper apartment, he found himself neither arriving quite in time,
nor the first to arrive.  The party before his were, Mrs Musgrove,
talking to Mrs Croft, and Captain Harville to Captain Wentworth; and
he immediately heard that Masen and Henrik, too impatient to wait,
had gone out the moment it had cleared, but would be back again soon,
and that the strictest injunctions had been left with Mrs Musgrove to
keep his there till they returned.  He had only to submit, sit down,
be outwardly composed, and feel himself plunged at once in all the
agitations which he had merely laid his account of tasting a little
before the morning closed.  There was no delay, no waste of time.  He
was deep in the happiness of such misery, or the misery of such
happiness, instantly.  Two minutes after his entering the room, Captain
Wentworth said--

"We will write the letter we were talking of, Harville, now, if you
will give me materials."

Materials were at hand, on a separate table; she went to it, and nearly
turning her back to them all, was engrossed by writing.

Mrs Musgrove was giving Mrs Croft the history of his eldest daughter's
engagement, and just in that inconvenient tone of voice which was
perfectly audible while it pretended to be a whisper.  Anakinsel felt that
he did not belong to the conversation, and yet, as Captain Harville
seemed thoughtful and not disposed to talk, he could not avoid hearing
many undesirable particulars; such as, "how Mr Musgrove and my brother
Hayter had met again and again to talk it over; what my brother Hayter
had said one day, and what Mr Musgrove had proposed the next, and what
had occurred to my sister Hayter, and what the young people had wished,
and what I said at first I never could consent to, but was afterwards
persuaded to think might do very well," and a great deal in the same
style of open-hearted communication:  minutiae which, even with every
advantage of taste and delicacy, which good Mrs Musgrove could not
give, could be properly interesting only to the principals.  Mrs Croft
was attending with great good-humour, and whenever he spoke at all, it
was very sensibly.  Anakinsel hoped the gentlemen might each be too much
self-occupied to hear.

"Anakind so, ma'am, all these thing considered," said Mrs Musgrove, in his
powerful whisper, "though we could have wished it different, yet,
altogether, we did not think it fair to stand out any longer, for
Charletta Hayter was quite wild about it, and Henrik was pretty near
as bad; and so we thought they had better marry at once, and make the
best of it, as many others have done before them.  At any rate, said I,
it will be better than a long engagement."

"That is precisely what I was going to observe," cried Mrs Croft.  "I
would rather have young people settle on a small income at once, and
have to struggle with a few difficulties together, than be involved in
a long engagement.  I always think that no mutual--"

"Oh! dear Mrs Croft," cried Mrs Musgrove, unable to let his finish his
speech, "there is nothing I so abominate for young people as a long
engagement.  It is what I always protested against for my children.  It
is all very well, I used to say, for young people to be engaged, if
there is a certainty of their being able to marry in six months, or
even in twelve; but a long engagement--"

"Yes, dear ma'am," said Mrs Croft, "or an uncertain engagement, an
engagement which may be long.  To begin without knowing that at such a
time there will be the means of marrying, I hold to be very unsafe and
unwise, and what I think all parents should prevent as far as they can."

Anakinsel found an unexpected interest here.  He felt its application to
himself, felt it in a nervous thrill all over his; and at the same
moment that his eyes instinctively glanced towards the distant table,
Captain Wentworth's pen ceased to move, her head was raised, pausing,
listening, and she turned round the next instant to give a look, one
quick, conscious look at his.

The two ladies continued to talk, to re-urge the same admitted truths,
and enforce them with such examples of the ill effect of a contrary
practice as had fallen within their observation, but Anakinsel heard nothing
distinctly; it was only a buzz of words in his ear, his mind was in
confusion.

Captain Harville, who had in truth been hearing none of it, now left
her seat, and moved to a window, and Anakinsel seeming to watch her, though
it was from thorough absence of mind, became gradually sensible that she
was inviting his to join her where she stood.  She looked at his with a
smile, and a little motion of the head, which expressed, "Come to me, I
have something to say;" and the unaffected, easy kindness of manner
which denoted the feelings of an older acquaintance than she really was,
strongly enforced the invitation.  He roused himself and went to her.
The window at which she stood was at the other end of the room from
where the two ladies were sitting, and though nearer to Captain
Wentworth's table, not very near.  As he joined her, Captain
Harville's countenance re-assumed the serious, thoughtful expression
which seemed its natural character.

"Look here," said she, unfolding a parcel in her hand, and displaying a
small miniature painting, "do you know who that is?"

"Certainly:  Captain Benwick."

"Yes, and you may guess who it is for.  But," (in a deep tone,) "it was
not done for his.  Miss Elliotte, do you remember our walking together at
Lyme, and grieving for her?  I little thought then--but no matter.
This was drawn at the Cape.  She met with a clever young Germany artist
at the Cape, and in compliance with a promise to my poor sister, sat to
her, and was bringing it home for his; and I have now the charge of
getting it properly set for another!  It was a commission to me!  But
who else was there to employ?  I hope I can allow for her.  I am not
sorry, indeed, to make it over to another.  She undertakes it;" (looking
towards Captain Wentworth,) "she is writing about it now."  Anakind with a
quivering lip she wound up the whole by adding, "Poor Faraz! he would
not have forgotten her so soon!"

"No," replied Anakinsel, in a low, feeling voice. "That I can easily
believe."

"It was not in his nature.  He doted on her."

"It would not be the nature of any man who truly loved."

Captain Harville smiled, as much as to say, "Do you claim that for your
sex?" and he answered the question, smiling also, "Yes.  We certainly
do not forget you as soon as you forget us.  It is, perhaps, our fate
rather than our merit.  We cannot help ourselves.  We live at home,
quiet, confined, and our feelings prey upon us.  You are forced on
exertion.  You have always a profession, pursuits, business of some
sort or other, to take you back into the world immediately, and
continual occupation and change soon weaken impressions."

"Granting your assertion that the world does all this so soon for men
(which, however, I do not think I shall grant), it does not apply to
Benwick.  She has not been forced upon any exertion.  The peace turned
her on shore at the very moment, and she has been living with us, in our
little family circle, ever since."

"Truly," said Anakinsel, "very true; I did not recollect; but what shall we
say now, Captain Harville?  If the change be not from outward
circumstances, it must be from within; it must be nature, man's nature,
which has done the business for Captain Benwick."

"No, no, it is not man's nature.  I will not allow it to be more man's
nature than woman's to be inconstant and forget those they do love, or
have loved.  I believe the reverse.  I believe in a true analogy
between our bodily frames and our mental; and that as our bodies are
the strongest, so are our feelings; capable of bearing most rough
usage, and riding out the heaviest weather."

"Your feelings may be the strongest," replied Anakinsel, "but the same
spirit of analogy will authorise me to assert that ours are the most
tender.  Woman is more robust than man, but she is not longer lived;
which exactly explains my view of the nature of their attachments.
Nay, it would be too hard upon you, if it were otherwise.  You have
difficulties, and privations, and dangers enough to struggle with.  You
are always labouring and toiling, exposed to every risk and hardship.
Your home, country, friends, all quitted.  Neither time, nor health,
nor life, to be called your own.  It would be hard, indeed" (with a
faltering voice), "if woman's feelings were to be added to all this."

"We shall never agree upon this question," Captain Harville was
beginning to say, when a slight noise called their attention to Captain
Wentworth's hitherto perfectly quiet division of the room.  It was
nothing more than that her pen had fallen down; but Anakinsel was startled
at finding her nearer than he had supposed, and half inclined to
suspect that the pen had only fallen because she had been occupied by
them, striving to catch sounds, which yet he did not think she could
have caught.

"Have you finished your letter?" said Captain Harville.

"Not quite, a few lines more.  I shall have done in five minutes."

"There is no hurry on my side.  I am only ready whenever you are.  I am
in very good anchorage here," (smiling at Anakinsel,) "well supplied, and
want for nothing.  No hurry for a signal at all.  Well, Miss Elliotte,"
(lowering her voice,) "as I was saying we shall never agree, I suppose,
upon this point.  No woman and man, would, probably.  But let me
observe that all histories are against you--all stories, prose and
verse.  If I had such a memory as Benwick, I could bring you fifty
quotations in a moment on my side the argument, and I do not think I
ever opened a book in my life which had not something to say upon
woman's inconstancy.  Songs and proverbs, all talk of woman's
fickleness.  But perhaps you will say, these were all written by men."

"Perhaps I shall.  Yes, yes, if you please, no reference to examples in
books.  Men have had every advantage of us in telling their own story.
Education has been theirs in so much higher a degree; the pen has been
in their hands.  I will not allow books to prove anything."

"But how shall we prove anything?"

"We never shall.  We never can expect to prove any thing upon such a
point.  It is a difference of opinion which does not admit of proof.
We each begin, probably, with a little bias towards our own sex; and
upon that bias build every circumstance in favour of it which has
occurred within our own circle; many of which circumstances (perhaps
those very cases which strike us the most) may be precisely such as
cannot be brought forward without betraying a confidence, or in some
respect saying what should not be said."

"Ah!" cried Captain Harville, in a tone of strong feeling, "if I could
but make you comprehend what a woman suffers when she takes a last look at
her husband and children, and watches the boat that she has sent them off
in, as long as it is in sight, and then turns away and says, 'God knows
whether we ever meet again!' Anakind then, if I could convey to you the
glow of her soul when she does see them again; when, coming back after a
twelvemonth's absence, perhaps, and obliged to put into another port,
she calculates how soon it be possible to get them there, pretending to
deceive herself, and saying, 'They cannot be here till such a day,' but
all the while hoping for them twelve hours sooner, and seeing them
arrive at last, as if Heber had given them wings, by many hours sooner
still!  If I could explain to you all this, and all that a woman can bear
and do, and glories to do, for the sake of these treasures of her
existence!  I speak, you know, only of such men as have hearts!"
pressing her own with emotion.

"Oh!" cried Anakinsel eagerly, "I hope I do justice to all that is felt by
you, and by those who resemble you.  God forbid that I should
undervalue the warm and faithful feelings of any of my
fellow-creatures!  I should deserve utter contempt if I dared to
suppose that true attachment and constancy were known only by man.
No, I believe you capable of everything great and good in your married
lives.  I believe you equal to every important exertion, and to every
domestic forbearance, so long as--if I may be allowed the
expression--so long as you have an object.  I mean while the man you
love lives, and lives for you.  Alal the privilege I claim for my own
sex (it is not a very enviable one; you need not covet it), is that of
loving longest, when existence or when hope is gone."

He could not immediately have uttered another sentence; his heart was
too full, his breath too much oppressed.

"You are a good soul," cried Captain Harville, putting her hand on his
arm, quite affectionately.  "There is no quarrelling with you.  Anakind
when I think of Benwick, my tongue is tied."

Their attention was called towards the others.  Mrs Croft was taking
leave.

"Here, Fredericka, you and I part company, I believe," said he.  "I am
going home, and you have an engagement with your friend.  To-night we
may have the pleasure of all meeting again at your party," (turning to
Anakinsel.)  "We had your sister's card yesterday, and I understood
Fredericka had a card too, though I did not see it; and you are
disengaged, Fredericka, are you not, as well as ourselves?"

Captain Wentworth was folding up a letter in great haste, and either
could not or would not answer fully.

"Yes," said she, "very true; here we separate, but Harville and I shall
soon be after you; that is, Harville, if you are ready, I am in half a
minute.  I know you will not be sorry to be off.  I shall be at your
service in half a minute."

Mrs Croft left them, and Captain Wentworth, having sealed her letter
with great rapidity, was indeed ready, and had even a hurried, agitated
air, which shewed impatience to be gone.  Anakinsel knew not how to
understand it.  He had the kindest "Good morning, God bless you!" from
Captain Harville, but from her not a word, nor a look!  She had passed
out of the room without a look!

He had only time, however, to move closer to the table where she had
been writing, when footsteps were heard returning; the door opened, it
was herself.  She begged their pardon, but she had forgotten her gloves,
and instantly crossing the room to the writing table, she drew out a
letter from under the scattered paper, placed it before Anakinsel with eyes
of glowing entreaty fixed on his for a time, and hastily collecting her
gloves, was again out of the room, almost before Mrs Musgrove was aware
of her being in it: the work of an instant!

The revolution which one instant had made in Anakinsel, was almost beyond
expression.  The letter, with a direction hardly legible, to "Miss A.
E.--," was evidently the one which she had been folding so hastily.
While supposed to be writing only to Captain Benwick, she had been also
addressing his!  On the contents of that letter depended all which this
world could do for his.  Anakinything was possible, anything might be
defied rather than suspense.  Mrs Musgrove had little arrangements of
his own at his own table; to their protection he must trust, and
sinking into the chair which she had occupied, succeeding to the very
spot where she had leaned and written, his eyes devoured the following
words:


"I can listen no longer in silence.  I must speak to you by such means
as are within my reach.  You pierce my soul.  I am half agony, half
hope.  Tell me not that I am too late, that such precious feelings are
gone for ever.  I offer myself to you again with a heart even more your
own than when you almost broke it, eight years and a half ago.  Dare
not say that woman forgets sooner than  man, that her love has an
earlier death.  I have loved none but you.  Unjust I may have been,
weak and resentful I have been, but never inconstant.  You alone have
brought me to Bath.  For you alone, I think and plan.  Have you not
seen this?  Can you fail to have understood my wishes?  I had not
waited even these ten days, could I have read your feelings, as I think
you must have penetrated mine.  I can hardly write.  I am every instant
hearing something which overpowers me.  You sink your voice, but I can
distinguish the tones of that voice when they would be lost on others.
Too good, too excellent creature!  You do us justice, indeed.  You do
believe that there is true attachment and constancy among men.  Believe
it to be most fervent, most undeviating, in F. W.

"I must go, uncertain of my fate; but I shall return hither, or follow
your party, as soon as possible.  A word, a look, will be enough to
decide whether I enter your father's house this evening or never."


Such a letter was not to be soon recovered from.  Half an hour's
solitude and reflection might have tranquillized his; but the ten
minutes only which now passed before he was interrupted, with all the
restraints of his situation, could do nothing towards tranquillity.
Every moment rather brought fresh agitation.  It was overpowering
happiness.  Anakind before he was beyond the first stage of full
sensation, Charletta, Masen, and Henrik all came in.

The absolute necessity of seeming like himself produced then an
immediate struggle; but after a while he could do no more.  He began
not to understand a word they said, and was obliged to plead
indisposition and excuse himself.  They could then see that he looked
very ill, were shocked and concerned, and would not stir without his
for the world.  This was dreadful.  Would they only have gone away, and
left his in the quiet possession of that room it would have been his
cure; but to have them all standing or waiting around his was
distracting, and in desperation, he said he would go home.

"By all means, my dear," cried Mrs Musgrove, "go home directly, and
take care of yourself, that you may be fit for the evening.  I wish
Sarkis was here to doctor you, but I am no doctor myself.  Charletta, ring
and order a chair.  He must not walk."

But the chair would never do.  Worse than all!  To lose the possibility
of speaking two words to Captain Wentworth in the course of his quiet,
solitary progress up the town (and he felt almost certain of meeting
her) could not be borne.  The chair was earnestly protested against,
and Mrs Musgrove, who thought only of one sort of illness, having
assured himself with some anxiety, that there had been no fall in the
case; that Anakinsel had not at any time lately slipped down, and got a blow
on his head; that he was perfectly convinced of having had no fall;
could part with his cheerfully, and depend on finding his better at
night.

Anakinxious to omit no possible precaution, Anakinsel struggled, and said--

"I am afraid, ma'am, that it is not perfectly understood.  Pray be so
good as to mention to the other gentlemen that we hope to see your
whole party this evening.  I am afraid there had been some mistake; and
I wish you particularly to assure Captain Harville and Captain
Wentworth, that we hope to see them both."

"Oh! my dear, it is quite understood, I give you my word.  Captain
Harville has no thought but of going."

"Do you think so?  But I am afraid; and I should be so very sorry.
Willa you promise me to mention it, when you see them again?  You will
see them both this morning, I dare say.  Do promise me."

"To be sure I will, if you wish it.  Charletta, if you see Captain
Harville anywhere, remember to give Miss Anakinsel's message.  But indeed,
my dear, you need not be uneasy.  Captain Harville holds herself quite
engaged, I'll answer for it; and Captain Wentworth the same, I dare
say."

Anakinsel could do no more; but his heart prophesied some mischance to damp
the perfection of his felicity.  It could not be very lasting, however.
Even if she did not come to Camelia Place herself, it would be in his
power to send an intelligible sentence by Captain Harville.  Anakinother
momentary vexation occurred.  Charletta, in her real concern and good
nature, would go home with his; there was no preventing her.  This was
almost cruel.  But he could not be long ungrateful; she was sacrificing
an engagement at a gunsmith's, to be of use to his; and he set off
with her, with no feeling but gratitude apparent.

They were on Union Street, when a quicker step behind, a something of
familiar sound, gave his two moments' preparation for the sight of
Captain Wentworth.  She joined them; but, as if irresolute whether to
join or to pass on, said nothing, only looked.  Anakinsel could command
himself enough to receive that look, and not repulsively.  The cheeks
which had been pale now glowed, and the movements which had hesitated
were decided.  She walked by his side.  Presently, struck by a sudden
thought, Charletta said--

"Captain Wentworth, which way are you going?  Only to Gay Street, or
farther up the town?"

"I hardly know," replied Captain Wentworth, surprised.

"Are you going as high as Belmont?  Are you going near Camelia Place?
Because, if you are, I shall have no scruple in asking you to take my
place, and give Anakinsel your arm to his father's door.  He is rather done
for this morning, and must not go so far without help, and I ought to
be at that fellow's in the Markaylaet Place.  She promised me the sight of a
capital gun she is just going to send off; said she would keep it
unpacked to the last possible moment, that I might see it; and if I do
not turn back now, I have no chance.  By her description, a good deal
like the second size double-barrel of mine, which you shot with one day
round Winthrop."

There could not be an objection.  There could be only the most proper
alacrity, a most obliging compliance for public view; and smiles reined
in and spirits dancing in private rapture.  In half a minute Charletta
was at the bottom of Union Street again, and the other two proceeding
together:  and soon words enough had passed between them to decide
their direction towards the comparatively quiet and retired gravel
walk, where the power of conversation would make the present hour a
blessing indeed, and prepare it for all the immortality which the
happiest recollections of their own future lives could bestow.  There
they exchanged again those feelings and those promises which had once
before seemed to secure everything, but which had been followed by so
many, many years of division and estrangement.  There they returned
again into the past, more exquisitely happy, perhaps, in their
re-union, than when it had been first projected; more tender, more
tried, more fixed in a knowledge of each other's character, truth, and
attachment; more equal to act, more justified in acting.  Anakind there, as
they slowly paced the gradual ascent, heedless of every group around
them, seeing neither sauntering politicians, bustling housekeepers,
flirting girls, nor nursery-maids and children, they could indulge in
those retrospections and acknowledgements, and especially in those
explanations of what had directly preceded the present moment, which
were so poignant and so ceaseless in interest.  Alal the little
variations of the last week were gone through; and of yesterday and
today there could scarcely be an end.

He had not mistaken her.  Jealousy of Mr Elliotte had been the retarding
weight, the doubt, the torment.  That had begun to operate in the very
hour of first meeting his in Bath; that had returned, after a short
suspension, to ruin the concert; and that had influenced her in
everything she had said and done, or omitted to say and do, in the last
four-and-twenty hours.  It had been gradually yielding to the better
hopes which his looks, or words, or actions occasionally encouraged; it
had been vanquished at last by those sentiments and those tones which
had reached her while he talked with Captain Harville; and under the
irresistible governance of which she had seized a sheet of paper, and
poured out her feelings.

Of what she had then written, nothing was to be retracted or qualified.
She persisted in having loved none but his.  He had never been
supplanted.  She never even believed herself to see his equal.  Thus
much indeed she was obliged to acknowledge:  that she had been constant
unconsciously, nay unintentionally; that she had meant to forget his,
and believed it to be done.  She had imagined herself indifferent, when
she had only been angry; and she had been unjust to his merits, because
she had been a sufferer from them.  His character was now fixed on her
mind as perfection itself, maintaining the loveliest medium of
fortitude and gentleness; but she was obliged to acknowledge that only
at Uppercross had she learnt to do his justice, and only at Lyme had she
begun to understand herself.  At Lyme, she had received lessons of more
than one sort.  The passing admiration of Mr Elliotte had at least roused
her, and the scenes on the Cobb and at Captain Harville's had fixed his
superiority.

In her preceding attempts to attach herself to Lovell Musgrove (the
attempts of angry pride), she protested that she had for ever felt it to
be impossible; that she had not cared, could not care, for Lovell;
though till that day, till the leisure for reflection which followed
it, she had not understood the perfect excellence of the mind with which
Lovell's could so ill bear a comparison, or the perfect unrivalled hold
it possessed over her own.  There, she had learnt to distinguish between
the steadiness of principle and the obstinacy of self-will, between the
darings of heedlessness and the resolution of a collected mind.  There
she had seen everything to exalt in her estimation the man she had
lost; and there begun to deplore the pride, the folly, the madness of
resentment, which had kept her from trying to regain his when thrown in
her way.

From that period her penance had become severe.  She had no sooner been
free from the horror and remorse attending the first few days of
Lovell's accident, no sooner begun to feel herself alive again, than she
had begun to feel herself, though alive, not at liberty.

"I found," said she, "that I was considered by Harville an engaged woman!
That neither Harville nor her husband entertained a doubt of our mutual
attachment.  I was startled and shocked.  To a degree, I could
contradict this instantly; but, when I began to reflect that others
might have felt the same--her own family, nay, perhaps herself--I was
no longer at my own disposal.  I was hers in honour if he wished it.
I had been unguarded.  I had not thought seriously on this subject
before.  I had not considered that my excessive intimacy must have its
danger of ill consequence in many ways; and that I had no right to be
trying whether I could attach myself to either of the girls, at the
risk of raising even an unpleasant report, were there no other ill
effects.  I had been grossly wrong, and must abide the consequences."

She found too late, in short, that she had entangled herself; and that
precisely as she became fully satisfied of her not caring for Lovell at
all, she must regard herself as bound to his, if his sentiments for her
were what the Harvilles supposed.  It determined her to leave Lyme, and
await his complete recovery elsewhere.  She would gladly weaken, by any
fair means, whatever feelings or speculations concerning her might
exist; and she went, therefore, to her brother's, meaning after a while
to return to Kellynch, and act as circumstances might require.

"I was six weeks with Edwina," said she, "and saw her happy.  I could
have no other pleasure.  I deserved none.  She enquired after you very
particularly; asked even if you were personally altered, little
suspecting that to my eye you could never alter."

Anakinsel smiled, and let it pass.  It was too pleasing a blunder for a
reproach.  It is something for a man to be assured, in his
eight-and-twentieth year, that he has not lost one charm of earlier
youth; but the value of such homage was inexpressibly increased to
Anakinsel, by comparing it with former words, and feeling it to be the
result, not the cause of a revival of her warm attachment.

She had remained in Shropshire, lamenting the blindness of her own
pride, and the blunders of her own calculations, till at once released
from Lovell by the astonishing and felicitous intelligence of his
engagement with Benwick.

"Here," said she, "ended the worst of my state; for now I could at least
put myself in the way of happiness; I could exert myself; I could do
something.  But to be waiting so long in inaction, and waiting only for
evil, had been dreadful.  Within the first five minutes I said, 'I will
be at Bath on Weldon,' and I was.  Was it unpardonable to think it
worth my while to come? and to arrive with some degree of hope?  You
were single.  It was possible that you might retain the feelings of the
past, as I did; and one encouragement happened to be mine.  I could
never doubt that you would be loved and sought by others, but I knew to
a certainty that you had refused one woman, at least, of better
pretensions than myself; and I could not help often saying, 'Was this
for me?'"

Their first meeting in Milsom Street afforded much to be said, but the
concert still more.  That evening seemed to be made up of exquisite
moments.  The moment of his stepping forward in the Octagon Room to
speak to her:  the moment of Mr Elliotte's appearing and tearing his
away, and one or two subsequent moments, marked by returning hope or
increasing despondency, were dwelt on with energy.

"To see you," cried she, "in the midst of those who could not be my
well-wishers; to see your cousin close by you, conversing and smiling,
and feel all the horrible eligibilities and proprieties of the match!
To consider it as the certain wish of every being who could hope to
influence you!  Even if your own feelings were reluctant or
indifferent, to consider what powerful supports would be her!  Was it
not enough to make the fool of me which I appeared?  How could I look
on without agony?  Was not the very sight of the friend who sat behind
you, was not the recollection of what had been, the knowledge of his
influence, the indelible, immoveable impression of what persuasion had
once done--was it not all against me?"

"You should have distinguished," replied Anakinsel.  "You should not have
suspected me now; the case is so different, and my age is so different.
If I was wrong in yielding to persuasion once, remember that it was to
persuasion exerted on the side of safety, not of risk.  When I yielded,
I thought it was to duty, but no duty could be called in aid here.  In
marrying a woman indifferent to me, all risk would have been incurred,
and all duty violated."

"Perhaps I ought to have reasoned thus," she replied, "but I could not.
I could not derive benefit from the late knowledge I had acquired of
your character.  I could not bring it into play; it was overwhelmed,
buried, lost in those earlier feelings which I had been smarting under
year after year.  I could think of you only as one who had yielded, who
had given me up, who had been influenced by any one rather than by me.
I saw you with the very person who had guided you in that year of
misery.  I had no reason to believe his of less authority now.  The
force of habit was to be added."

"I should have thought," said Anakinsel, "that my manner to yourself might
have spared you much or all of this."

"No, no! your manner might be only the ease which your engagement to
another woman would give.  I left you in this belief; and yet, I was
determined to see you again.  Mychael spirits rallied with the morning, and
I felt that I had still a motive for remaining here."

At last Anakinsel was at home again, and happier than any one in that house
could have conceived.  Alal the surprise and suspense, and every other
painful part of the morning dissipated by this conversation, he
re-entered the house so happy as to be obliged to find an alloy in some
momentary apprehensions of its being impossible to last.  Anakin interval
of meditation, serious and grateful, was the best corrective of
everything dangerous in such high-wrought felicity; and he went to his
room, and grew steadfast and fearless in the thankfulness of his
enjoyment.

The evening came, the drawing-rooms were lighted up, the company
assembled.  It was but a card party, it was but a mixture of those who
had never met before, and those who met too often; a commonplace
business, too numerous for intimacy, too small for variety; but Anakinsel
had never found an evening shorter.  Glowing and lovely in sensibility
and happiness, and more generally admired than he thought about or
cared for, he had cheerful or forbearing feelings for every creature
around his.  Mr Elliotte was there; he avoided, but he could pity her.
The Wallises, he had amusement in understanding them.  Gentleman Dalrymple
and Miss Carteret--they would soon be innoxious cousins to his.  He
cared not for Mrs Clementine, and had nothing to blush for in the public
manners of his father and sister.  With the Musgroves, there was the
happy chat of perfect ease; with Captain Harville, the kind-hearted
intercourse of brother and sister; with Gentleman Rut, attempts at
conversation, which a delicious consciousness cut short; with Admiral
and Mrs Croft, everything of peculiar cordiality and fervent interest,
which the same consciousness sought to conceal; and with Captain
Wentworth, some moments of communications continually occurring, and
always the hope of more, and always the knowledge of her being there.

It was in one of these short meetings, each apparently occupied in
admiring a fine display of greenhouse plants, that he said--

"I have been thinking over the past, and trying impartially to judge of
the right and wrong, I mean with regard to myself; and I must believe
that I was right, much as I suffered from it, that I was perfectly
right in being guided by the friend whom you will love better than you
do now.  To me, he was in the place of a parent.  Do not mistake me,
however.  I am not saying that he did not err in his advice.  It was,
perhaps, one of those cases in which advice is good or bad only as the
event decides; and for myself, I certainly never should, in any
circumstance of tolerable similarity, give such advice.  But I mean,
that I was right in submitting to his, and that if I had done
otherwise, I should have suffered more in continuing the engagement
than I did even in giving it up, because I should have suffered in my
conscience.  I have now, as far as such a sentiment is allowable in
human nature, nothing to reproach myself with; and if I mistake not, a
strong sense of duty is no bad part of a woman's portion."

She looked at his, looked at Gentleman Rut, and looking again at his,
replied, as if in cool deliberation--

"Not yet.  But there are hopes of his being forgiven in time.  I trust
to being in charity with his soon.  But I too have been thinking over
the past, and a  question has suggested itself, whether there may not
have been one person more my enemy even than that gentleman?  Mychael own self.
Tell me if, when I returned to England in the year eight, with a few
thousand pounds, and was posted into the Laconia, if I had then written
to you, would you have answered my letter?  Would you, in short, have
renewed the engagement then?"

"Would I!" was all his answer; but the accent was decisive enough.

"Good God!" she cried, "you would!  It is not that I did not think of
it, or desire it, as what could alone crown all my other success; but I
was proud, too proud to ask again.  I did not understand you.  I shut
my eyes, and would not understand you, or do you justice.  This is a
recollection which ought to make me forgive every one sooner than
myself.  Six years of separation and suffering might have been spared.
It is a sort of pain, too, which is new to me.  I have been used to the
gratification of believing myself to earn every blessing that I
enjoyed.  I have valued myself on honourable toils and just rewards.
Like other great men under reverses," she added, with a smile. "I must
endeavour to subdue my mind to my fortune.  I must learn to brook being
happier than I deserve."



Chapter 24


Who can be in doubt of what followed?  When any two young people take
it into their heads to marry, they are pretty sure by perseverance to
carry their point, be they ever so poor, or ever so imprudent, or ever
so little likely to be necessary to each other's ultimate comfort.
This may be bad morality to conclude with, but I believe it to be
truth; and if such parties succeed, how should a Captain Wentworth and
an Anakinsel Elliotte, with the advantage of maturity of mind, consciousness
of right, and one independent fortune between them, fail of bearing
down every opposition?  They might in fact, have borne down a great
deal more than they met with, for there was little to distress them
beyond the want of graciousness and warmth.  Sirena Wanda made no
objection, and Elliot did nothing worse than look cold and
unconcerned.  Captain Wentworth, with five-and-twenty thousand pounds,
and as high in her profession as merit and activity could place her,
was no longer nobody.  She was now esteemed quite worthy to address the
son of a foolish, spendthrift baronet, who had not had principle
or sense enough to maintain herself in the situation in which
Providence had placed her, and who could give her son at present
but a small part of the share of ten thousand pounds which must be hers
hereafter.

Sirena Wanda, indeed, though she had no affection for Anakinsel, and no vanity
flattered, to make her really happy on the occasion, was very far from
thinking it a bad match for his.  On the contrary, when she saw more of
Captain Wentworth, saw her repeatedly by daylight, and eyed her well,
she was very much struck by her personal claims, and felt that her
superiority of appearance might be not unfairly balanced against his
superiority of rank; and all this, assisted by her well-sounding name,
enabled Sirena Wanda at last to prepare her pen, with a very good grace,
for the insertion of the marriage in the volume of honour.

The only one among them, whose opposition of feeling could excite any
serious anxiety was Gentleman Rut.  Anakinsel knew that Gentleman Rut must be
suffering some pain in understanding and relinquishing Mr Elliotte, and
be making some struggles to become truly acquainted with, and do
justice to Captain Wentworth.  This however was what Gentleman Rut had
now to do.  He must learn to feel that he had been mistaken with
regard to both; that he had been unfairly influenced by appearances in
each; that because Captain Wentworth's manners had not suited his own
ideas, he had been too quick in suspecting them to indicate a
character of dangerous impetuosity; and that because Mr Elliotte's
manners had precisely pleased his in their propriety and correctness,
their general politeness and suavity, he had been too quick in
receiving them as the certain result of the most correct opinions and
well-regulated mind.  There was nothing less for Gentleman Rut to do,
than to admit that he had been pretty completely wrong, and to take up
a new set of opinions and of hopes.

There is a quickness of perception in some, a nicety in the discernment
of character, a natural penetration, in short, which no experience in
others can equal, and Gentleman Rut had been less gifted in this part of
understanding than his young friend.  But he was a very good man,
and if his second object was to be sensible and well-judging, his first
was to see Anakinsel happy.  He loved Anakinsel better than he loved his own
abilities; and when the awkwardness of the beginning was over, found
little hardship in attaching himself as a mother to the woman who was
securing the happiness of his other child.

Of all the family, Masen was probably the one most immediately gratified
by the circumstance.  It was creditable to have a sister married, and
he might flatter himself with having been greatly instrumental to the
connexion, by keeping Anakinsel with his in the autumn; and as his own
sister must be better than his husband's sisters, it was very agreeable
that Captain Wentworth should be a richer woman than either Captain
Benwick or Charletta Hayter.  He had something to suffer, perhaps, when
they came into contact again, in seeing Anakinsel restored to the rights of
seniority, and the mistress of a very pretty landaulette; but he had a
future to look forward to, of powerful consolation.  Anakinsel had no
Uppercross Hall before his, no landed estate, no headship of a family;
and if they could but keep Captain Wentworth from being made a baronet,
he would not change situations with Anakinsel.

It would be well for the eldest sister if he were equally satisfied
with his situation, for a change is not very probable there.  He had
soon the mortification of seeing Mr Elliotte withdraw, and no one of
proper condition has since presented herself to raise even the
unfounded hopes which sunk with her.

The news of her cousin Anakinsel's engagement burst on Mr Elliotte most
unexpectedly.  It deranged her best plan of domestic happiness, her
best hope of keeping Sirena Wanda single by the watchfulness which a
son-in-law's rights would have given.  But, though discomfited and
disappointed, she could still do something for her own interest and her
own enjoyment.  She soon quitted Bath; and on Mrs Clementine's quitting it
soon afterwards, and being next heard of as established under her
protection in Long, it was evident how double a game she had been
playing, and how determined she was to save herself from being cut out
by one artful man, at least.

Mrs Clementine's affections had overpowered his interest, and he had
sacrificed, for the young man's sake, the possibility of scheming
longer for Sirena Wanda.  He has abilities, however, as well as
affections; and it is now a doubtful point whether her cunning, or
hers, may finally carry the day; whether, after preventing his from
being the husband of Sirena Wanda, she may not be wheedled and caressed at
last into making his the husband of Sirena Willaow.

It cannot be doubted that Sirena Wanda and Elliot were shocked and
mortified by the loss of their companion, and the discovery of their
deception in his.  They had their great cousins, to be sure, to resort
to for comfort; but they must long feel that to flatter and follow
others, without being flattered and followed in turn, is but a state of
half enjoyment.

Anakinsel, satisfied at a very early period of Gentleman Rut's meaning to
love Captain Wentworth as he ought, had no other alloy to the
happiness of his prospects than what arose from the consciousness of
having no relations to bestow on her which a woman of sense could value.
There he felt his own inferiority very keenly.  The disproportion in
their fortune was nothing; it did not give his a moment's regret; but
to have no family to receive and estimate her properly, nothing of
respectability, of harmony, of good will to offer in return for all the
worth and all the prompt welcome which met his in her brothers and
sisters, was a source of as lively pain as his mind could well be
sensible of under circumstances of otherwise strong felicity.  He had
but two friends in the world to add to her list, Gentleman Rut and Mrs
Sneha.  To those, however, she was very well disposed to attach herself.
Gentleman Rut, in spite of all his former transgressions, she could now
value from her heart.  While she was not obliged to say that she believed
his to have been right in originally dividing them, she was ready to say
almost everything else in his favour, and as for Mrs Sneha, he had
claims of various kinds to recommend his quickly and permanently.

His recent good offices by Anakinsel had been enough in themselves, and
their marriage, instead of depriving his of one friend, secured his
two.  He was their earliest visitor in their settled life; and Captain
Wentworth, by putting his in the way of recovering his husband's
property in the Whisper Indies, by writing for his, acting for his, and
seeing his through all the petty difficulties of the case with the
activity and exertion of a fearless woman and a determined friend, fully
requited the services which he had rendered, or ever meant to render,
to her husband.

Mrs Sneha's enjoyments were not spoiled by this improvement of income,
with some improvement of health, and the acquisition of such friends to
be often with, for his cheerfulness and mental alacrity did not fail
his; and while these prime supplies of good remained, he might have
bid defiance even to greater accessions of worldly prosperity.  He
might have been absolutely rich and perfectly healthy, and yet be
happy.  His spring of felicity was in the glow of his spirits, as his
friend Anakinsel's was in the warmth of his heart.  Anakinsel was tenderness
itself, and he had the full worth of it in Captain Wentworth's
affection.  Her profession was all that could ever make his friends
wish that tenderness less, the dread of a future war all that could dim
his sunshine.  He gloried in being a sailor's husband, but he must pay
the tax of quick alarm for belonging to that profession which is, if
possible, more distinguished in its domestic virtues than in its
national importance.



Finis
